                        Technical Terms
       Arminianism is a branch of conditional security which teaches that
God contingently predetermines that those who persevere will be chosen
(elected) for heaven.* Human perseverance is the condition for divine
election. The free will of the believer is emphasized. In Arminianism,
reaching heaven is conditioned on the believer’s perseverance. (Arminians
may be defined broadly at the popular level as those who believe that
regeneration actually might be lost.) The Arminian order of conditional
results is: perseverance, then predestination, then heaven. Arminianism is
summarized by the acronym PEARS:
• Prevenient Grace—means God’s preparatory grace by which He takes
    the prerequisite step of providing mankind with the pragmatic ability to
    search for Him and find Him in saving faith.
• Election on Condition—means that God conditionally has elected all
    mankind to salvation. However, God has only predestined that those
    who of their own free will respond in faith to this offer of salvation and
    persevere in this faith will be saved. Predestination is thus conditioned
    on persevering faith.
• Atonement for All—means that the atonement is available sufficiently
    for all mankind, and its efficaciousness is limited only by their response.
• Resistible Grace—means that God’s prevenient grace may be resisted
    so that it does not result in salvation.
• Salvation through Perseverance—means that God’s grace is not only
    resistible; it is also reversible. Believers may fall irremediably from the
    state of saving grace if they fail to persevere. Those who persevere are
    elected to salvation from eternal damnation as a result of persevering.
    Believers must persevere in order to be saved, in the end. This doctrine
    also is popularly known as NOSAS, which stands for not once saved

*
  Technically, some Arminians affirm the unconditional corporate election of
those in Christ but make perseverance in faith the condition for remaining in
Christ. Those particular individuals who persevere in faith are conditionally
predestined for heaven. Hence, this approach simultaneously affirms conditional
particular predestination. Other Arminians affirm the election of individual
believers. Being elected is conditioned on being a believer. This latter Arminian
approach is less convoluted. Either way, the bottom line remains the same: In
Arminian theology, only those who persevere are elected for heaven.
Consequently, Arminians may be described as teaching conditional election (in
regard to the individual).
Technical Terms                                                         Page ix
    always saved. This form of NOSAS affirms that one actually may lose
    one’s regeneration and cease to be saved if one does not persevere.

       Bema is a transliteration of the Greek word used in 2Cor 5:10 to refer
to the Judgment Seat of Christ and will be used repeatedly in the present
book as an abbreviated reference to the Judgment Seat of Christ. Christians
are judged at this judgment seat after the rapture. Unbelievers are judged
1,000 years later at the Great White Throne Judgment—the thronos of
Rev 20:11.

       Calminianism = Calvinism + Arminianism. The term Calminianism
was popularized by Norman Geisler in his endeavor to adopt a mediating
position between Calvinism (TULIP)* and Arminianism (PEARS).† His
Calminian approach basically takes the point of agreement between the two
systems regarding the soteriological necessity of perseverance and replaces
the Arminian S of PEARS with a synthesis of the Calvinistic P from
TULIP.‡ The end result is an abbreviated PS Calminian acronym.1
       Regardless of the preferred full acronym for Calminianism, final
salvation from hell comes through perseverance in all forms of
Calminianism (as well as in Calvinism and Arminianism). By definition,
then, Calminianism is a mediation between Calvinism and Arminianism
that, like its two predecessors, makes perseverance necessary for
salvation from eternal damnation. Since the present book does not make
perseverance necessary for salvation from eternal damnation, it should be
categorized separately from Calminianism. Contrary to Calminian claims
otherwise, in making perseverance descriptively necessary, Calminianism
also necessarily makes perseverance prescriptively necessary: both
exegetically and logically. The present book rejects any and all Calminian
attempts at harmonization in favor of a Marrowistic mediation which teaches
that perseverance is necessary for rewards in heaven rather than for entrance
into heaven itself.

      Calvinism is a branch of conditional security which teaches that God
sovereignly determines (i.e., elects) who will persevere and thus will make it
to heaven. Divine election is the basis for human perseverance. The
sovereignty of God is emphasized. The elect persevere because they are
unconditionally predetermined by God to persevere so that they might reach

*
  See Calvinism, ix.
†
  See Arminianism, viii.
‡
  In S those who persevere are predestined, while in P those who are predestined
persevere. Geisler attempts to reject any logical order between perseverance and
predestination. Other Calminians do not necessarily reject a logical order.
Page x                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
heaven. Calvinists sometimes like to present themselves as advocates of
unconditional security, but Calvinism actually is a failed attempt to enjoin
unconditional security with conditional security. The Calvinist might assert
that the believer is unconditionally assured of reaching heaven, but what a
Calvinist actually means with such an articulation is that the believer is
unconditionally assured of persevering, thereby reaching heaven. In
Calvinism, reaching heaven is conditioned on the believer’s perseverance.
The Calvinistic order of conditions and result is: election, then perseverance,
then heaven. Calvinism is summarized by the acronym TULIP:
• Total Depravity—means total inability. Man is totally unable to believe
     the gospel unless God acts upon him with irresistible grace in
     regeneration. Regeneration, at least in its incipient form, must precede
     belief. God regenerates man so that man may believe in accordance with
     his new nature.
• Unconditional Election—means that God unconditionally elects who
     will believe and be saved. Man is not elected because he believes.
     Rather, he believes because he is elected. God predetermines who will
     believe and be saved. It was never God’s intention to save the nonelect.
     Election is selection. God pre-selects who will believe and causes them
     to believe. No one else genuinely can believe the gospel. No one else
     will persevere in saving faith. No one else can be saved.
• Limited Atonement—means that the benefits of the atonement are
     extended only to the elect. In summary, Christ died only for the elect,
     not for the nonelect. God limited the available benefits of Christ’s death
     to the elect. God never had any true intention of making salvation
     possible for everyone.
• Irresistible Grace—means that God irresistibly causes the elect to
     become regenerate so that the elect will believe. Regeneration,
     therefore, precedes faith. Faith is a gift from God that the elect do not
     have the power to refuse.
• Perseverance of the Saints—means that saints who persevere are
     preserved and thus saved, in the end, as a result of their perseverance.
     Only those who persevere are preserved. Preservation does not apply to
     those who do not persevere. Thus, perseverance of the saints is not to be
     confused with eternal security of the saints. Only the elect persevere,
     and they persevere because they are predestined to persevere. Yet they
     must persevere in order to reach heaven. From the perspective of
     unconditional election, this model teaches OSAS (once saved always
     saved). However, from the perspective of conditionality, this model
     teaches NOSAS (not once saved always saved). This form of NOSAS
     affirms that one hypothetically may lose one’s regeneration and cease
     to be saved if one does not persevere.
Technical Terms                                                          Page xi
       Conditional security is the conviction that once a person believes in
Christ for eternal life, that person is conditionally secure in Christ and
conditionally assured of reaching heaven. According to conditional
securitists, a believer must persevere in order to reach heaven. (Both
proveitism and forfeitism advocate conditional security.)

       Conditionalist refers to an advocate of conditional security—the
conviction that once a person believes in Christ for eternal life, that person is
conditionally secure in Christ and must persevere in order to reach heaven.

       Forfeitist refers to an advocate of conditional security who believes
that those who do not persevere forfeit (i.e., lose) the gift of eternal life.

       Free Grace Salvationists are those who insist that the biblical
requirement for salvation from eternal damnation is that one simply believes
in the Lord Jesus for eternal life. This understanding is exemplified by the
Grace Evangelical Society (GES) motto: Faith Alone in Christ Alone. From
their perspective, unconditional security is guaranteed upon the recognition
that the free gift of eternal life is offered freely to one who simply believes
in Jesus Christ for eternal life.

       Insecuritist refers to an advocate of conditional security.
Perseverance (in faith and/or faithfulness) is the postconversional condition
for reaching heaven according to insecuritists.

       Linear faith (£ ) is ongoing faith that takes place over a period of
time. Although linear aspect is represented frequently by a line (—) in Greek
grammars, herein it is represented as an arrow so that a line can be used to
represent a state in contradistinction to an ongoing action.

       Marrowism is the present author’s coined term for describing his
mediating non-Calvinistic Free Grace theology, which is positioned between
Arminianism (PEARS) and Calvinism (TULIP) and summarized herein by
the author’s acronym GRAPE. Historically, this movement was expressed in
seminal form by the Marrow Controversy. In a number of key areas, the
present author may be considered a modern-day Marrowist. The present
book is an introduction to Marrowism as the present author has come to
refine and define it. The author’s fuller explanation will be detailed in his
book, The Outer Darkness. As used by the author, Marrowism refers to a
defense of unconditional security consisting of soteriological and
misthological consistency. Marrowism is summarized by the acronym
GRAPE:
• Gift is Free—means that the gift of eternal life is offered as a free gift
     and must be passively received as such.
Page xii                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

•   Rewards are Earned—means that the distinction between unmerited
    gifts and merited rewards must not be blurred.
•   Absolute Assurance—means that personal assurance of one’s salvation
    from eternal damnation is the essence of saving faith.
•   Passive Persuasion—means that faith is a passive persuasion, not an
    active commitment.
•   Eternal Security—means that the person who trusts in Christ alone for
    eternal life immediately and eternally thereafter has eternal life and
    cannot experience eternal damnation. This doctrine of eternal security is
    known popularly as OSAS (once saved always saved) and is
    consistently and emphatically affirmed by GRAPE, in contrast to the
    conditional denunciation this doctrine receives in TULIP and PEARS.
    The affirmation of eternal security (E) is placed strategically at the end
    of the GRAPE acronym in order to contrast the most distinctive aspect
    of Marrowistic theology from the other two acronyms. Unlike
    Arminianism and Calvinism, Marrowism emphatically does not
    condition salvation from hell on the believer’s perseverance. GRAPE
    affirms true eternal security, not some Arminian or Calvinistic imitation
    of security. Marrowistic eternal security is unconditional security.

       Mistholic is an abbreviation for misthological (that has been
shortened syllabically to match its counterpart soteric, which, in turn, is the
abbreviation for soteriological).

       Misthologist: refers to an advocate of unconditional soteriological
security who has a sufficient degree of misthological competency so as to
recognize that the biblical teaching regarding the outer darkness pertains to
misthological themes so that salvation from the outer darkness depicts
conditional misthological security.

       Misthology is a coined term derived from the Greek word misthos
(reward) and logos (teaching) and refers to the doctrine of temporal and
eternal rewards but is used primarily in reference to eternal rewards.

        NOSAS is the popular acronym for Not Once Saved Always Saved
(i.e., conditional security). Popularly, OSAS is not the same thing as
unconditional security in that many proveitists believe in OSAS yet insist
that one must persevere in order to reach heaven. This hybrid by the
proveitist camp is not considered a valid form of OSAS in the present book.

      OSAS is the popular acronym for Once Saved Always Saved (i.e.,
unconditional security). Technically, OSAS is not the same thing as
unconditional security in that many proveitists believe in OSAS and yet
Technical Terms                                                          Page xiii
insist that one must persevere in order to reach heaven. Thus, reaching
heaven is conditioned by the proveitist camp on perseverance. This
proveitist hybrid is not a valid form of OSAS. The only form of OSAS
considered legitimate herein is the unconditional affirmation of OSAS even
if one does not persevere.

        Outer Darkness is a picturesque term used three times in the Bible
(Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30). The author’s book The Outer Darkness defends the
thesis that this term, when used in the Bible, refers to the loss of rewards in
heaven. For example, Charles Stanley’s popular book Eternal Security takes
much the same position. Exploring what such a loss would entail is beyond
the scope of the present work. Such matters have been explored in the
author’s major work on that subject. Suffice it to say here that since rewards
are eternal, loss of rewards is also eternal.

       Overcomer is a coined expression used to refer to those believers who
overcome. Although the word overcome is used as a statement in Scripture
to refer to the positional victory already achieved by all believers in the past,
it sometimes is used in Scripture as an exhortation, urging believers to
progress on to achieve experiential victory in the present. Finally, it is used
futuristically as a promise of rewards to those relatively few believers who
will be victorious in faithfulness until the end, thus urging experiential
victory in the present. Its chief usage herein is as an exhortation and promise
for believers to become what they are so that they may rule as such.

       Proveitists is a coined abbreviation for prove-it-tists: advocates of
prove-it theology. Many proveitists will object to being calling Calvinists.
Perhaps they are Calminians or closet Calvinists. In any event, the term
proveitists is used broadly to denote those who (like Calvinists) maintain
that the way a person lives proves whether that person is a genuine believer.
Proveitism asserts that fruit in the form of perseverance is necessary proof of
salvation: If you do not have this fruit, then you never were saved initially.
Perseverance is necessary to prove that you are saved and to enter heaven.
Calvinists, closet Calvinists, and Calminians thus would be included under
the proveitistic umbrella.

        Punctiliar faith (z) is a response of faith that takes place at a point in
time.

       Regeneration is the theological term used to describe the impartation
of eternal life as a free gift (to one’s spirit) which occurs when a person
believes in Christ for eternal life. Such a person is said to be born again (i.e.,
given spiritual life in the form of eternal life as a free gift).
Page xiv                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
       Saving faith is a theological term used to refer to the faith which
results in salvation from hell. For an unconditional securitist, such faith is
regarded as punctiliar (z). For the conditional securitist, such faith is
regarded as linear (£). In other words, securitists believe that initial faith is
sufficient for salvation from eternal damnation. Insecuritists, in contrast,
teach that initial faith is insufficient since it must be followed by
perseverance in faith in order to qualify for heaven.

      Securitist refers to an advocate of unconditional security—the
conviction that once a person believes in Christ for eternal life, that person is
unconditionally secure in Christ and is unconditionally assured of reaching
heaven unconditionally.

       Soteriology is based on two biblical Greek words, soteria (salvation)
and logos (teaching) and correspondingly refers to biblical teaching about
salvation. Although the biblical concept of salvation includes many
dimensions other than deliverance from hell, it is this aspect of salvation to
which writers refer frequently when they use the word soteriology. Thus,
salvation from hell (or more specifically, from eternal damnation in the Lake
of Fire) will be referred to as soteriological salvation or eternal salvation. A
soteriological interpretation of a passage thus would treat the passage as
dealing with eternal salvation from the Lake of Fire (Rev 20:14-15).

        Unconditional security is the conviction that once a person believes
in Christ for eternal life, that person is unconditionally secure in Christ and
unconditionally assured of reaching heaven unconditionally. Technically,
this redundant articulation is superior to saying simply that a believer is
unconditionally assured of reaching heaven. A Calvinist might affirm the
latter but could not affirm the former.

        Unconditional securitist is someone who believes that perseverance
is not necessary to enter heaven.
                         Chapter 1.
                     Fallen From Grace
      Introduction
       Defining terms is a critical aspect of any debate. Many years ago
when I was a teenager working in a steak house for my summer job and
trying to be a witness of the grace of God and its attendant security, one of
the waitresses asked me, “Do you believe that you can fall from grace?” I
responded by asking, “What do you mean by fall from grace?” She gave no
answer, so neither did I. This book will provide the answer.

                     Falling From Grace
      Misthological Grace
       The NT indicates that believers can fall from grace (Gal 5:4), fall
short of grace (Heb 12:15), or be severed from the grace in which they
presently stand (Rom 5:2; 11:20-22). The fact that believers can be such
dismal failures in regard to God’s grace implies that a dimension of God’s
grace is conditioned on the believer’s perseverance. Thus, the simplistic
definition of grace as unmerited favor (independent of the context) will not
withstand scrutiny. Grace is not always free. The thought in the above
passages is not that grace results in perseverance. Instead, perseverance
results in grace. Such grace is merited.
       That grace is a multidimensional word is normally granted.
Nevertheless, one such dimension that frequently is overlooked is
misthological grace—earned grace.* Sometimes grace is deserved. Paul
frequently uses the verb eucharisteo “to give thanks” to God. However, he
also uses the noun charis (grace) to ascribe “thanks” to God (Rom 6:17;
7:25; 1Cor 15:57; 2Cor 2:14; 8:16; 9:15). In literal terms, Paul is saying,
“Grace to God.”
       Obviously, Paul is giving grace/thanks to God. Does this mean that
God does not deserve the grace/thanks that Paul gives to God? Of course
not! Grace can be deserved. God absolutely deserves the grace that Paul
gives Him. Conversely, depending on the context, believers can deserve the


*
  Misthology is a coined term derived from the Greek words misthos
(reward) and logos (teaching) and refers to the doctrine of temporal and
eternal rewards but is used primarily in reference to eternal rewards.
Page 16                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
grace that God gives them to a relative degree, that is, relative to the
promises God has made to reward them on the basis of their works.
        Wilkin wisely defines grace as “the bestowal of favor upon a person.
In the case of eternal salvation God’s grace is unmerited favor.”2 Implicitly,
by restricting the unmerited nature of God’s grace to only certain cases
(specifically in this case to soteriological* contexts), Wilkin leaves the door
open to the concept of merited grace in non-soteriological passages. Hunt
likewise affirms that grace “is not a technical term for divine favor or
unmerited favor.”3 Implicitly, one could anticipate that in certain passages
grace could refer to merited favor. Indeed, Wilkin is explicit in GNTC that
this is the case:

             Grace means “favor”…In this case it is favor that is
      merited…The gift of eternal life is an extension of unmerited
      favor (Eph 2:8-9). The reward of ruling with Christ and
      sharing His glory is an extension of merited favor. (Emphasis
      his.)4

       Accordingly, Wilkin correctly regards co-ruling with Christ to be an
example of merited grace. The existence of merited grace should be readily
apparent to readers of the Greek NT. The Greek word for grace (charis)
does not automatically exclude merit. In several passages, grace is given in
response to works rather than in contrast to works and is thus a reward
(Lk 6:32-35; Jam 4:6; 1Pet 2:19-20; 5:5-6). One may consult a standard
Greek dictionary such as BDAG (2.b) for confirmation. This lexicon
acknowledges that in such cases grace is a metonymy for winning God’s
favor. BDAG even hesitantly admits, “In these passages the meaning comes
close to reward.” Another Greek lexicon, Thayer (TY), on the other hand,
shows no hesitancy in explicitly affirming that the use of grace in Lk 6:32 is
“equivalent to recompense, reward.” TY is correct, since in the Matthean
parallel the Lukan charis is explicitly called a reward (misthos, Mt 5:46).



*
  Soteriology is based on two biblical Greek words, soteria (salvation) and
logos (teaching), and correspondingly refers to biblical teaching about
salvation. Although the biblical concept of salvation includes many
dimensions other than deliverance from hell, it is this aspect of salvation to
which writers refer frequently when they use the word soteriology. Thus,
salvation from hell (or more specifically, from eternal damnation in the Lake
of Fire) will be referred to as soteriological salvation or eternal salvation. A
soteriological interpretation of a passage thus would treat the passage as
dealing with eternal salvation from the Lake of Fire (Rev 20:14-15).
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 17
•   “And if you love those who love you, what grace is that to you?”
    (Lk 6:32; TM)

•   “For if you love those who love you, what reward have you?” (Mt 5:46)

       In these parallel passages, Lukan grace is a Matthean reward. Of
course, one does not have to compare Luke with Matthew to see the Lukan
intent. Contextually, Luke makes it very clear that Jesus is talking about
believers earning grace as a reward:
       32
          And if you love those who love you, what grace (charis) is
       that to you? For even sinners love those who love them.
       33
          And if you do good to those who do good to you, what grace
       (charis) is that to you? For even sinners do the same. 34 And if
       you lend to those from whom you expect to receive, what
       grace (charis) is that to you? Even sinners lend to sinners, in
       order to receive back the same amount. 35 But love your
       enemies, and do good, and lend, expecting nothing in return;
       and your reward will be great. (Lk 6:32-35)

Understandably, NIDNTT also defines grace accordingly:

       In the few places where Luke introduces the concept of grace
       into Jesus’ words it means reward in the last days, payment
       for something taken as a matter of course (Lk. 6:32-34; cf.
       Matt. 5:46; Lk. 17:9), and means almost the opposite to its
       basic meaning.5

       L&S also similarly states that grace may mean: “To lay up a store of
gratitude with a person, i.e. earn his thanks” or “to please him, do a thing
to oblige him.” Indeed, finding grace in 1Pet 2:19-20 could almost be
considered an idiom for earning grace because of the multiple occasions this
expression is used in the OT with the connotations of merited or innate
value.6 Like the OT maidens who found grace, one suspects that Mary found
favor (i.e., grace) with God because of some quality about her (Lk 1:30). It
was not by chance that God chose a virgin rather than a prostitute for Jesus’
mother! Like His mother, Jesus grew in favor with God and man because of
His moral character (Prov 3:3-4; Lk 2:52). Grace may be a reward based on
performance and perseverance; such instances could be described accurately
as misthological grace.

       Finding Grace
        The popular definition of grace as unmerited favor fails to account for
texts in which grace is merited favor. Even at the popular level, one should
Page 18                                Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
be uneasy with the popular definition. Popular texts clash with the popular
definition of grace as unmerited favor: “But Noah found favor [i.e., grace] in
the eyes of the Lord. These are the records of the generations of Noah. Noah
was a righteous man, blameless in his time; Noah walked with God”
(Gen 6:8-9). The implication is that Noah found grace because he was
righteous. Likewise, Peter informs believers that they can find grace if they
live right: “But if when you do what is right and suffer for it you patiently
endure it, this finds favor [= grace] with God” (1Pet 2:20). The OT gives
similar pronouncement: “The Lord gives [didomi] grace and glory; no good
thing does He withhold from those who walk uprightly” (Ps 84:11).* Those
who walk uprightly are rewarded with grace and glory. They are given grace
as their payment:

          •   Grace is their reward.
          •   Grace is God’s favor.

       These two statements are not contradictory. Whether God’s favor is
earned or unearned is dependent on the context. The key is to note whether
grace is the result that is given or the basis for what is given. In the example
provided by Peter, God’s favor is the reward for morally upright behavior.
Peter informs us that believers can still find grace today, just as Noah did in
his day, by doing what is right, that is, as a result of practical righteousness.
Believers who are righteous in this manner find grace because they are
righteous in their behavior. The point in these texts is not that they were
made righteous by grace but rather they found grace by being righteous.
Practical righteousness rather than positional righteousness is in view.
       To be sure, Paul teaches a justification by grace (Rom 3:24; Tit 3:7).
He is the champion of imputed righteousness in the book of Romans.
Imputed righteousness is unmerited righteousness given to believers as a gift
(Rom 5:17). Grace and works are mutually exclusive in such texts.
Imputational righteousness refers to being declared legally righteous in
soteriological contexts rather than to being made practically righteous in
misthological contexts. Imputational righteousness is given to us by grace
and pertains to our position in Christ. Yet here in the cases of Noah and
Peter, we find the exact opposite: grace by righteousness rather than
righteousness by grace.

         Harmonization of Tension
      The tension between these two principles can be harmonized by
observing that when the transaction or status is based solely (i.e.,
monergistically) on grace, it is not based on synergistic performance. Those
*
    See “Didomi (give/reward)” in Soteric and Mistholic Faith.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 19
who seek soteriological righteousness by performance have fallen from
grace as the basis (Gal 5:4). Likewise, those who seek practical
righteousness by fleshly performance have fallen from grace as the means.
Yet when grace is the result, it is based on performance.
       In other words, when God wants to show someone His favor despite
his or her performance, God’s favor is not conditioned on that person’s
performance. In contrast, when God wants to show someone His favor
because of his or her performance, then His grace naturally is conditioned on
his or her performance. God’s grace then covers the basis, means, and the
result of our performance.* Saving grace (which is grace at the ground level)
and enabling grace (at the initial intermediate level) are unearned. Although
the basis and (initiatory) means of grace are unmerited, the final product or
result that we produce by grace requires our synergistic cooperation. This
synergetic result is produced by the energy supplied by God and is
meritorious in that it is rewardable.
       For example, if your employer gives you a job and provides you with
the means to do that job, these things are unearned. You cannot say that you
deserve the job or the tools with which to do the job. But if you do the job,
you can say that you have earned your paycheck.†

       Deductive Summarization
      To capture the gist of what Paul is saying concerning the relationship
between rewards and grace in such soteriological texts as Rom 4:4-5 and
11:6 versus misthological texts such as 1Cor 3:14 and Col 3:23-24, the
following deductive summarizations can be made:
       1. If something is by grace, then it is unmerited.
       2. If something is by works, then it is merited.
       3. Since rewards are based on works, rewards are merited.

       From these deductions, it follows that if grace is the result of works,
then grace is not a gift. When grace is the result of works, grace is a reward.
Such grace is merited. The misthological nature of such grace is apparent.
What about when we produce works by enabling grace? Are we to conclude
*
  Basis = grounds. Grace covers the full spectrum of GMR (grounds, means,
and result). G and M are unmerited; R is merited (when M ≠ R). If we respond
favorably to the initial phase of enabling grace, that initial phase of enabling
grace may become our means (M) for receiving more enabling grace as a
merited result (R). Thus, the initiatory means (IM) of enabling grace is
unmerited. But the ensuing enabling grace awarded us because of our obedience
is the resulting means (RM) to earn grace as the final result (R). Like a Tree
Planted by the Water has a complementary color model.
†
  As to GMR in this example, G = job; M = tools; R = paycheck.
Page 20                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
that such grace-enabled-works are a gift since they are produced by grace?
Or should the opposite affirmation be made? Should we assume that since
grace-enabled-works are works, they are not by grace?
        Obviously, neither option is valid for enabling grace. Even though
some try to turn the works performed by believers and resultant rewards
earned by believers into gifts, grace-enabled-works are not a gift even
though such works are produced through grace. The works are a (R) result
of God’s enabling grace; these works entail merit. Likewise, the rewards
earned as a (R) result of these works are merited.
        By the grace of God, people are able to believe and are able to work.
If it were not for the common grace of God, we could do nothing because
“in Him we live and move and exist” (Acts 17:28). If it were not for
prevenient grace, humanity would not be able to respond rightly to God’s
soteriological drawing and seek God and come to faith. If it were not for
enabling grace, the believer would not be able to please God misthologically
by faith and works. When Paul rejects the combination of grace and works,
it is in his discussion of saving grace—grace that results in soteriological
justification. No human works of any type can have any part in receiving the
unearned benefit freely offered via saving grace.

       Works of the Law
       For soteriological justification not to be merited, the human response
necessary to receive it has to be nonmeritorious. Human performance has to
be ruled out completely. The problem with works of the law is not merely
that such works are of the law but that they are works. Granted, works are
polymorphous in nature. Yet works universally should be defined as
meritorious.7 Considering the importance of understanding just what is being
rejected as being earnable by works, this topic may be addressed from
multiple vantage points.* For the present discussion, an overview of the
research into this area provided by Moo will suffice.
       The rejection of works of the law as a possible means of justification
in Rom 3:28 cannot be limited to this type of works per say because Paul
immediately goes on to use Abraham as an example of the point he is
making (Rom 4:2-5). Abraham, of course, predates the law. Similarly, when
Paul asserts in Rom 9:11 that God’s election was not because of Jacob’s
works, Paul could not be referring to works of the law since the law was not
yet given. Rather, Paul is alluding to anything Jacob did, whether good or
bad. Obviously, since the text is dealing with beneficial results, what Paul is
denying is that Jacob was elected on the basis of good works (bad works are

*
  For another vantage point, see “Two Types of Works” in my book, Salvation:
A Categorical Analysis. For another FG vantage point regarding Jacob’s
election, see “Election and Salvation” in that book.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                        Page 21
not in view). On this basis Moo defines works as “good actions which could
be regarded as meritorious.”8 The same definition is applicable to the
parallel usage of works of the law. Paul’s objection to works of the law is not
because they are of the law but because they are works. Moo then cites
Fuller in support of his assessment: “‘Works of the law’ are rejected by Paul
for the simple reason that they are by definition improper—involving the
attempt of man to gain favor with God by virtue of his own performance.”9
        Misthologists would demur slightly with this last statement. Seeking
to earn God’s favor is not necessarily wrong. Believers should seek to earn
God’s misthological favor misthologically. The problem is when one
misthologically seeks to earn God’s soteriological favor. In this case, one
would be trying to earn the unearnable, or merit the unmeritable, or deserve
the undeservable (to use coined words to describe this impossibility). God’s
soteriological approval requires absolute perfection. The relative perfection
provided by misthological pursuits is completely inadequate to meet this
standard. Nevertheless, God graciously has chosen misthologically to accept
our misthological efforts when they are done in dependence upon and in
cooperation with the Holy Spirit—if we first have accepted the absolute
soteriological perfection purchased for us by the blood of His Son.
        Confirmation is derivable from the writings of James. Unlike Paul,
who hardly had anything good to say about justification by works,* James
has nothing bad to say about justification by works (2:21,24-25). The
primary difference is not to be found in how they use the phrase of works (ek
ergon) but how they use the word justification. When Paul denies that
Abraham was justified by works, Paul supplies the reason: “Now to the one
who works, his reward is not counted as a grace, but as what is due”
(Rom 4:4; TM).
        Paul is not talking about rewards in the verse that follows it: “But to
the one who does not work, but believes in Him who justifies the ungodly,
his faith is reckoned as righteousness” (Rom 4:5). If Paul were talking about
rewards in 4:5, then (by his own admission in 4:4) he would have affirmed
that Abraham was justified by works.
        James, on the other hand, is talking about rewards. Therefore, in
complete agreement with Paul, James confirms that Abraham subsequently
was justified by works at a later point in his life when Abraham offered up
Isaac: “Was not Abraham our father justified by works, when he offered up
Isaac his son on the altar?...You see that a man is justified by works, and not




*
  Rom 6:16 is a positive exception to this rule. In this verse, Paul (like James)
positively expects believers to be justified by works, yet not soteriologically
speaking of course.
Page 22                              Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
by faith alone.” Justification for the believer at the bema (which is what
James has in view) will be by works.*
        If the justification under discussion is by grace through faith, then
such justification involves a nonmeritorious response and is a gift. If the
justification is by grace through works, then this type of justification requires
a meritorious response and is a reward. That which is earned is not freely
given. Proper deduction from Paul’s emphasis in Rom 11:6, therefore,
allows amplification: “But if it is by grace [apart from works], it is no longer
on the basis of works, otherwise grace is no longer grace.” If something is
by grace apart from works, then it is unmerited. Paul himself supplies this
clarification: “For we maintain that a man is justified by faith apart from
works of the Law” (Rom 3:28). “Just as David also speaks of the blessing
upon the man to whom God reckons righteousness apart from works”
(Rom 4:6). Justification (in the form of imputational righteousness) is apart
from works and thus unmerited.
        On the other hand, Paul does not deny that rewards may be graciously
earned. Grace and works are not mutually exclusive in all aspects: “But by
the grace of God I am what I am, and His grace toward me did not prove
vain; but I labored even more than all of them, yet not I, but the grace of
God with me” (1Cor 15:10). By the power of God’s enabling grace, Paul
outperformed them all. Grace and works can be companions. Grace-based
performance is expected of Christians. However, if the result is based
exclusively on grace (and therefore implicitly excludes works), which is the
case in Rom 11:6, then the result is a gift not based on works.

         Misthological Mercy
       This same phenomenon discerned concerning grace is also observable
in the use of mercy. The concept of merited mercy has its roots in the OT,
where God says, “And I will have mercy through a thousand generations on
those who have love for me and keep my laws” (both Ex 20:6 and Dt 5:10 in
BBE; cp. Dt 7:9; Dan 9:4). If you love God, He will have mercy on you.
This thought may be the background for the NT exhortation: “Keep
yourselves in the love of God, waiting anxiously for the mercy of our Lord
Jesus Christ to eternal life” (Jude 1:21). To put this coarsely, believers are
exhorted to keep themselves as the object of God’s love (i.e., to keep God
loving them) so that they can obtain the misthological mercy expressed in
the form of misthological life. Of course, the way we keep ourselves in this
realm of God’s misthological love is by loving Him (Dt 7:12-13). Unlike
soteriological love, this misthological dimension of agape love is
conditional upon the believer’s performance.10


*
    See Jam 2:14 in Salvation: A Categorical Analysis.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 23
       In each of these OT passages above, the Hebrew chesed
(lovingkindness, also commonly transliterated as hesed) is translated into
Greek by the LXX as eleos (mercy). Many would say that chesed may also
be translated as grace. If so, then these texts also supply endorsement for the
concept of misthological grace.
       Mercy is popularly defined as God withholding something bad from
us that we deserve (such as punishment). Grace, in contrast, is commonly
defined as God giving something good to us that we do not deserve.
Nonetheless, this typical differentiation between mercy and grace is open to
question since in Lk 10:37 the Good Samaritan (and thus the good neighbor)
is described as the one who shows mercy (eleos). The Samaritan did
something good for the beaten man that the latter did not deserve. This
would be a picture of grace according to popular definition. So the popular
distinction between mercy and grace is not necessarily valid.
       Likewise, the frequent assumption derived from this parable is that
we deserve neither mercy nor grace. We are presumed to be like the beaten
man who can do nothing to deserve the merciful grace that God bestows
upon us. Once again, however, this popular presumption is not necessarily
true. To be sure, the beaten man in this parable is shown undeserved mercy,
but this is only one side of the truth being presented about mercy within that
context. The flipside is that the Samaritan earns mercy from God by his
works of mercy. By showing undeserved mercy toward another, the
Samaritan earns mercy from God. In the parable of the Good Samaritan, we
are instructed to show mercy so that we may inherit eternal life (Lk 10:25).
Conceptually, this parable is parallel to the truth that Jesus teaches in
Lk 14:12-14. If you perform works of mercy for those who cannot repay
you, you will be repaid by God. If you want to be paid mercy, show mercy.
Such mercy from God is earned by showing unearned mercy to others.
       In Rom 9:10-18, mercy is unmerited. Yet in passages such as Mt 5:7
and Jam 2:13, the opposite is true: mercy is merited. “Blessed are the
merciful, for they shall receive mercy” (Mt 5:7). “For judgment will be
merciless to one who has shown no mercy; mercy triumphs over judgment”
(Jam 2:13). Indeed, mercy and grace are combined in Heb 4:16 and are not
automatic to the believer: “Let us therefore draw near with confidence to the
throne of grace, that we may receive mercy and may find grace to help in
time of need.” Mercy and grace must be appropriated on a daily basis by
faith. Likewise, temporal and misthological grace is conditioned on
perseverance. Perseverance in faith is required for preservation in this realm
of grace. As already deduced, the answer as to why grace may promote merit
or prohibit merit is to be found in the simple observation that when grace is
the basis, then merit is prohibited. Alternatively, when grace is the result,
then merit is promoted.
Page 24                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

         Mega Grace
        We became believers through grace (Acts 18:27).11 Grace makes
faith possible (cp. Jn 6:44). God’s enabling grace makes faith possible, not
irresistible. In turn, we are saved by grace through faith (Eph 2:8). When
we respond in faith to God’s grace, we are made passive recipients of His
saving grace. Once we experience His saving grace and receive imputed
righteousness, His grace enables us to live righteously by faith so that we
may in turn actively earn misthological grace. The sequence then is: grace-
faith-grace-faith-grace.*
                        Illustration 1. Grace-Faith Spiral




*
    Compare the “Grace-Works Spiral” in Like a Tree Planted by the Water.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                     Page 25
       The grace-faith interaction is initialized by grace but activated by
faith, which spirals outward in ever-increasing circles in our lives. As long
as the interaction continues, grace and faith increase. God has more and
more grace to give as we continue to walk in humble obedience to His will.
There is more grace available to us if we are willing to earn it: “He gives
more grace. Therefore He says: ‘God resists the proud, but gives grace to the
humble.’” (Jam 4:6; NKJ). This greater grace in Jam 4:6 is misthological
grace. This expression mega grace (megas charis) is also found in Acts 4:33
and associated with having mega power (megas dunamis). In the context of
Jam 4:6, however, mega grace is equated with the exaltation found a few
verses later in Jam 4:10.

            But He gives a greater grace. Therefore it says,
      God is opposed to the proud, but gives grace to the humble…
   Humble yourselves in the presence of the Lord, and He will exalt you.

        The misthological nature of this mega grace is readily apparent. You
humble yourself so that God will give you grace in the form of exaltation.
Peter taught the same truth: “Humble yourselves, therefore, under the
mighty hand of God, that He may exalt you at the proper time” (1Pet 5:6).
The reason provided by James and Peter for our humbling ourselves before
God is so that we may be exalted by God. James throws in the added
clarification that this reward is a form of mega grace.

      Contingent Grace
       This harmonization between performance and God’s resulting grace
is suggested in Paul’s writings as well. In fact, the same Pauline epistle
which contains one of the most beloved verses in all the Bible concerning
unmerited grace, namely Eph 2:2-9, concludes with a reference to
misthological grace: “Grace be with all those who love our Lord Jesus Christ
with a love incorruptible” (Eph 6:24). Here Paul pronounces a special
blessing of grace upon those believers who already have been saved by
grace. However, this extra dimension of grace from God is invoked for
believers who love the Lord Jesus with an incorruptible love.
       A Calvinistic approach might take lovers of Christ as a metonymy for
believers in Christ. This approach limits saving grace to believers who love
Christ with a love that perseveres and thus assumes that the love of all
genuine believers will persevere. As a result, genuine believers will never
completely or totally fall from the state of saving grace. An Arminian
approach, conversely, would be to argue that not all believers in Christ
persevere in their love for Christ and, therefore, may fall from the sphere of
saving grace. Actually, from both the Calvinistic and Arminian perspectives,
continuation in love for Christ is a soteriological condition for continuation
Page 26                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
in state of saving grace. Arminians just tend to be a little more forthright
about admitting it.
        A Marrowistic mediation, however, is available in-between these two
approaches that affirms that believers are unconditionally secure in not
falling from saving grace.* Bond, the GNCT commentator for Ephesians,
correctly comments regarding Eph 6:24: “Blessing or cursing come to
believers depending on whether they love the Lord Jesus.”12 Obviously,
Bond is thinking more comprehensively than this singular verse when he
mentions misthological blessing and cursing. This verse only deals with the
former.
        Although Paul normally opens and closes all his epistles with an
unqualified invocation of God’s grace (blessing) upon the entire readership
of his epistles—by virtue of the simple fact that they are saints (i.e.,
believers)—there are at least two remarkable exceptions. In 1Cor 16:22-23,
Paul invokes a blessing and curse on believers: “If anyone does not love the
Lord, let him be accursed. Maranatha. The grace of the Lord Jesus be with
you.” As rightly noted by GNTC, whether believers experience a curse (in
the form of an anathemazation) or receive a blessing (in the form of grace)
in this salutation is contingent upon their love for the Lord. Of course, this
type of closing goes back to the OT background in which blessings were
promised to Israel for loving God and curses for not doing so (Dt 6:5; 11:13-
17, 22-28; 30:16-19; Josh 23:11-13). Unfortunately, the love of this elect
nation for God did not hold fast, as the history of Israel clearly attests.
        Calvinism fails to deal adequately with the historical reality that the
love of the majority of the NT believers in Ephesus was also by-and-large
corrupted over time. At this later point of time, Jesus solemnly rebukes the
Ephesian believers when He admonishes, “I have this against you, that you
have left your first love” (Rev 2:4). Although at an earlier point in time Paul
expresses appreciation for their love in general (Eph 1:15), the love of many
of these believers failed to achieve mature stabilization. The grace Paul
envisions in Eph 6:24 advances beyond the saving grace described in
Eph 2:5-8. The misthological nature of this grace in 6:24 renders the
Arminian approach invalid as well because salvation from hell is not a
reward.
*
  Marrowism is the present author’s coined term for describing his mediating
non-Calvinistic Free Grace theology, which is positioned between Arminianism
(PEARS) and Calvinism (TULIP) and summarized herein by the author’s
acronym GRAPE. Historically, this movement was expressed in seminal form
by the Marrow Controversy. In a number of key areas, the present author may
be considered a modern day Marrowist. As used by the author, Marrowism
refers to a defense of unconditional security consisting of soteriological and
misthological consistency. (For definition of GRAPE, see Technical Terms,
viii.)
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 27
         Both Paul and James teach that loving the Lord is a misthological
issue that results in a crown. “In the future there is laid up for me the crown
of righteousness, which the Lord, the righteous Judge, will award to me on
that day; and not only to me, but also to all who have loved His appearing
(2Tim 4:8). “Blessed is a man who perseveres under trial; for once he has
been approved, he will receive the crown of life, which the Lord has
promised to those who love Him” (Jam 1:12). James likewise clarifies, just a
few verses later, that those believers who love the Lord will be the ones who
inherit the kingdom: “Listen, my beloved brethren: did not God choose the
poor of this world to be rich in faith and heirs of the kingdom which He
promised to those who love Him?” (Jam 2:5).
        The crown (thus rulership) that is promised to those believers who
love the Lord is a reward that conceptually is equivalent to inheriting the
kingdom of heaven. Of course, the only way to inherit a kingdom is as a
king or queen. Inheriting a kingdom entails far more than merely entering a
kingdom.
        Christians can be accursed for not loving the Lord (1Cor 16:22) or
blessed for loving the Lord (Eph 6:24). Blessing and curses, as both
temporal and misthological (R) results, are conditioned on our perseverance
in intellectual, volitional, and emotional affection toward the Lord and in our
devotion to, and satisfaction with, the Lord. The believer has to choose
between crowns or curses.
        Crowns are not the only tangible thing at stake. The tree of life was
promised misthologically to those believers in Ephesus who had an
overcoming love (Rev 2:7). God’s grace in Eph 6:24 is likewise limited to
those believers who love the Lord with a love that is immortal and
incorruptible. Enduring rewards are reserved for believers who have an
undying love for the Lord.
        Our soteriological salvation is based on His love for us, not on our
love for Him. However, our misthological salvation is contingent upon our
loving response to Him. Regardless of which aspect of salvation is being
discussed, salvation is by grace from start to finish. Justification,
sanctification, and co-glorification salvation are all by grace. The Christian
experience is intended to be one of grace from beginning to end.
Misthological grace is available to those who have been saved by grace and
who now humble themselves through God’s enabling grace.

       Rejected Grace
       Believers may fall from, fall short of, and be cut off from grace—if
they fail to walk by faith in obedience to God’s commands. Obviously, grace
is not necessarily irresistible in such contexts; otherwise, believers could not
fall from it. Perseverance in neither grace nor faith is guaranteed. The
immediate question that one encounters in response to this affirmation is,
“Can believers lose their salvation?” Of course they can! Salvation is by
Page 28                                Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
grace. To reject God’s grace is to reject His salvation. Those believers who
reject God’s grace have forfeited the salvation that otherwise could have
been theirs if they had remained in grace. So what affect does this have on
their eternal security? Absolutely none!
        Grace, like salvation, is a multifaceted term. It is possible for
believers to fall from grace theologically, experientially, temporally, and
misthologically and thus fail to experience the fullness of the salvation that
could have been theirs otherwise. Nevertheless, the soteriological foundation
cannot be demolished because it was never based on the believer’s
performance to begin with (1Cor 3:11-12; 2Tim 2:19). Soteriological
justification already has been accomplished and is never based on
performance. Thus, even if we fail to achieve the fullness that is available to
us by grace, we do not lose what already has been given freely to us by
grace.

         Being Cut off from Grace in Romans

         Standing in Grace
       Understandably, some are impressed by the tense and verb choice in
Rom 5:2 and consider this an example of soteriological grace: “Through
whom also we have [come to have] access by faith into this grace in which
we [have come to] stand, and rejoice in hope of the glory of God” (NKJ).
Access and stand are both perfect tense* verbs in Greek. Thus, some would
appeal to this verse as a picture of unconditional security and claim that our
stand is unmovable. It is as if we were standing in concrete.
       On the other hand, acceptance of unconditional security does not
require agreement with this assessment of grace in v. 2. Our continued
access to the grace, being discussed within in this verse, appears to be
conditioned on perseverance in faith. The soteriological dimension of this
grace was accessed permanently by punctiliar faith. This soteric result of
soteriological grace has to be permanent because the future justification
already has been granted, as noted in the previous verse: “Therefore having
been justified [z▬▬ ] by faith, we have peace with God through our Lord
Jesus Christ” (Rom 5:1). Our initial entry with its resulting permanent
perfect state is thus envisioned. Nevertheless, although the justification of v.
1 is soteriological and eternal, this need not necessarily mean that our
standing in v. 2 advances no further than this soteriological foundation.




*
    The perfect tense shows our present state (▬ ), not our present action.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 29

       Access to Grace
        Is it really necessary to limit the picture of grace in Rom 5:2 to a
static, permanent, positional standing? Is this merely soteriological grace? Is
the verse saying that by punctiliar faith we have made a punctiliar and
permanent entry into grace?
        Apparently, this grace of v. 2 refers back to Rom 5:1 and to the
soteriological justification in that preceding verse. But does Paul want to
limit grace to that dimension? Is he not saying that the same grace that saved
us from the penalty of sin can also save us from the power of sin? Later in
this same chapter, Paul seemingly refers to a experience of grace that results
temporally in experiential righteousness and misthologically in eternal life:
“Even so grace might reign through righteousness to eternal life through
Jesus Christ our Lord” (Rom 5:21). The only other use of this word for
access (prosagoge) is in Eph 2:18 and 3:12, and in these passages Paul
suggests a dynamic rather than soteriological access: “In whom we have
boldness and confident access through faith in Him” (Eph 3:12). In
Heb 4:16, a believer’s receiving dynamic grace is conditioned on daily
appropriation: “Let us therefore draw near with confidence to the throne of
grace, that we may receive mercy and may find grace to help in time of
need.” Thus, the words and context do not indicate that our standing in grace
prevents us from falling from grace. Rather, our daily allocation of grace is
conditioned on our daily appropriation.

       Tense
       What about the tense? Does the perfect tense indicate that we stand
immoveable in grace? The perfect tense used to describe our standing in
grace by faith in Rom 5:2 is also used later by Paul within Romans (again in
reference to standing), but it does not denote permanence: “Quite right, they
were broken off for their unbelief, but you [have come to] stand [z▬ ] by
your faith.* Do not be conceited, but fear” (Rom 11:20). The you refers to
the readership, that is, to believers. Paul is describing their standing yet
again by faith with the perfect tense, but he adds a warning in which he
explains why these believers should fear in spite of their present standing:
“For if God did not spare the natural branches, neither will He spare you.
Behold then the kindness and severity of God; to those who fell, severity,
but to you, God’s kindness, if you continue in His kindness; otherwise you
also will be cut off” (Rom 11:21-22). Hence, believers may indeed be cut off
from the kindness of God’s grace if they do not persevere in faith.13

*
 The typical symbol for the perfect state (z▬ ) only denotes the original action
and resulting state. The permanent perfect state (z▬▬ ) accentuates the state by
an elongated bar to denote its contextually deduced permanence.
Page 30                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
Certainly, believers would not lose the soteriological benefits of that grace
which already has been permanently and irrevocably granted (Rom 5:1;
11:29), but believers could lose any temporal or misthological benefits to
which they otherwise might have been entitled.*

       Unconditional Security in Galatians
        In discussing a believer’s security with a group of Arminians, the
author had opportunity to share his conviction that the falling from grace (a
form of apostasy) experienced by the believers at Galatia is a strong
argument in favor of unconditional security. When it comes to the subject of
apostasy, the book of Hebrews is a favorite among Arminians since it
acknowledges the potential apostasy of believers. This being the case, the
book of Galatians should provide an even greater opportunity to consider
Arminian claims since this epistle describes the actual apostasy of believers.
Yet, like the book of Hebrews, Galatians makes it clear that what is at stake
is rewards, especially loss of inheritance rights, not the complete loss of all
aspects of salvation. The soteriological benefits remain intact.
        In the book of Galatians, Paul actually asserts the believers’ sonship
despite the severe moral and theological failure on their part. Sonship, like
salvation, has static and dynamic components. Paul is, therefore, confirming
their static sonship. Contrastively, there are passages in which sonship may
be lost (see below on Hebrews). Such passages reveal the dynamic element
of sonship. The loss of the dynamic status has no impact on the static
element. In other words, the eternal security of the sons of God is not
jeopardized by whether or not they live like children of God.

       Thessalonian Parallel
        The duality of this sonship is evident in Paul’s exhortation to the
Thessalonian believers, to whom he says, “You are all sons of light and
sons of the day” (1Thess 5:5). He then proceeds to urge these sons of light to
live like sons of light, to be morally awake. Yet he also assures them that
they will be spared from God’s wrath and live with Jesus even if they are
morally asleep (1Thess 5:6-10).14 In other words, they are and remain God’s
sons temporally and eternally even if they fail to demonstrate this reality
dynamically. Static sonship provides the basis for dynamic sonship, but
*
  The present book does not appeal indiscriminately to the perfect tense in
defense of unconditional security. Indeed, the author has passed up making such
a defense based on the prefect tense in Rom 5:2. Therefore, the judicious appeal
to the perfect tense used in the second chapter of this book in defense of
unconditional security should be given fair hearing and not discounted offhand
by those opposed to unconditional security. Both the tense and context are
factored into the arguments being employed herein.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 31
static sonship does not guarantee dynamic expression, nor does dynamic
failure dissolve the static relationship. Put differently, the relationship
denoted by sonship in this passage is permanent. Further, the affirmation of
sonship is applied to all these believers, even the ones who are morally
asleep.

       Genuine Sonship
       The same confirmation is made by Paul concerning the believers at
Galatia: “For you are all sons of God through faith in Christ Jesus”
(Gal 3:26). Again, the pronouncement is made of all the believers, the entire
readership. Paul affirms that all the persons intended by his address of you
are sons of God; they have been saved by faith.
       6
         And because you are sons, God has sent forth the Spirit of
       His Son into our hearts, crying, “Abba! Father!” 7Therefore
       you are no longer a slave, but a son; and if a son, then an heir
       through God. 8 However at that time, when you did not know
       God, you were slaves to those which by nature are no gods.
       9
         But now that you have come to know God, or rather to be
       known by God, how is it that you turn back again to the weak
       and worthless elemental things, to which you desire to be
       enslaved all over again? (Gal 4:6-9)

Paul makes a triple affirmation regarding his readers that they still are sons
even though they have fallen from grace:

•   You are all sons of God.
•   You are sons.
•   You are no longer a slave, but a son.

 Moreover, he confirms that they still have the Spirit, crying out in their
hearts, “Abba! Father!” They have become heirs of God through grace; they
are no longer slaves; they have come to a saving knowledge of God. They
became children of God in the past and remain children of God in the
present. Aspectually, their soteriological status would be described
contextually as being perfective [z▬ ]. Those addressed by you are still
regenerate Christian brethren. “And you brethren, like Isaac, are children of
promise” (Gal 4:28). They are children of God. There is no question as to
the reality of their salvation experience; they are genuine believers. They are
regenerate believers now because they became regenerate at some time in
the past and remain in that regenerate state in the present.
Page 32                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

       Genuine Apostasy
        What is astonishing is that Paul is going out of his way to make
certain that these readers realize that they are still sons of God even though
they have committed apostasy! These believers had been severed from
Christ and fallen from grace. “You have been severed from Christ, you who
are seeking to be justified by law; you have fallen from grace” (Gal 5:4).
You cannot fall from grace if you have never genuinely experienced grace,
nor can you be severed from Christ if you have never been joined to Christ.
The simultaneous genuineness and retention of their regeneration must be
confirmed in spite of their practice, not because of it!*
        They had fallen from grace both theologically and experientially.
Salvation from hell by grace through faith apart from works cannot be lost
because it already has resulted in a justification that has nothing to do with
perseverance. As will be demonstrated momentarily, it results in permanent
sonship (z▬▬ ).
        Grace as a way of life and as a theological perception can easily be
lost. These Galatian Christians had fumbled the ball. Of course, you cannot
fumble it if you never had it, but these believers had started well. “You were
running well; who hindered you from obeying the truth?” (Gal 5:7) They
successfully had received the handoff and had started running with the ball
and then fumbled it. Certainly, this good start included having experienced
regeneration (being part of the team). Paul, like a referee, blows the whistle
on them and cries, “Fumble!” Yet what they fumbled was not regeneration,
as is clear since Paul reminds them that they are (even now still in the
present) sons of God and have the Spirit (and remain part of the team).
        Paul is upset with the membership at large because of their apostasy.
The point being made herein does not require that every single individual in
the church be guilty of apostasy. Observing that the church in general had
committed apostasy suffices. Paul exclaims, “You foolish Galatians, who
has bewitched you” (Gal 3:1)! Finding one example of a believer who
remained a son of God in spite of his or her apostasy is all that is necessary
in order to prove that apostasy does not nullify sonship. Here, Paul shows us
a whole church full of them! They had left the true gospel for a false one.
        In his opening address to this church, Paul makes it clear that this is
no small doctrinal defection. The juxtaposition of Greek words for different
(heteros) and another (allos) in Gal 1:6-7 is often noted as showing that Paul
is stressing that they completely have abandoned the kind of gospel which
he preached to them for a totally different type of gospel. Accepting this
acknowledgement and coupling it with LXX syntactical negation allow
simultaneous concurrence with other translations that acknowledge that this
*
 For a review of apostasy and list of similar passages, see “Apostasy” in The
Outer Darkness.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 33
alternative form of the gospel is not truly the good news of the gospel at all.
Paul expresses shock: “ I am amazed that you are so quickly deserting Him
who called you by the grace of Christ, for a different kind of gospel; which
is not even a gospel. But only there are some who are disturbing you and
want to distort the gospel of Christ” (Gal 1:6-7; TM). They are deserting
Christ for a false gospel.
        This defection is perhaps a little more clearly pronounced if Paul’s
subsequent description of their defection is rendered as follows. “You were
running well; who has hindered you from being persuaded [peitho] by the
truth? This dissuasion [peismone] did not come from Him who calls you”
(Gal 5:7-8; TM).15 They had ceased to obey the truth by ceasing to yield to
its persuasion. The play on words in Greek with the cognate of peitho in v. 8
and the repetition of peitho in v. 10 leads to the conclusion that Paul is
focusing primarily on intellectual assent to the truth rather than on volitional
assent. He is using this play on words to express the irony that although they
ceased to be persuaded by the truth of the gospel (because of those
disturbing them), he nevertheless continues to be persuaded that they would
return to the correct persuasion (despite his being disturbed by their
defection and those disturbing them). In other words, he is using himself as a
personal example to show that being disturbed is no excuse for one’s faith
being disrupted. He was disturbed but nevertheless remained confident of
their return. Notwithstanding his confidence, let us not minimize the nature
of their departure.
        A horse obeys its master by submitting to its master volitionally (cp.
Jam 3:3). But a believer obeys the soteric truth of the gospel by submitting
to it intellectually, or stated more precisely, persuasionally. In this context
peitho should be allowed to have its normal meaning of being persuaded.
These believers were no longer persuaded by the gospel. They no longer
believed it. Paul expressed the same concern earlier: “You foolish Galatians,
who has bewitched you from being persuaded by the truth” (Gal 3:1; MT;
TM). Truth must be believed to be obeyed. Certainly, believing the wrong
thing often accompanies doing the wrong thing. These believers were
running into the ditch on both accounts and wrecking their lives in the
process. Still, what Paul is pressing home most vividly in these passages is
that these believers were no longer believers since they no longer believed
the true gospel. Dynamically, they were no longer believers.

       Permanent Sonship
       Yet amazingly Paul repeatedly reassures his readers that they are still
God’s sons (in the soteric sense of belonging to God as His regenerate
children)—despite their failure. Paul interweaves his affirmations of their
sonship within his warnings, repeatedly addressing the same group of people
with you:
Page 34                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
•   You are completely deserting the gospel of grace (Gal 1:6-8).
•   You are all sons of God (Gal 3:26).
•   You are sons (Gal 4:6).
•   You have the Spirit (Gal 4:6).
•   You are a son (Gal 4:6).
•   You have come to know God (Gal 4:9).
•   You turned back from God (Gal 4:9).
•   You are children of promise (Gal 4:28).
•   You will be profited nothing by Christ (Gal 5:2).16
•   You have been severed from Christ (Gal 5:4).
•   You have fallen from grace (Gal 5:4).
•   You are brethren (Gal 1:11; 3:15; 4:12,28,31; 5:11,13; 6:1,18).17

       They all remain God’s sons even though they have been severed from
Christ and fallen from grace! Theological defection does not nullify static
relation.18 The only conclusion that can be drawn is that Paul indeed fully
intends to affirm the unconditional security of the believer’s relationship
with God with the word son in these passages. In terms of relationship, these
sons were still believers even though they no longer believed. Astonishing!
As expressed in the following syllogism, the Arminian response that
believers only remain God’s children as long as they continue to believe
completely misses the point.
                          Illustration 2. Fallen Sons
       1. You are sons (Gal 3:26).
       2. You have fallen from grace (Gal 5:4).
       3. Therefore, you are sons who have fallen from grace.

       They remained sons even though they had fallen from grace. To be
sure, Paul says, “You are all sons of God through faith in Christ Jesus”
(Gal 3:26). However, they are not sons of God because of their current
faith (which is soteriologically defective as Paul points out repeatedly) but
because of their original faith. Their original saving response of faith to the
gospel (as Paul originally had preached it to them) had been abandoned. This
original response of faith had resulted in their experiencing spiritual birth
and becoming sons of God soteriologically. And they retain that original
status of sonship in the present regardless of their failure to remain in grace.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                         Page 35
             Illustration 3. Fallen Sons are Still Regenerate Sons




        These fallen brethren had been made, and continued to remain sons,
of God because of their past faith which they now had abandoned. Paul
affirms that they retain their classification as sons of God, although they
have abandoned their original faith. The Arminian contention that one must
continue in faith in order to remain a son of God in this soteriological sense
fails to do justice to the context. Static classification continues despite
discontinuation in the means by which that classification was derived.* Or
stated differently, these Christian unbelievers were still believers (in terms of
being possessors of regeneration) because of the effects of their original
belief.

       Misthological Warning to Soteriological Heirs
       The problem was not that they had lost their salvation from hell or
that they never genuinely had believed the gospel; rather, their problem was
that they were failing to press on to the full experience and implications of
their salvation to which Paul enjoined them: “But I say, walk by the Spirit,
and you will not carry out the desire of the flesh” (Gal 5:16). Like the epistle
of Romans, Galatians contains a warning addressed to the readership with
the word you. “I forewarn you just as I have forewarned you that those who
practice such things shall not inherit the kingdom of God” (Gal 5:21). This
verse is either a litmus test to determine who is a genuine believer, or it is a



*
 Also see “Dynamic Classification” and “Static Classification” in Believe: An
Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the Gospel of John.
Page 36                              Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
test to evaluate if the genuine believer is a misthological heir.* The latter is
preferable.
        Calvinistic LS takes Gal 5:21 as proof that those who practice sin are
not genuinely saved. However, the logical conclusion of such a position is
that although salvation is initially by grace, it is ultimately by works. In the
end, the believer’s practice will determine whether or not that believer
reaches heaven or goes to hell. In the final analysis, this LS theology teaches
that you cannot reach heaven (i.e., soterically inherit the kingdom from the
LS perspective) without gaining victory over these fleshly activities. As the
text makes clear, practice of such sins makes it impossible to inherit the
kingdom. Thus, LS logically must conclude that your performance
determines whether you will reach heaven, since inheriting the kingdom is
equated with soteriological entrance from their perspective.
        In such an equation, practical sanctification (i.e., the victorious
Christian life) becomes a theological and logical necessity to reach heaven.
If you do not progress in sanctification to the point of experiencing and
maintaining the victorious Christian life, then according to LS you will not
reach heaven since this level of performance is necessary to reach/inherit the
kingdom, which LS takes as synonymous with entering the kingdom. Such
sanctification is inevitable for the genuine believer from this LS perspective.
You cannot reach heaven without persevering in practical sanctification. The
possibility of long-term Christian carnality is practically denied in LS. Even
some otherwise fine FG Salvationists have, unwittingly perhaps, adopted
this Lordship view of inheriting the kingdom without realizing their
theological inconsistency.
        The alternative Marrowistic interpretation being defended herein is
consistent with FG and maintains that salvation from hell is both initially
and ultimately by grace. This FG view can be held consistently in
Gal 5:21—if inheriting the kingdom is understood as dealing with
misthology (rewards within heaven) rather than soteriology (entrance into
heaven). In the book of Galatians, eternal security applies even to those who
commit apostasy.
        To reiterate the arguments in favor of this Marrowistic view of
sonship, this discussion began by noting that the warning of Gal 5:21 is
addressed explicitly to the readers with the repeated word you. Thus, the
warning is addressed to genuine believers, as the context of the epistle
makes clear. The problem is not that they had not been saved by grace in the
first place. Certainly, they had come to saving faith in Christ. After all, a
person cannot desert Christ unless that individual has first come to Christ.
Nevertheless, as Paul warns these believers, just because they are heirs of
God this does not mean that they unconditionally will inherit the kingdom.
