    See “Misthological Inheritance” in The Outer Darkness.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 37
Realization of this kingdom aspect of their inheritance is conditioned on
their performance (Gal 5:21). Soteriological sonship had qualified them to
be misthological heirs; however, the soteriological qualification to live in
such a manner that they might obtain the kingdom prize did not assure them
that they would actualize their potential inheritance. Substandard practice
will not qualify soteriological heirs to receive the misthological inheritance.
Active inheritance can indeed be lost.*
        The Calvinistic Lordship interpretation of Gal 5:21 teaches that all
believers will walk by the Spirit. Paul, in contrast, teaches just the opposite.
He commands believers to walk by the Spirit; he does not promise them that
they will walk by the Spirit. Paul only presents two alternatives. Believers
will either walk by the flesh or by the Spirit. The exhortation to walk by the
Spirit would be pointless if addressed to illegitimate believers who had not
experienced regeneration by the Spirit. False believers need to be exhorted
to experience spiritual birth rather than to take a spiritual walk. Logically,
Paul’s exhortation to walk by the Spirit must be aimed at those who have
been born by the Spirit. Accordingly, if the exhortation to walk by the Spirit
in Gal 5:16 uses the word you to address genuine believers, as maintained
herein, then the warning of v. 21 must be understood likewise:

           I forewarn you just as I have forewarned you that those who
           practice such things shall not inherit the kingdom of God
           (Gal 5:21).

       Paul is saying that as a believer you are either going to walk by the
flesh or the Spirit. The fact that these believers had been born of the Spirit
does not guarantee that they will walk by the Spirit; otherwise, Paul would
not have had to exhort these genuine believers to walk by the Spirit. All
believers do not walk by the Spirit. Therefore, not all believers will inherit
the kingdom. Only those believers who heed the exhortation and warning
will achieve the desired goal of inheriting the kingdom.
       The soteriological interpretation of Gal 5:21, on the other hand,
conditions entrance into the kingdom on how we walk, that is, how we live.
According to this interpretation, the way we live determines whether or not
we will enter heaven. A soteriological paraphrase of Gal 5:21 could be
rendered as, “Those who practice such things [i.e., live in such a manner]
shall not inherit the kingdom of God [i.e., enter heaven].” Yet to say that the
way we live determines whether we will enter heaven is highly inconsistent
with Pauline theology. Contrastively, to affirm that rewards in heaven are
determined by the way a believer lives is thoroughly consistent with Pauline
theology and with the NT. Contrary to conditional security, one’s initial

*
    See “Sons of God” in The Outer Darkness.
Page 38                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
reaction to this text should be to suspect that it is dealing with rewards
within heaven rather than entrance into heaven. This misthological
understanding of Paul’s words is confirmed by his statement concerning
inheritance elsewhere.
        23
            Whatever you do, do your work heartily, as for the Lord
         rather than for men; 24 knowing that from the Lord you will
         receive the reward of the inheritance. It is the Lord Christ
         whom you serve. 25 For he who does wrong will receive the
         consequences of the wrong which he has done, and that
         without partiality (Col 3:23-25)

       Paul explicitly says that the inheritance is a reward, not a gift. This
inheritance is conditioned on the things believers do—the way believers live.
Entering heaven is one thing; owning it is quite another. The alternatives
being presented to these believers are merely entering the kingdom versus
becoming a ruler of that kingdom. Rulership of the kingdom is not free. This
privilege may be lost or forfeited; hence, the basis for the warning is
logically consistent with the Marrowistic position. As a result, this
misthological interpretation is to be preferred to the soteriological
interpretation. The context of Gal 5 (and the whole book of Galatians for
that matter) contrasts the flesh with the Spirit. Those who seek justification
or sanctification based on the law are seeking to be justified or sanctified by
fleshly performance rather than by grace.19 Paul is asserting that carnal
(fleshy) believers will not inherit the kingdom.
       Since carnal (legalistic) believers will not inherit the kingdom,
equating inheriting the kingdom with entering the kingdom logically leads to
the erroneous conclusion that carnal believers are not genuine believers.
Since the conclusion that carnal believers are lost is false, the premise that
inheriting the kingdom is equivalent to entering it must also be false. The
conclusion is false because the premise upon which it is built is false.
Simplistically equating entering the kingdom with inheriting the kingdom is
a grave mistake.

         Coming Short Of Grace in Hebrews
       Popular articles concerning good parenting relate the importance of
having high expectations for your children. In the book of Hebrews, God
likewise is presented as a good Father who has high expectations of His
children. God expects His children to share His holiness experientially
(Heb 12:10)—to be righteous in their behavior (Heb 12:11)—so that they
can have the special access to Him described as seeing Him (Heb 12:14).*

*
    See “Misthologically Seeing God” in The Outer Darkness.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                     Page 39
According to Bailey, the three primary motivations that Scripture gives
believers for meeting God’s expectations are, in order of frequency, the
following: (1) rewards, (2), fear, and (3) love.20 Not surprisingly, then, the
writer of Hebrews gives rewards and fear as the reasons to respond
positively to God’s loving discipline:
      5
        You have forgotten the exhortation which is addressed to you
      as sons, “My son, do not regard lightly the discipline of the
      Lord, nor faint when you are reproved by Him; 6 for those
      whom the Lord loves He disciplines, and He scourges every
      son whom He receives.” 7 It is for discipline that you endure;
      God deals with you as with sons; for what son is there whom
      his father does not discipline? 8 But if you are without
      discipline, of which all have become partakers, then you are
      illegitimate children [nothos] and not sons [huios].
      9
        Furthermore, we had earthly fathers to discipline us, and we
      respected them; shall we not much rather be subject to the
      Father of spirits, and live?…15 See to it that no one comes short
      of the grace of God; that no root of bitterness springing up
      causes trouble, and by it many be defiled; 16 that there be no
      immoral or godless person like Esau, who sold his own
      birthright for a single meal. 17 For you know that even
      afterwards, when he desired to inherit the blessing, he was
      rejected, for he found no place for repentance, though he
      sought for it with tears. (Heb 12:5-17)

      Two Types of Sons
        Although the most frequently used motivation in Scripture—
rewards—is employed in this passage by the writer of Hebrews, this
mistholic motivation is often overlooked, since it is embedded in two
different types of sonship. Soteriological Sonship is the status that you have
by virtue of your birth as a child of God. Misthological sonship is the status
that you have by virtue of your performance as a child of God. All believers
are sons/heirs in a general soteric sense, but only faithful believers are
sons/heirs in the fullest sense. As shown in the highlighted text above,
illegitimate children are rejected, but sons inherit the blessing. Two
different types of children are being presented by the writer of Hebrews.

      Heb 12:5
      And you have forgotten the exhortation which is addressed to
      you as sons, “My son, do not regard lightly the discipline of the
      Lord, nor faint when you are reproved by Him.”
Page 40                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

       You = Brethren
       The designation you addresses God’s children (i.e., His sons) in the
passage. The broader context of Hebrews makes it clear that genuine
believers are in view. The author of Hebrews affirms those being addressed
as you are “holy brethren, partakers of the heavenly calling” (3:1). He
addresses the warning of falling away to the brethren (3:12). And he
concludes his letter by stating that the exhortations are addressed to them as
brethren (13:22).

       We = Permanently Sanctified
       The writer of Hebrews includes himself with his readership as those
who have been passively and permanently sanctified but who are
nevertheless in danger (10:10,14,26). He includes himself with the readers as
those to whom the warnings apply (2:1-3; 10:26). He explicitly states that
the topic he is dealing with is the judgment of God’s people rather than
God’s judgment upon the lost (10:30). The writer specifically states that
what is at stake for his readers is the loss of rewards (10:35).

       Two Types of Failures (Despise/Faint)
       There are two basic responses to God’s disciple in this passage.
Believers can either endure it (Heb 12:7) or fail to endure it (Heb 12:5). The
failures of this current passage are broken up into two types of believers in v.
5. On the one hand, there are believers who take God’s discipline too lightly;
on the other hand, there are believers who regard it too heavily.
       Those who despise the discipline are in the first category. They do not
think God’s discipline has any real value and hold it in contempt. God’s
discipline is no big deal to them. They are children of God and that is all that
matters to them. In their minds, whether they live for God is of little
consequence.
       The second type of believer thinks that God is too hard on them. They
faint under God’s disciple. They lose heart. They throw in the towel. They
quit. The first type of believer ignores God’s discipline. The second type
collapses under God’s discipline.

       Heb 12:6-8
       6
         For whom the Lord loveth he chasteneth, and scourgeth every
       son whom he receiveth. 7 If ye endure chastening, God dealeth
       with you as with sons; for what son is he whom the father
       chasteneth not? 8 But if ye be without chastisement, whereof
       all are partakers, then are ye bastards, and not sons. (KJV)
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                        Page 41

       Initial Discipline
       God disciplines all of His children initially so that they may be
entitled ultimately to partake of their full inheritance as mature sons.
However, God will only discipline His children up to a certain point. If they
persist in refusing to respond positively to His discipline, they may reach the
point where God finally says, “Ok, have it your way, no more discipline—no
more training to inherit the family estate and to run the family business. If
you want to live like a bastard, then you will be treated like a bastard.”
(Bastard is the KJV word for an illegitimate child.)
       Relating to this biblical teaching should be easy pragmatically. As our
children get increasingly older, our responsibilities as parents to discipline
them decrease. They reach a point where they are no longer considered
children. They become responsible adults, no longer subject to our child
training. Our children become completely responsible for their own actions.
God has some children who reject the earlier discipline they received from
their Father. They want to be the captain of their own fates. But it is a fatal
mistake to despise the discipline of their youth. They will be held fully
responsible for their actions.

       Without Discipline
       The bastard in v. 8 represents the believer who has despised or
fainted under the Lord’s discipline so long and to such an extent that he
reaches the point where God no longer disciples him with the purpose of
qualifying him to inherit His kingdom. Four types of God’s discipline of His
children are discussed in The Outer Darkness: (1) internal, (2) external,
(3) terminal, and (4) misthological. These levels of discipline are
progressive. God deals with His children internally and then externally. If
they reject these types of discipline, God will then discipline them terminally
and then misthologically. The types of discipline being described in vv. 6-8
belong to the first two categories. If God’s children fail to respond
appropriately to these initial types of discipline, they are still subject to His
terminal discipline and ultimately to His subsequent misthological
discipline.

       You are Illegitimate
       One reason it must be concluded that these bastards represent
regenerate believers is because the warning of being potential bastards is
addressed to the readership with the second person plural. The you refers to
genuine believers. Yet not all genuine believers will be genuine heirs. The
warnings of the book of Hebrews are addressed to genuine believers, urging
them to qualify for their heavenly inheritance. In this present warning, the
writer of Hebrews teaches that God has two types of children: illegitimate
and legitimate (Heb 12:8). Naturally, both illegitimate and legitimate
Page 42                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
children are given life by their father, but the inheritance is limited to
legitimate children. The son is the legitimate heir, the rightful heir, the legal
heir. He inherits his father’s estate in this passage. The bastard is not the
legitimate heir and thus does not inherit the kingdom.

       Illegitimate Children
        The word son is being contrasted with illegitimate child by the writer
of Hebrews. If someone is your illegitimate son, he is physically your son
but not legally your son. Similarly, to be God’s illegitimate child means to
be His child spiritually but not legally (in terms of the mistholic heirship).
Illegitimate describes believers who do not persevere and who thus lack the
legal status of sonship as a result; although, they remain God’s spiritual
children. Legal entitlement to one’s inheritance requires two things: spiritual
birth and perseverance (i.e., having God as your Father and behaving like it).
Many believers have experienced the new birth and have God as their Father
and yet refuse to live accordingly. They live like God is not their Father.
And when it comes time to inherit the kingdom, God will treat them as if
they were not His sons (i.e., His heirs). If they do not treat God as their
Father, then He will not treat them as His mistholic sons—as the legal heirs
to His throne. They are His children, to be sure, but they never matured
under His discipline. Mature sons rule; immature children do not.
                     Illustration 4. Two Types of Children


                                     Children




              legitimate                            illegitimate




                  sons                                bastards




               inherited                            disinherited
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 43

       Types of Illegitimacy
        Hodges notes that “in the Roman world, an ‘illegitimate child’ had no
inheritance rights.” Illegitimate children in the passage refers to believers
who lose their misthological inheritance as a result of disobedience.21 The
KJV translates nothos as bastards. From the OT background and from the
Greek dictionaries, two types of illegitimate children may be distinguished.
In consideration of the OT background, the lexicons by Strong as well as by
Lindel-Scott note two types of bastards: (1) a child born out of wedlock, an
illegitimate child or (2) one born of a slave or concubine.
        Legally, being a bastard is contrasted with being a son. The Greek
word for son (huios) means: son; descendant, offspring, heir. The son is the
heir; the bastard is not. The point of this contrast by the author of Hebrews is
to use an illustration that would be readily understandable to his readers in
terms of their contemporary society and in terms of their OT background in
order to present the same truth that Paul expressed in the book of Galatians.
The point being made by both biblical writers is that believers who
otherwise would be qualified to inherit the kingdom may fail to do so if they
do not persevere in grace. As already noted lexically, the Greek word for
bastard may refer to one born out of wedlock or to one born of a slave, but
in either case the child was not entitled to the inheritance. Examples for both
types of bastards are observable in the OT.

       Out of Wedlock
       From the OT background, note that Gilead’s legitimate sons drove off
his illegitimate son, Jephthah, so that Jephthah would not share in the
inheritance because he was a bastard—the son of a harlot.
       1
         Now Jephthah the Gileadite was a valiant warrior, but he was
       the son of a harlot. And Gilead was the father of Jephthah.
       2
         And Gilead's wife bore him sons; and when his wife’s sons
       grew up, they drove Jephthah out and said to him, “You shall
       not have an inheritance in our father’s house, for you are the
       son of another woman” (Jdg 11:1-2).

      Gilead’s legitimate sons had a legal right to reject Jephthah as an heir.
As the text states in the KJV, Gilead begat Jephthah: Gilead was the
physical father, and Jephthah was his physical son, born of his seed.
Nonetheless, this physical birth did not entitle Jephthah to the birthright.
Similarly, the writer of Hebrews also points out that spiritual birth does not
automatically entitle those children whom God begets to the birthright.
God’s children can be considered bastards regarding the inheritance. Being
born of His seed does not necessarily mean being entitled to His throne.
Page 44                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
        Son of a Bondwoman
        Paul also appeals to the OT background to a make a corresponding
point in the book of Galatians. As noted, bastard can also mean son of a
bondwoman, and Paul emphatically warns the Galatian believers that such
bastards will not inherit the kingdom: “But what does the Scripture say?
‘Cast out the bondwoman and her son, for the son of the bondwoman shall
not be an heir with the son of the free woman’” (Gal 4:30). Paul states that
the Galatians are heirs of God through faith (Gal 3:29; 4:7). These Galatian
heirs are qualified soteriologically to be heirs. By virtue of their spiritual
birth, they are qualified to inherit the promises. But the mistholic realization
of this promised inheritance is based on the way they live (Gal 5:21). Paul
warns them that if they live like sons of the bondwoman, they will be treated
like sons of the bondwoman (i.e., as bastards).
        Abraham had two sons: Isaac and Ishmael. He was the physical father
of both boys. Nevertheless, Ishmael was a bastard because he was the son of
the bondwoman. He was not the rightful heir. As noted above, one meaning
for bastard can mean son of a slave or concubine, and Ishmael was just that:
“Abram’s wife Sarai took Hagar the Egyptian, her maid, and gave her to her
husband Abram as his wife” (Gen 16:3). Ishmael, the son born to Abraham
by Hagar, was a bastard. Paul’s point is that Ishmael was a misthological
bastard since Ishmael was the son of a slave. Ishmael was not entitled to the
inheritance. The fact that Ishmael was a bastard does not mean that Abraham
was not his physical father. Likewise, the fact that some Christians are
bastards does not mean that God is not their spiritual Father. Being a bastard
simply means that such believers will be rejected from inheriting the
kingdom. Only those believers who have the legal status as sons will inherit
the kingdom. Sons inherit the blessings; bastards are rejected regarding the
inheritance (cp. Heb 12:7 with 12:17). The inheritance is a reward based on
how believers respond to God’s discipline (see Heb 6:12; cp. Col 3:23-25).
Soteriological status, as well as misthological status, is not obtained by
fleshly performance in obedience to the OT law but through faith in God’s
saving grace and His enabling grace, respectively.

       Promised Inheritance
       The writer of Hebrews likewise warns his born-again readers that
receiving the promised inheritance is based on endurance: “For you have
need of endurance, so that when you have done the will of God, you may
receive what was promised” (Heb 10:36). He desires that they “may not be
sluggish, but imitators of those who through faith and patience inherit the
promises (Heb 6:12). And he cites Abraham as an example: “And thus,
having patiently waited, he obtained the promise” (Heb 6:15).
       Through patiently enduring God’s discipline, believers mature and
qualify for the realization of the promised inheritance. They can either
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 45
inherit the blessing or be rejected from inheriting it (Heb 12:17). Those
believers who fail to endure will lose their inheritance. They will be
disinherited. Those who have been born of the Spirit must walk by the Spirit
in order to inherit the kingdom of their Father. In terms of the inheritance,
they will be treated like bastards and disinherited from the realization of the
potential misthological inheritance they could have received. They are
children of God, but if they fail to live like it, they would not be considered
heirs of the kingdom.

       Heb 12:9
       Furthermore, we had earthly fathers to discipline us, and we
       respected them; shall we not much rather be subject to the
       Father of spirits, and live?

       The point of this verse is that just as we are subject to our physical
fathers (physical genitors) who gave us physical life so we should be subject
to our heavenly Father (spiritual genitor) who has given us spiritual life. He
is the Father of our spirit, having imparted spiritual life to us in the act of
regeneration. Those believers who do not subject themselves to their Father
who gave them spiritual life will be subject to premature physical death.
Terminal discipline may be defined as God cutting off a believer’s life
prematurely because of his or her refusal to submit to His internal and
external discipline. Such discipline can happen when a believer is young or
old. It is terminal in that it cuts off his or her opportunity to inherit the
kingdom and thus to receive a full inheritance.
       The age of the believer is not what determines whether the death is
terminal discipline. The terminal status is determined by the believer’s stage
rather than age. The young believer who is killed by a drunk driver, in what
generally would be considered a premature death from the human
perspective, need not automatically be considered an example of terminal
discipline—if that young believer has been responsive to the Lord’s leading
up until that stage of his or her development. This type of premature death is
not due to the believer’s refusal to submit to the Lord’s will but due to the
drunk driver’s failure to submit to the Lordship of Christ. Our children need
to be taught the importance of submitting to the Lordship of Christ in view
of the Judgment Seat of Christ (JSC), regardless of their age, so that at
whatever stage of development the rapture or death finds them, they will be
prepared to stand before the JSC and be found faithful for their stage of
development.
Page 46                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

       Heb 12:15
       See to it that no one comes short of the grace of God; that no
       root of bitterness springing up causes trouble, and by it many
       be defiled.

        A number of negative words in vv. 15-16 show what can happen to
undisciplined believers. The warning is addressed to the readership and thus
to those who are genuine believers. After all, it is certainly possible for a
Christian to become bitter and defiled—to live, act, and think like a lost
person. In doing so, a believer may fail to experience God’s grace. This
failure to experience God’s grace (i.e., to come short of the grace of God)
does not refer to God’s saving grace (the soteriological dimension of His
grace) but to His enabling grace. Believers may fail to become what they
could have been. They may fail to live up to their potential of becoming
godly like their Father (Heb 12:10,14). This word for come short (hustereo)
is the same word as come short or fall short in Rom 3:23. Just as unbelievers
fall short of God’s glory, so believers can fall short of being all they should
have been through God’s grace.

       Heb 12:16
       That there be no immoral or godless person like Esau, who
       sold his own birthright for a single meal.

       As noted regarding Heb 12:5, there are two types of negative
responses to God’s discipline in that verse. On the one hand, there are
believers who faint under God’s discipline, and on the other hand, there are
believers who despise God’s discipline by thinking lightly of it. Which type
was Esau? He was the latter type: “Esau despised his birthright” (Gen
25:34). He sold his birthright. He sold his inheritance. Does this mean that
he sold his salvation, that he lost his salvation, or that he was no longer
Isaac’s child? No!
       Even Lenski, who does not believe in eternal security, acknowledges
the obvious fact that Esau did not sell his salvation to Jacob. That was not
what was at stake.22 For that matter, even Shank, in his argument against
eternal security, concedes:

              First, let us recognize that in the case of Esau, the
      ‘rejection’ had nothing to do with his salvation….
              ….God had rejected Esau as the heir to the Abrahamic
      Covenant and the progenitor of the Messianic line—a matter
      which has nothing to do with the question of his salvation.23
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 47
         What Esau sold was his birthright as the firstborn son, that is, his
inheritance. The firstborn received twice as much property as the other
children (Dt 21:16-17). The passage is not talking about salvation from hell.
It is talking about inheritance rights.24

       Heb 12:17
       For you know that even afterwards, when he desired to inherit
       the blessing, he was rejected, for he found no place for
       repentance, though he sought for it with tears.

        This verse is not saying that Esau lost his salvation from hell and was
unable to get it back again. This point is conceded by astute conditionalists
and confessed by securitists. Then what was it that Esau tearfully sought in
v. 17? In other words, to what does the word it refer in this verse? As
denoted in the following chart, there are two grammatical possibilities that
result in three hermeneutical possibilities. The word it could refer to either
the blessing or repentance.
                        Illustration 5. Esau Sought It



                                           IT




                    Repentance                           Blessing




            Esau's                    Isaac's




                                     Rewards
Page 48                               Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

       Esau’s Repentance (Heb 6:6)
        Whether Esau was an actual believer (although it seems that he
probably was), typologically at least, Esau must represent a genuine believer
since he was the genuine firstborn heir.25 Nonetheless, through his lack of
self-discipline, he lost his inheritance rights as the firstborn. The writer of
Hebrews warns that the same thing can happen to believers if they refuse to
endure God’s discipline. Many think that the it refers to Esau’s lack of
repentance. That is, they believe he did not repent and could not repent.
Indeed, an example of this very thing occurs in Heb 6:6—the Scripture
teaches that believers can reach the point where repentance is impossible. If
they reach the point of no return, then forfeiture of future rewards is assured.
So even if this were the case here in 12:17, then Marrowists would conclude
that this passage, like Heb 6:6, is also teaching that believers may forfeit the
reward that could have been theirs otherwise. If this is the case, then both
passages are talking about rewards. Accordingly, there is no need to
misconstrue the passage as denying eternal security, even if this improbable
interpretation of it as personal repentance is adopted in 12:17.

       Isaac’s Repentance (Lang, Hodges)
       Another popular option is to claim that Esau was seeking repentance
on the part of his father Isaac, but Esau could not get Isaac to change his
mind and give him the blessing instead. So what Esau was seeking was
Isaac’s repentance. Lang, Hodges, and Tanner take this position.26
Nevertheless, they astutely conclude that the issue at hand is rewards rather
than salvation. Both this interpretation and the previous one are well suited
to a misthological understanding of the passage.

       Blessing (Lenski, Robertson, Bruce)
       Notwithstanding the feasibility of these two splendid misthological
approaches, the superior interpretation pictured on the chart in Illustration 5
(with the darker arrows) is that Esau was after the blessing.* Lenski likewise

*
   The arrows are weighted. (1) The darkest arrows reference it as referring
directly to the blessing. This interpretation is most probable. The direct object of
it is the same as the direct object of his desire—the blessing. (2) The second
most probable interpretation is that it refers directly to Isaac’s repentance (i.e., to
a change of mind and corresponding change of action on Isaac’s part) regarding
the blessing. Thus, the blessing is the indirect object. Esau desired that Isaac
change his mind about it (the bestowment of the blessing) and give it to him
(Esau) instead of Jacob. Grammatically, these two interpretations are clearly
distinguishable. Pragmatically, they are one in the same. Tanner, for instance,
interprets Heb 12:17 misthologically (just as I do). (3) The lightest arrows
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 49
argues that it in Heb 12:17 grammatically must refer to the blessing. Greek
grammarian A. T. Robertson, in his Word Pictures, states that it refers to the
blessing. Bruce also concurs that this is the more probable interpretation.27
        The OT background concurs with the NT Greek grammatical
assessments. Esau’s actions in Gen 27:30ff make it clear that he had a
change of heart and action. Ironically, just as he originally had lost the
birthright because of a meal, he was now making a meal for his father
because he wanted the blessing. The Greek word thelo used in Heb 12:17a
means to desire or want. What Esau desired (NAS) and wanted (NKJ) in the
first half of verse 17 was the blessing. This blessing is the same thing he
sought in the second half of the verse. What he wanted in the first half is
what he sought in the second half. He wanted and sought the blessing—the
inheritance. But it was too late. Based on the grammar, the immediate
context, the contrasts (between sons and bastards), the broader context of the
book of Hebrews, the OT background, and the contemporary Roman culture,
the preferred interpretation and reasonable translation is: “For you know that
even afterwards, when he desired to inherit the blessing, he was rejected (for
he found no place for repentance), even though he sought for the blessing
with tears” (TM). 28

       Application
       Nevertheless, lest anyone hastily conclude that God’s discipline must
always be effective (and thereby ignore the scriptural evidence to the
contrary), this study on Esau will conclude with some practical applications
about the importance and limitations of discipline and inheritance rights.

       Importance of Discipline
       The writer of Hebrews draws his material concerning discipline from
the book of Proverbs. One of the most beloved proverbs is that of Prov 22:6,
which assures, “Train up a child in the way he should go, even when he is
old he will not depart from it.” As parents, we discipline our children with
high hopes and high expectations in light of this verse. And we should do so.
Does this mean that this proverb should be regarded as an ironclad
guarantee? No. Likewise, it would be wrong to apply this verse from

denote the least probable interpretation. Like the previous interpretation, this
option likewise makes the blessing the indirect object. Tanner and I are also in
agreement that Heb 6:6 affirms the potential impossibility for obtaining the
misthological blessing in some cases of apostasy because repentance may
become impossible. Understandably, some misthologists may wish to import
this understanding from Heb 6:6 into Heb 12:17. I do not, however.
Notwithstanding, misthologically, the three approaches practically are
equivalent in the latter passage.
Page 50                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
Hebrews to God and claim that God’s discipline will always ensure that His
children will never depart from the way they should go. A parallel certainly
exists between our paternal discipline and that of God’s discipline. Children
get their first impression as to what God is like from their paternal father.
The writer of Hebrews draws upon this observation by pointing out a
parallel between how earthly fathers’ discipline their children and how God
disciplines His children. In fact, the NT commands fathers to adhere to this
pattern (Eph 6:4).

       Limitations of Discipline
       Prodigal’s Father
       We must be realistic about the limitations of discipline. God is the
perfect parent. He disciplines His children perfectly. Yet even He has
children who fail to respond properly to His discipline. Recall Adam and
Eve. Does the failure of His children mean that He is a failure as a Father?
Hardly! Rather, there is a limit to how much even our heavenly Father will
discipline us. He will not take away our free will. We are responsible as His
children for our own actions and attitudes. He does not shoulder all the
responsibility Himself. He shares it with us. We are accountable. As a result,
we should be realistic and acknowledge that there are good parents who
have bad kids. For example, consider the father of the prodigal son. Was this
parabolic father a bad parent? No. Did his son depart from the way he should
go? Yes. Does this mean that Prov 22:6 is not true or that the prodigal son’s
father did not train his child properly since his child departed from the way
he should go? No. The prodigal’s father was not to blame for his wayward
son. In his study on biblical application, Kuhatschek laments:

              On more than one occasion I have heard Proverbs 22:6
       quoted as a promise about child rearing. Some claim this is a
       guarantee that if we properly raise our children, they will turn
       out well. Others go so far as to say that if a child becomes
       rebellious, it is the parent’s fault, not the child’s. Obviously
       this view is bound to produce false expectations in some and
       tremendous guilt in others.29

        Kuhatschek offers Adam and Eve as an illustration of the fact that the
rebellion of one’s children is not a demonstration that a parent is necessarily
to be blamed for the failure of his or her offspring. Adam could not blame
God, and we cannot blame our parents. We are responsible for our own
actions. Our parents do not irresistibly control our fate, nor does God
irresistibly control our actions. Kuhatschek is certainly correct but also
heart-wrenchingly inconsistent.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 51
        Just a few pages prior to this accurate acknowledgement of the
possibility of failure on the part of both our children and God’s children,
Kuhatschek shares the account of a friend, who after years of resisting the
gospel, finally came to embrace the gospel and experienced a radical
transformation into Christlikeness as a result. Tragically, however, four
years later, his friend’s faith started to waver until over a period of time he
had become apostate. Kuhatschek grieves over his friend’s departure from
the faith, considering it an indication of lack of regeneration.
        Kuhatschek’s Calvinist outlook is obvious: “Although I firmly
believe in eternal security, I also believe that those who are eternally secure
will persevere in the faith. As Paul says, we must not only believe, we must
continue in the faith” (italics his; bold mine).30 In Kuhatschek’s mind, those
who will persevere must persevere. His Calvinistic will is the same as the
Arminian must. Those who fail to persevere will go to hell. In his theology,
hell is for failures. According to Kuhatschek, his friend’s initial faith in
Christ was insufficient to save him from hell since he did not persevere.
        Regrettably, Kuhatschek fails to consider the doctrine of rewards as
an alternative possibility that could provide clarification as to what happens
to disobedient children. The outer darkness could be considered a
misthological result for rebellion on the part of those who have experienced
regeneration. God disciplines His children, but even His discipline does not
guarantee irresistible results. Kuhatschek should have realized this to be the
case since he correctly deduced that parental discipline does not always
produce the desired results. A proper understanding of the nature of
proverbial material regarding discipline (from which the author of Hebrews
draws) should lead to the conclusion that discipline (even God’s discipline)
is not always heeded. Kuhatschek practically stumbled over such an
admission with his using Adam and Eve as an illustration. Unfortunately, his
Calvinistic blindness resulted in his stumbling in the dark.

       Colloquial Proverbs
        A few biblical proverbs will be provided forthwith to prove the
limitations of proverbial statements.* First, however, perhaps several popular
colloquial proverbs will clarify the principle. When the author’s oldest son,
Jeremy, was leaving home for his first week of church camp, his parents
anticipated that he would get homesick. He, likewise, wanted to know if his
parents would miss him. As his parents, we proverbially assured him that
“absence makes the heart grow fonder.” We would certainly miss him. His
younger brother, on the other hand, chidingly was conveying a very different
attitude that could be expressed more appropriately by another proverb: “Out


*
    Also see “Proverbial Limitations” in The Outer Darkness.
Page 52                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
of sight, out of mind.” Both proverbs are true because they are not absolute.
Tension is permissible between the two summations.
       Another popular pair of contrasting proverbs illustrate the potential
for proverbial tension: “A bird in the hand is worth two in the bush” versus
“Nothing ventured, nothing gained.” In discussing several logical fallacies
associated with overgeneralization, Geisler pits the latter cliché against
another equally popular one: “Better safe than sorry.”31 Some clichés
caution you to not take chances; others urge you to take chances. Still, both
platitudes offer wise pieces of advice.
       Other popular contrasts would include: “Haste makes waste” versus
“A stitch in time saves nine.” The caution warns against acting swiftly; the
rejoinder prompts you to act quickly. The same is true of this duo: “Never
put off till tomorrow what you can do today” versus “Haste makes waste.”
Or take this one: “The more the merrier” versus “Two’s company, three’s a
crowd.” There are times when one is true; there are times when the other is
true. Or consider this contrasting attitude toward learning: “You can't teach
old dogs new tricks” versus “It’s never too late to learn.”

       Biblical Proverbs

       Mustard Seed
       These colloquial examples of proverbial statements are amusing, but
failing to recognize something as a proverbial statement or failing to
understand the nature of a proverbial statement is not always a source of
amusement. There are those who claim that since the mustard seed is not the
smallest seed on the planet, the Bible is not infallible and that Jesus is wrong
in calling it the smallest seed (Mt 13:32). Such critics are mistaken. Jesus
speaks accurately because He is speaking proverbially about the relative and
comparative sizes of garden herbs. He is citing a Jewish proverb, and it is
accurate. His statement was not intended to be taken as a superlative or
absolute statement. The intent of the speaker must be kept in mind.

        Fool
        Likewise, consider these contrasting Biblical proverbs: “Do not
answer a fool according to his folly...Answer a fool according to his folly”
(Prov 26:3-4). Is this a contradiction? No. This contrast is proverbial not
irrational. Sometimes one course of action is best; sometimes the other is
better. These proverbs are guidelines, not laws. They are pragmatic
observations and principles about the way life normally works. As principles
they reflect general truth rather than absolute truth. Moral absolutes exits,
but not everything is morally absolute. These proverbs are not ironclad
contracts. They are not unconditional guarantees or rules. For this reason, it
is often said that proverbs are principles not promises. Yet this rejoinder
possibly goes too far the opposite direction. It would probably be better to
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                        Page 53
say that we have principles in tension. Forlines calls “this principle of
interpretation: the principle of tension and counterbalance” (emphasis his).32

             One verse tells you not to answer a fool. The other verse
       tells you to answer a fool. Obviously you cannot do both of
       these in every situation….What you have to do is consider
       what the greatest risk is. If the greatest risk is that you will be
       like him, you do not answer him. If the greatest risk is that he
       will be wise in his own eyes, you do answer him. It will not
       always be easy to decide which to do, but you must do one of
       them. It is a serious mistake to choose one of these and adopt it
       as your approach to every situation.
             ….There are some truths that cannot be set forth in one
       principle alone, but must be set forth in two or more principles
       which counterbalance each other…This principle of
       interpretation guides us in areas where we are dealing with
       what we might call general truth instead of absolute
       truth….This principle is similar to the principle, ‘There are
       many facets of truth.’…
             …there are absolute truths such as the moral teachings of
       the Ten Commandments. These we must obey. But there are
       some areas of life for which we have general principles rather
       than absolute truths to guide us. (Emphasis his.) 33

        Training
        Giving due recognition to principles in tension or tension and
counterbalance, as parents we should realize that even if we raise our
children correctly, they could still turn out bad. We must be ever mindful of
another proverbial statement made by Paul: “Bad company corrupts good
morals” (1Cor 15:33). Bad influences can corrupt good children. Parents
need to be diligent and ever aware of this real danger. Certainly, part of our
responsibility as parents is to point out the realities of these dangers to our
children and teach them that despite our godly discipline they put themselves
in real danger of departing from the way they should go if they hang out
with the wrong crowd.
        On more than one occasion, my wife and I have had our own children
want to do certain things with a group of friends, and we have not permitted
them. Our children assured us that as our children they had been reared
properly and could be trusted. My wife wisely has responded that it is not
that we do not trust them (our children) but that we do not necessarily trust
their friends in certain circumstances. So we were unwilling to allow them to
participate in some group activity. Their godly training did not make them
immune to ungodly corruption.
Page 54                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
        Likewise, we must point our children to these passages in Hebrews
and show them that they will fail to inherit these blessings if they do not
respond properly to God’s discipline. Discipline is vital to their well-being.
Nevertheless, godly discipline does not necessarily or automatically assure
godly performance.
        Even so, children eventually reach the point where they are the ones
responsible for their behavior, not the parent. The parent is no longer the one
responsible. God is not at fault for our faults. So what is to be concluded
about the parents of prodigal children?
        If you are the parent of a prodigal child and if you have done your
part in raising him or her properly, then recognize two things: (1) There is
still hope, and (2) even God Himself has prodigal children. Do not beat
yourself to death with a club of guilt if you have done everything possible to
raise your children in a godly fashion. Still, at the same time, do everything
you can to make sure you are raising them in a godly fashion with positive
expectations.

        Proverbial Expectations
        Although proverbs are not absolute promises, they are general
maximums which give expected rather than guaranteed results. They are not
absolute rules, but they are rules of thumb. For this reason, when we raise
our children according to Prov 22:6, we are justified in having high
expectations concerning their behavior. The writer of Hebrews, Paul, and
John also tend to have optimistic outlooks concerning their spiritual children
(1Cor 1:8; Gal 5:10; Phil 1:6; 2Thess 3:4; Heb 6:9; 10:39; 1Jn 2:27—MT).34
These proverbial expectations are not prophetic declarations. These biblical
writers expected their readers to respond positively to their warnings.
However, at the same time, these writers were realists and acknowledged
that failure was a real possibility. Pastoral and parental optimism must not be
devoid of realism. A balanced proverbial tension gives due consideration to
both the positive and negative dimensions. Optimistic expectancy should not
be confused with infallible certainty.

       Repent Now
       The parable of the prodigal son is a balance of hope and warning.
Repentance was possible. Show your children the importance of heavenly
rewards. The writer of Hebrews does so in addressing these potential
prodigals. Give them the warning that if they wait until they stand before the
Bema, then they (like Esau) will find that their repentance is too late to
obtain the heavenly inheritance. When the prodigal son came home and his
older brother was standing outside pouting, what words of consolation did
his father give the older brother? Did the father tell his older son, “All that I
have will be equally divided between you and your repentant younger
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                      Page 55
brother”? No. His younger brother’s inheritance was gone—forever. His
father told the older son, “All that I have is yours” (Lk 15:31, NKJ).
        Jesus already had made this point clear earlier in the parable when He
pointed out that the father “divided his wealth between them” (Lk 15:12). As
Kendall notes, “he divided his property between his two sons so that the
elder son received his share at the same time.”35 Everything that did not pass
into the possession of the prodigal son passed into the possession of the
faithful son as the rightful heir before the prodigal son went into the far
country.
        So when the faithful son who stayed home became upset when his
father threw a party for his repentant younger brother, the father reminds his
faithful son who stayed home and worked that he—the faithful son (and he
alone)—is now the heir to everything. A realized heirship in which
possession of the father’s estate already had passed to the older brother as
co-owner with his father is thus envisioned parabolically.
        The fattened calf slain for the younger brother and the ring and robe
given to the younger brother already had been given to the older brother.
These items are not exceptions to the father’s statements in Lk 15:12,31. The
fatten calf, robe, and ring belonged jointly to the father and older brother.
The older son had inherited his father’s property, all of it, including the
rulership of the family. As the head of the family, the older son would be
expected to treat his younger brother as one of the family. The younger son
would not have to live on the streets. So in giving these items to the younger
son, the father was not revoking the fact that these items belonged to the
older son. The older son would be expected to do the same. As a result of the
belated repentance on the part of the younger son, this prodigal son was to
be provided for as part of the family and restored to fellowship with his
father and brother. Yet the unfaithful son had forfeited his stewardship over
the part of the inheritance that otherwise would have been his.
        If one wishes to press the question as to what would have happened to
the prodigal had he remained in the far country, Marrowists simply may
point out that his eschatological end would have been that of the far
country—the (remotest regions of) the outer darkness. His familial death
would have turned out to be a misthological death (Lk 15:24). He would
have lost his soul in the far country.* In any event, since the prodigal son
came back, he would at least be able to live in the house (possibly picturing
the Heavenly City) with his father and brother—under their authority.
        Had the prodigal son repented earlier he would not have lost all his
inheritance. He could have come home before he squandered it all. Instead,
the prodigal son, like Esau, had lost his inheritance. And his repentance, like

*
 The loss of his soul-life would have been a misthological tragedy, to be sure,
but it would have nothing to do with soteriology.
Page 56                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
that of Esau, was too late to change the fact that his inheritance was gone.
Similarly, Esau repented of his decision to kill Jacob and was blessed. Esau
had 400 men with him and plenty of material possessions (Gen 33:9). The
writer of Hebrews himself acknowledges that Esau did, in fact, receive a
blessing (Heb 11:20). True, it was a lesser blessing. Nonetheless, his
repentance concerning the importance of the greater blessing did at least win
him a lesser blessing.
        We must urge our prodigals who have fallen from grace to repent
now—the sooner the better. They must respond to God’s discipline while
there is still time. A belated repentance is certainly better than no
repentance. Even a belated repentance might be enough to save a brother
from the outer darkness, or at least from the remotest parts of it. The twilight
zone is better than the dark zone. More optimistically, perhaps they still
might achieve the lowest positions of privilege in the Heavenly City, even if
their repentance happens at the end of their lives. Certainly, it is better to be
last at the Father’s table than to be excluded altogether from that table and
be in the outer darkness. Who wants to spend eternity in the dog house?
        Kendall does well to interpret the prodigal son as a picture of a
backslidden Christian, and yet he rightly notes that the picture of the older
son is aimed at the Pharisees. Nevertheless, Kendall correctly interprets the
older brother as dealing with older Christians and rewards in heaven.36 The
crucial distinction between interpretation and application is that a text
(which may have a single interpretation) may have multiple applications.
        In terms of interpretation, both sons represent believers, but the
application is intended for both believers and unbelievers. Jesus is assuring
unbelieving, self-righteous, ethically good Pharisees that being morally good
in the kingdom does in fact pay off. They need not let their ethical goodness
prevent them from becoming believers and from having harmony with God
since they will inherit the kingdom as a result of doing so. Jesus uses the
picture of a believer with a pharisaical attitude to show the Pharisees that
this attitude is unwarranted in light of the kingdom truth concerning the
misthological inheritance. As for the prodigal son, many unbelievers feel
like that prodigal believer. Even unbelievers may be invited to follow his
example in coming home with him to meet his father.

       Calvinistic Objections
        The fallacy of taking proverbial statements as absolute promises
rather than treating them as positive expectations has been demonstrated.
The principle passages of Scripture that sometimes suffer the same abuse
would be the following: 1Cor 1:8; Gal 5:10; Phil 1:6; 2Thess 3:4; Heb 6:9.37
These passages of Scripture were written by the same men who teach that
believers can fall from grace and fall short of grace. Consequently, it should
be evident that these verses are not intended to be taken as promises of
irresistible grace in the lives of believers.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 57

       Heb 6:9
       But, beloved, we are convinced of better things concerning you
       [humon], and things that accompany salvation, though we are
       speaking in this way.

        This confident expectation is addressed to the readership with the
word you. Two verses later, the writer proceeds to address this same group
of people but counterbalances his optimistic conviction with realistic
concern: “And we desire that each one of you [humon], show the same
diligence to the full assurance of hope until the end” (Heb 6:11). His
conviction is the expression of a desire rather than the provision of an
unconditional guarantee. This is the same group of people whom he
repetitively warns with the word you (Heb 3:12-13,15; 4:1,7; 12:3,8,25;
13:17).
       12
          Take care, brethren, lest there should be in any one of you
       [humon] an evil, unbelieving heart, in falling away from the
       living God. 13 But encourage one another day after day, as long
       as it is still called “Today,” lest any one of you [humon] be
       hardened by the deceitfulness of sin….15 while it is said,
       “Today if you hear His voice, Do not harden your [humon]
       hearts, as when they provoked Me.” (Heb 3:12-13,15)

       The warnings apply to the same group of people—the beloved
brethren. Failure is clearly possible. Considering the frequency and severity
of his epistolary warnings, it is not surprising that this biblical writer takes
this opportunity to point out to his readers that he is not a pessimist. He does
not expect the worst of them. His warnings are to be taken as expressions of
realism rather than pessimism.
       Apostasy is indeed possible, though, for those who have experienced
regeneration if they do not press on to maturity. Being an optimist does not
mean that the author of Hebrews is making an unqualified promise that his
readers will avoid the negative rewards or obtain the positive rewards. He
expects the best but acknowledges the realistic possibility of the worst.
Optimistic realism should not be considered an absolute assurance anymore
than a proverb should be considered a promise. There are exceptions to both.
Related texts show concord.

       Gal 5:10 and 2Thess 3:4
       I have confidence in you in the Lord, that you will adopt no
       other view; but the one who is disturbing you shall bear his
       judgment, whoever he is. (Gal 5:10)
Page 58                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
       And we have confidence in the Lord concerning you, that you
       are doing and will continue to do what we command.
       (2Thess 2:4)

         As is readily apparent, Paul is expressing the same thought in both of
these verses—a dual confidence in both the Lord and his readers.38 The same
Greek word is used for confidence (peitho) in both these verses (as well as in
Heb 6:9 and Phil 1:6), concerning which Hart surmises that it is “often used
to express a pastoral heart in thinking the best of a congregation or
individual without indicating the absolute certainty of the future.”39
         Paul’s confidence in the church of Thessalonica is not surprising. In
his initial letter he spoke of his fatherly and motherly concern for them as his
own children (1Thess 2:7,11) and of their positive response in this same
chapter and the previous one. Yet, in the very next chapter, he acknowledges
that the reason he sent Timothy back to them was out of concern that they
might abandon the faith under persecution: “For this reason, when I could
endure it no longer, I also sent to find out about your faith, for fear that the
tempter might have tempted you, and our labor should be in vain”
(1Thess 3:5). Paul’s confidence that they would continue in his teaching was
not absolute. On the contrary, it was conditioned on their initial response and
continual obedience: “For now we really live, if you stand firm in the Lord”
(1Thess 3:8).
         In his second letter, Paul shares that he prays for these believers
constantly that God may count them worthy of their misthological calling
(2Thess 1:11). He prays, rather than promises, that they will be counted
worthy of this reward. In the next chapter, Paul expresses concern that even
these believers could be dissuaded by the false teaching which they
subsequently had encountered (2Thess 2:2-3). Apostasy was a realistic
possibility, so much so that his reason for writing this second letter was to
warn them concerning this false teaching in order to prevent their defection.
         Because of their positive response to his teachings in the past, Paul
has sound reason to expect this church at large to respond positively on this
occasion as well. Nevertheless, in the very chapter in which he expresses
this confidence concerning their reaction to his exhortation, he instructs
them on how to deal with genuine Christian brethren in the congregation
who fail to follow his instructions: “Now we command you, brethren, in the
name of our Lord Jesus Christ, that you keep aloof from every brother who
leads an unruly life and not according to the tradition which you received
from us” (2Thess 3:6). “And if anyone does not obey our instruction in this
letter, take special note of that man and do not associate with him, so that he
may be put to shame. And yet do not regard him as an enemy, but admonish
him as a brother” (2Thess 2:14-15). Those believers who refuse to follow
Paul’s command are not to be considered lost; they still are to be considered
and treated as brothers in Christ. What a stark contrast is found in
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                        Page 59
Kuhatschek’s Calvinistic reaction in which he refuses to consider his brother
in Christ as saved because of his brother’s subsequent failure. Paul’s
soteriological outlook is completely different from that of Kuhatschek. But
then again, Paul was not a Calvinist. Pauline soteriology accepts the reality
of genuine apostasy.
        The genuineness of the Galatian apostasy has been demonstrated
already. Consequently, Paul’s confidence in Gal 5:10 concerning the church
at Galatia does not show that apostasy is impossible. Without a doubt, they
had already committed apostasy. They had fallen from grace into legalism.
Paul’s confidence in this case is that he will be able to win them back from
legalistic apostasy. These believers had become apostate. Paul is in high
spirits that, in pointing out the error of both their behavior and theology, he
would be able to succeed in bringing them back to orthodoxy.
        Yet once again, his positive expectation was not free of all doubt or
concern, as is clear in his exasperation: “I could wish to be present with you
now and to change my tone, for I am perplexed about you” (Gal 4:20). This
perplexity was more than a wrinkled brow. Louw-Noda defines the verb for
being perplexed (aporeo) as “to be in perplexity, with the implication of
serious anxiety—‘to be at a loss, to be uncertain, to be anxious, to be in
doubt, consternation.’” The NKJ translates it as: “I have doubts about you.”*
Or as Paul expressed his lack of confidence earlier in this chapter: “I fear for
you, that perhaps I have labored over you in vain” (Gal 4:11).40
        One must avoid overreacting to Paul’s concern by claiming that this
means that he has soteriological doubts about their salvation. Far from it, he
affirms their unconditional security despite their apostasy.† He is simply a
realist in that he realizes that permanent misthological failure is possible.
Thus, one must not overestimate Paul’s confidence so as to assume that he

*
  Contrary to some of my fellow FG soteriologists, I find irrefutable evidence in
the NT record that doubt and faith may coexist concerning the same proposition.
For example, Paul says that he fears (phobeo) that his labor for these believers
may be in vain (Gal 4:11). This phobia denotes doubt. He has his doubts about
their positive response to his ministry. Contrastively, yet simultaneously, he
affirms that he is persuaded (peitho) that they will respond positively to his
warning (Gal 5:10). As a parent of two godly FG sons, I am persuaded that they
will obey the Lord and remain true to the message of FG. Yet, like Paul, I am
fearful that their constant exposure to legalism at the church where we attend
may lead them astray. Simultaneously, I am persuaded that through my writing
such books as this one, they will not subcome to legalism, yet I am mindful that
they might. For this reason, with confidence in the Lord that victory will be
achieved, I bow my knees before the Father and take up my pen to refute
legalistic foes. I can empathize with Paul’s mindset and, like him,
enthusiastically affirm that by the grace of God I will not lose this battle.
†
  See Unconditional Security in Galatians, 30.
Page 60                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
believes that irremediable apostasy is impossible. Complete and final
apostasy is possible—hence Paul’s concern. Yet, in this particular instance,
he does not feel that apostasy would be irrevocable—thus his confidence.
When taken in context, these Galatian passages offer no confirmation for
either Calvinistic doubt or Calvinistic confidence. These passages do not
show soteriological doubt of salvation or soteriological confidence of
inevitable perseverance. On the contrary, these texts simultaneously
illustrate Pauline doubt and Pauline confidence: Realistic parental
assessment of the possibility of misthological failure (Gal 4:19-20) is
coupled with relative confidence of misthological success (Gal 5:10). The
limitation of this concern to the misthological sphere refutes Arminianism;
the existence of this concern nullifies Calvinism. Marrowism alone is in a
position to provide an exposition of Galatians.

      Phil 1:5-7
       Hart has written a comprehensive two-part article setting forth the
two primary interpretations of this Philippian passage. He refutes the
Calvinistic interpretation in favor of one that takes the occasion for the
writing of the letter as the decisive factor in understanding the text. The

      The epistolary prologue introduces the theme of the letter and is
complemented by the epilogue of the epistle. The thematic unity of the
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                     Page 61
prologue and epilogue of this epistle to the Philippians is confirmed by
structural, verbal, and conceptual analysis. The core of its introduction is
found in Phil 1:5-7. Within its immediate context, participation in the gospel
(v. 5) is evidently synonymous with being co-participants in grace in the
defense and confirmation of the gospel (v. 7), meaning the Philippians have
been participants with Paul in the gospel proclamation from the first day
(1:5)—denoted as what God began (1:6)—also called the first preaching of
the gospel (4:15).
       Their financial participation in the gospel is referred to in the
prologue and epilogue and is a central theme for the epistle, wherein Paul
expresses gratitude for their ministering to his monetary needs in expressing
their faith in terms of practical economic sacrifice and service through the
gift they sent through Epaphroditus (Phil 2:17; 25-30; 4:18). Aside from
these references, the most reasonable cross-reference to Phil 1:6 is to be
found in the Corinthian epistle (2Cor 8-9), which is the most extended
discussion on giving in the NT. A highly condensed translation of this
passage in context has been provided below.

      2Cor 8-9
      8:1
          The grace of God which has been given in the churches of
      Macedonia…3 [who] gave of their own accord…4 for the favor
      of participation in the support of the saints…6 Consequently
      we urged Titus that as he had previously made a beginning, so
      he would also perfect in you this gracious work as well…
      10
          [you] were the first to begin a year ago…11But now perfect
      doing it also; that…there may be also the perfection of it by

      brother…because of his great confidence in you….9:7for God
      loves a cheerful giver. 8 And God is able to make all grace
      abound to you, that…you may have an abundance for every
      good work…10[God] will supply and multiply your seed for
      sowing and increase the harvest of your righteousness; 11 you
      will be enriched in everything… 13 they will glorify God for
      your obedience to your confession of the gospel of Christ, and
      for the liberality of your participation to them and to
      all…14 because of the surpassing grace of God in you.

       Comparing these passages, one may appropriately surmise that these
texts describe confidence concerning what was happening in you (i.e., the
church) from the beginning in the perfection and participation of the good
work of supporting the gospel through God’s enabling grace expressed in
giving a financial gift. Contextually and cross-textually, this conclusion is
necessary.
Page 62                              Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
      Good Work
      As Hart documents, good work “always has humans as the primary
agent of the action and never God.”41 Hart goes on to add in a note to this
statement that of the 14 occurrences of good work (i.e., agathos ergon) in
the NT:

                It never refers to “salvation.” [Acts 9:36; Rom 2:7;
         13:3; 2 Cor 9:8; Eph 2:10; Phil 1:6; Col 1:10; 2 Thess 2:17; 1
         Tim 2:10; 5:10; 2 Tim 2:21; 3:17; Titus 1:16; 3:1] In each NT
         use the focus is on believers meeting the practical, earthly
         needs of others. Occasionally the phrase contains obvious
         overtones of financial or material giving (Acts 9:36; 2 Cor 9:8;
         and perhaps 1 Tim 5:10)….
                Even synonymous constructions cannot produce an
         example of the phrase “good work” as describing God’s
         redemptive plan or employing God as the subject of the
         work.42

        Good work (kalos ergon) is one such synonymous construction,
which also supports Hart’s contention. Paul again enjoins, “And let our
people also learn to maintain good works, to meet urgent needs, that they
may not be unfruitful” (Tit 3:14; NKJ). Humans are the primary agents
performing the good works, and these works are performed in order to meet
the financial needs of other believers. Another synonymous parallel that Hart
did not mention is the combination of good (agathos) with the verbal form
of work (ergazomai). There are three such examples (Rom 2:10; Gal 6:10;
Eph 4:28), the latter of which expresses the same perspective: “Let him who
stole steal no longer, but rather let him labor, working with his hands what is
good, that he may have something to give him who has need” (Eph 4:28;
NKJ). Again, believers are being called upon to perform good works so that
they may assist others. The Galatian citation may be understood likewise:
“Let us work good to all men, and especially to those who are of the
household of the faith” (Gal 6:10; TM). The reason for pointing out the
omission of this construction in support of Hart’s position is because, by
comparing the two lists, it may be seen that two synonymous verses occur
within the same passage: Rom 2:7,10. This is a misthological passage that
teaches that God will reward eternal life to those who do good works—but
only believers can qualify.* By enjoining the two lists, we can advance
beyond Hart’s introduction. The translation below fills in the Greek elliptical
material. This cross-reference suggests the type of perfection Paul has in
mind as a result of the good works.

*
    For example, see “The Throne is not Free” in The Outer Darkness.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 63
       Perfection
       6
         [God] will reward every man according to his works. 7 He
       will reward eternal life to those who seek for glory and honor
       and immortality by persevering in good work; 8 but He will
       reward wrath and indignation to those who are selfishly
       ambitious and do not obey the truth, but obey unrighteousness.

       Greek, 10 but glory and honor and peace will be rewarded to
       everyone who works what is good, to the Jew first and also to
       the Greek. (Rom 2:7-10; TM)

        Of course, this passage does not affirm the possibility of
soteriological salvation by good works. In fact, one of the 16 passages that
Hart lists above demonstrates in its context that soteriological salvation is
not by good works (Eph 2:8-10). Concerning v. 10 of that passage, the
statement that believers should walk in good works does not prove that they
will do so.* Thus, this passage in Eph 2:10 refutes, rather than supports, the
Calvinistic and monergistic understanding of Phil 1:6. Common sense
proves that Calvinists engage in a considerable error in trying to use Phil 1:6
to prove the perseverance of the saints. How could it? How could Paul utter
absolute, unconditional certainty about their perseverance and yet express
doubt concerning his own (1Cor 9:27; cp. Phil 3:12-14)?
        As shown above in Rom 2:7, eternal life in the misthological sense is
granted on the basis of perseverance in good works. God actually will
reward eternal life to those who seek for it according to the principles of His
word. Of course, only believers have the potential to attain life at this level,
having already experienced life at the soteriological level and thus having
this life as the basis from which to operate. Even so, rewards are not
unconditionally guaranteed, but they are the positive expectation in the case
of qualified contestants who are working to earn them. This is the
confidence seen in this Philippian passage.
        In Phil 1:5 their participation in spreading the gospel in anticipation
of eschatological perfection (1:6) is noted, and in Phil 3:10 their
participation in suffering in hopes of eschatological perfection (3:12) is
affirmed. The misthological perfection for which Paul strives in Phil 3:12 is
the out-resurrection.† It would seem probable that Paul is also introducing
this theme in the introduction of Phil 1:6, anticipating the misthological
perfection to be discussed within the epistle. Their financial sacrifice and

suffering will consummate in the out-resurrection at the Bema in the day of

epilogue where Paul states that what he is really after is fruitfulness in terms
of profit in their misthological account (Phil 4:17). Payday will be that
day—the day of Christ Jesus. Thus, the perfection of the prologue (Phil 1:6)
may be found in the misthological profitability of the epilogue (Phil 4:17), as
well as within the epistle in the misthological perfection of Phil 3:12.
       Misthological completeness will be experienced at that time by those
believers who persist in their present course of faithfulness. Or as Paul
exhorted in the first Corinthian epistle concerning the financial support of
others: “Therefore, my beloved brethren, be steadfast, immovable, always
abounding in the work of the Lord, knowing that your toil is not in vain in
the Lord. Now concerning the collection for the saints, as I directed the
churches of Galatia, so do you also” (1Cor 15:58-16:1). In the context of this
passage, he is discussing inheriting the kingdom eschatologically
(1Cor 15:50). The reason their toil is not in vain is because it will result in
their qualifying for the misthological inheritance.

       Phil 2:13
       When Paul returns to this topic of God working in the Philippian
believers in Phil 2:13, he is not confirming irresistible grace but enabling
grace. This verse offers no support for the Calvinistic contention otherwise.
       12
          So then, my beloved, just as you have always obeyed, not as
       in my presence only, but now much more in my absence, work

       Christ I may have cause to glory because I did not run in vain
       nor toil in vain. (Phil 2:12-16)

       Energize
       Verse 12 presents a misthological salvation obtained by works.* No
believer has any need to fear hell, but unfaithful believers have good reason
to fear the Bema. Verse 13 represents God energizing believers, not

Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 65
irresistibly manipulating them. In refuting the Calvinistic notion of
irresistible grace concerning this passage, Forster argues “both God and
Satan are energizing, and Christians must turn on to the right energy….God
energizes in us, but we ourselves must plug in to the energy in order to work
out our own salvaging process.”43 The lexical evidence to which he appeals
for support is energeo which means to energize with the primary ideas of
stimulation, impression, and influence.* This same word is used in Eph 2:2
to speak of the devil working in the lives of unbelievers (cp. 2Thess 2:7).
This influence does not enable the lost to excuse themselves by saying, “The
devil made me do it.” Nor can Calvinists, in an attempt to maintain their
doctrine of perseverance, claim that God irresistibly causes believers to live
in a holy manner. Forster cites Lk 7:30 in demonstrating that the will of God
can be rejected. Of course, the present context of Phil 2:12 is sufficient to
demonstrate this point since it indicates that the outworking of God’s will in
the life of the believer is not automatic, inevitable, or guaranteed.
        This contingent understanding of the passage also counters the
passive nikologist who believes that we are the passive recipients of God’s
victory. The same word for energizing is used in Col 1:29, and it certainly
does not convey the idea that God does all the work for us or apart from any
effort on our part: “And for this purpose also I work hard, agonizingly
striving [agonizomai] according to His energizing, which mightily energizes
me. For I want you to know how great a struggle [agon] I have on your
behalf” (Col 1:29-2:1; TM). So here also, “For God is the one who is
internally energizing you both to desire and to be energized according to His
good pleasure” (Phil 2:13; TM).

       Desire
       In this verse God is not sovereignly manipulating believers by means
of His sovereign will. The desire (thelo) in question is that of the believer—
energized by God. Unlike in Rom 7:18-20, where the believer is only
experiencing the godly desire (thelo) without the corresponding ability,
these believers are expected to experience the enabling power of the Spirit
and please God since they are not only being energized with godly desires
but also with matching spiritual energy/ability. Because God provides both
the desire and ability to work out their salvation, they are expected to avail
