        Do your Best
        Although the passive nikologist may respond that the verse cannot be
referring to strenuous effort since the same verb for striving [agonizomai] is
used in Lk 13:24 (in reference to entering the kingdom) and in Col 4:12 (in
reference to prayer), the reasonableness of such an objection arising from the
first text is nullified by a contemplation of preconditions. Some people have
to do a lot of agonizing work before they finally determine that entrance into
heaven is not conditioned on work. As to the second text, using it as a
passive proof text fails to do justice to the fact that prayer can indeed be
agonizing (remember Jesus’ agonizing prayer in the Garden of Gethsemane):
“And being in agony He was praying very fervently; and His sweat became
like drops of blood, falling down upon the ground” (Lk 22:44). This word is
used in conjunction with laboring (1Tim 4:10). Most importantly, Paul again
uses this same dual verbal nominal combination of agonizing struggle to
describe fighting the good fight: “Fight the good fight” (1Tim 6:12); “I have
fought the good fight” (2Tim 4:7); “I fight…a great fight’’ (Col 1:29-3:1).
This same noun for fight [agon] can also be translated as race (Heb 12:1). In
any event, Paul uses the same verb to describe the participation in Olympic
games (1Cor 9:25). FB defines it as meaning to do one’s very best in
Col 4:12. BDAG likewise notes concerning askeo in Acts 24:16 that Paul
says, “I do my best.”
        UBS defines spoudazo as conveying the same meaning: “Do one’s
best, spare no effort, work hard.” And this designation for hard work is used
in a variety of situations. For example, we are exhorted to do our best to
preserve unity (Eph 4:3). Some translations, such as the ESV and RSV,
translate the following five verses in that manner: do your best to be an
approved workman in interpreting the word (2Tim 2:15); do your best to
come as soon as possible (2Tim 4:9,21; Tit 3:12-13); do your best to achieve
rewards (Heb 4:11; 2Pet 1:10; 3:14). Of these latter three passages,
concerning doing your best to achieve the specified reward, it is ironic that
passive nikologists, who anathematize doing your best and who perceive the
rest of Heb 4:11 as being a passive temporal victory, fail to note that the rest
in that very verse is achieved by doing your best. As a matter of fact, the
CJB translates it as: “Let us do our best to enter that rest.”
        To be sure, once we have entered that rest, we will no longer have to
work to earn or keep it: “For the one who has entered His rest has himself
also rested from his works, as God did from His” (Heb 4:10). But that is
because the rest is a misthological blessing: “Blessed are the dead who die in
the Lord from now on!’ ‘Yes,’” says the Spirit, ‘that they may rest from
their labors, for their works follow with them’” (Rev 14:13; TM).
Constable is correct to reject the present-rest view in Hebrews:
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                        Page 67


               If rest equals enjoying our spiritual blessings now, the
       writer should have warned his readers about losing their rest if
       they departed from God (3:12). The writer himself could have
       done this. Instead he warned them about failing to enter into
       their rest….It is an eschatological rest, not a present rest. We
       enter into our rest after we cease from our labors in this life.44
       (Emphasis his.)

        Tanner also supplies multiple reasons for concluding that this resting
place* is a misthological rest of sharing in Messianic rulership.45 If we want
this futuristic rest, we are going to have to work hard for it. As L-N (68.63)
points out, the nominal form (spoude) carries the same meaning as the verbal
form (spoudazo). Peter uses the nominal form in 2Pet 1:5 to introduce his
list of Christian qualities necessary for fruitfulness. The expression there is
used idiomatically and means: “Do your very best to add goodness to your
faith” (L-N 68.64). Peter then closes this list with spoudazo: “Do your best
to make your calling and election secure [bebaios]” (2Pet 1:10; TM).† The
thought in the passage is not to make sure that you are saved; rather, it is to
guarantee the misthological realization of your election.‡
        Concerning bebaios, VGNT states: “Deissmann (BS, p. 104 ff.) has
shown very fully how much force the technical use of this word and its
cognates to denote legally guaranteed security adds to their occurrence in the
NT.” The verbal and nominal forms are used jointly to mean: “I will further
guarantee the property always against all claims with every guarantee.”
Concerning the verbal cognate (bebaioo) in 2Cor 1:21-22, VGNT also
relates that Paul uses it to denote “the guaranteeing the delivery of
something of which the earnest has already been paid.” Other examples cited
by VGNT concerning bebaioo include: “I shall give you a guarantee” and
“will guarantee the sale with every guarantee.” The feminine form
(bebaiosis) is likewise used “in Heb 6:16 ‘for a legal guarantee.’”
        Peter, therefore, is enjoining us to do our best to guarantee our calling
and election, not to guarantee that it is genuine but that it is secure. The NIV
picks up on this thought in Heb 6:19 were bebaios is translated accordingly,
“We have this hope as an anchor for the soul, firm and secure.” An anchor is
useful to secure a ship, but only if the anchor has a rope tied to it. In an
episode of Gilligan’s Island, it was discovered that Gilligan had failed to tie

*
  See discussions of misthopology in The Outer Darkness.

‡

Page 68                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
a rope to the anchor, which led to their becoming marooned on a deserted
island. Christians need to secure their calling and election by anchoring it
with a life of good works that meet the misthological requirement to inherit
the kingdom. The misthological promise will do no good if it is not secured
with a life of good works. So Peter uses both forms of doing your best
(spoude, spoudazo) as bookends for his list of Christian virtues necessary to
be a fruitful Christian and secure our calling (2Pet 1:5-8). Yet Paul provides
a similar list and calls these similar qualities the fruit of the Spirit necessary
to inherit the kingdom (Gal 5:22-23). So which is it? Are these qualities that
are necessary to secure our calling and inherit the kingdom the fruit of the
Spirit (so Paul) or are they the result of human effort (so Peter)? Which
biblical writer are we to believe? Both, of course.
        Passive nikologists love to talk about the abiding life of the vine in
the branches and picture the branch passively allowing the vine to produce
the Johannine fruit (Jn 15:4-5). The secret, then, is supposed to be to abide in
the vine and let the vine do all the work. What escapes their notice is that
this Petrine fruit of the Spirit is not attained by passively abiding but by
actively working. Peter introduces his list with the noun spoude, exhorting
his Christian readers to do their best in actively bearing this fruit (2Pet 1:5).
Then he seals this list with the verbal from spoudazo in v. 10, where he says
that they should work hard (i.e., do their best) at bearing this fruit so that
they can achieve a rich entrance into the kingdom in v. 11. If you want the
fruit of the Spirit, then you better be prepared to work for it. The list of
Greek words demanding effort on the part of Christians goes on and on
within the semantic ranges of spoude and spoudazo (see L-N). But the fact
that the fruit of the Spirit involves cooperative effort is self apparent since
the reward of inheriting the kingdom is based on our having the fruit of the
Spirit. This fruit must be considered our work since we are rewarded for
bearing it.
        This deductive conclusion can also be proven inductively from the
context of the Johannine fruit as well. The sequence in Jn 15:4-5 is “abide in
Me” and bear fruit as a result. However, just a few verses later we are told:
“If you keep My commandments, you will abide in My love” (Jn 15:10).
Keeping Jesus’ commandments is a means of abiding in His love and thus of
experientially remaining in Him. His love is a metonymy for Him. Obeying
Him is the means of abiding in Him. Keeping the Lord’s commandments
takes work. So abiding in Him takes work. To be sure, this work should be
understood as a concurrent requirement rather than a precondition, since we
can do nothing apart from the Lord (v. 5). Abiding in Him is synchronic,
synergistic work.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                        Page 69
        Gillham’s first book on the victorious life was considerably better
than his second in this regard.* In the first book, Lifetime Guarantee, he did
very well in expressing this synergistic truth by representing Christ as
saying, “Your job is to do the very best you can, trusting that I’m doing it
through you, and leave the results to Me” (italics his, bold mine).46 He then
followed up with a very good question: “Are you trusting the Lord Jesus to
do the work through you as you do your best, or are you doing your best
hoping that He will approve of your efforts?”47
        A profound and wonderful difference exits between the two. We must
do our best not merely for Him, but through Him, relying on His
performance through us. To portray the victorious Christian life as free of
strenuous effort and struggle is a complete misrepresentation of the situation.
What God does through us is not done for us in the sense of removing the
necessity of strenuous exertion on our part. It is a synergistic endeavor.
Through the strength, energy, stimulation, and motivation that God provides,
we must strive to be victorious and to attain the attending rewards. We must
become very active, intermediate agents and exert considerable effort in
earning these rewards.
        On the other hand, it is not us, but Christ working thorough us, when
we are talking about ultimate agency (Phil 4:13). God (the initializing agent)
is working through us (the secondary agent) to accomplish His will.
Likewise, those who are energized by Satan exert considerable effort in
fighting against the gospel. Although Satan is the ultimate agent, his
subordinate agents are still responsible intermediate agents. From these
offsetting examples, one may glean that both sets of intermediate agents
(those energized by the Holy Spirit and the unholy spirit) will be judged and
rewarded on the same basis: for the works they have done (Rom 2:6;
Rev 22:12). They are responsible for the way they respond to the influence
being exerted upon them and within them because it is not an overpowering
control. The active response of the intermediate agent to the ultimate agent
by either party is called “work.” The response given to both parties is called
a “reward.”
        Rewards are not granted for passive, non-voluntary responses but for
active, voluntary responses. Calling the reward (in this case the salvation
that the believer is to achieve, produce, work out in Phil 2:12) a gift is really
a complete distortion of what constitutes a gift. A gift is that which we
passively receive rather than something we actively attain, accomplish, or
bring about (katergazomai). In contrast, a reward is based on our work, our
performance, our active volitional response to God’s commands. One cannot
be completely passive and hope to earn a reward. This type of salvation is a
reward.

*
    See “Gal 2:20 and Passive Nikology” in Soteric and Mistholic Faith.
Page 70                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
        God is the ultimate agent. We are the intermediate agents. From this
perspective, we may thus be described as active co-agents with God. For this
reason, we may co-reign with Him in heaven. In working with Him, as well
as in ruling with Him, we actively are involved in the action. We labor
through the power of the enabling grace that God supplies (1Cor 15:10).
Although He takes the initiative, we have a choice as to whether we will
follow His leading. Contrary to Calvinistic assertion, God does not
irresistibly cause our effort any more than He irresistibly causes our faith. A
gift is that which we passively receive rather than something we actively
attain. Like Paul, we strive and fight in attaining these rewards. We are to be
very active intermediate agents and exert considerable effort in earning these
rewards. On the other hand, it is not I but Christ when we are talking about
ultimate agency.
        Naturally, Phil 2:14 is a command to believers. By its very nature, the
command demonstrates that not all believers inevitability avoid grumbling
or disputing; otherwise, there would be no need for the command to tell
them not to engage in this negative conduct. This sinful behavior among
believers is certainly subject to punishment at the Bema. Correspondingly,
poor performance gives grounds for the misthological fear in verse 12. Verse
15 is optimistic because such misthological blamelessness is achievable
through God’s sustaining grace.* Verse 16 shows the necessity rather than
inevitability of holding fast and contextually retains the imperatival force
from the impetrative of verse 14.† The readers are commanded to hold fast
so that Paul will not be found to have run in vain and thus fail to achieve the
fullest experience of misthological glory possible, that is, the biggest prize
achievable at the mistholic out-resurrection (Phil 3:11-14). As in Phil 1:6,
Paul has his eyes on the misthological goal line to be crossed on the day of

*
  See “Misthological Sinlessness” in The Outer Darkness.
†
  The Greek word that Paul uses in Phil 2:16 for hold fast is epecho, which is
a compound of epi + echo. The root (echo) means to hold. The prefix (epi)
intensifies the root meaning so that the compound is translated as hold fast.
Katecho is likewise a compound of kata + echo and is also translated as hold
fast. Paul uses the imperatival form of katecho in 1Thess 5:21 to command
believers to hold fast. Contextually, its synonym has the same force in the
Philippian context. Additionally, katecho is used contingently in 1Cor 15:2.
In both the Corinthian and Philippian passages, the believers’ misthological
salvation is at stake and is conditioned on their holding fast. This verb is also
used contingently of believers in Heb 3:6,14. The same compound verb is
used to exhort believers to hold fast in Heb 10:23. The writer of Hebrews
uses a synonym in Heb 4:14 to exhort believers to hold fast (krateo). This
verb is used misthologically in Rev 3:11: “Hold fast what you have, so that
no one will take your crown” (NAU).
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                       Page 71
Christ (2:16). Bema is coming, but misthological confidence is possible—if
believers work diligently to achieve the potential reward of salvation.

       Application
       The present writer thoroughly enjoys a song concerning this passage
of Phil 1:6 by Steve Green: He Who Began a Good Work in You. While the
author was riding in the car singing the song, the author’s wife asked: “How
can you sing that song since you do not believe it?” Talk about a failure to
communicate! The author had given his wife the Reader’s Digest version of
his theology, in which he had rejected the Calvinistic notion that the verse
unconditionally guarantees that God who began the work of sanctification at
the time of regeneration will irresistibly cause all saints to persevere in
progressive sanctification. In short, the verse does not teach the
soteriological preservation of the saints, much less the Calvinistic
perseverance of the saints. Instead, when interpreted in its historical context,
the verse teaches that God may be counted upon to bring the work of the
Philippians (in contributing to Paul’s ministry) to full fruition in the course
of Christian history with the result that they would derive a full reward when
they arrived at the JSC. Steve Green’s song contained nothing of this
historical context, so the author’s wife was confused by the pleasure her
husband found in singing the song.
       Perhaps, a similar verse may help further illustrate the author’s point
of view. In 1Cor 1:8, Paul is confident that God can be counted on to enable
believers to be misthologically blameless: “Who will also confirm you to the
end, that you may be blameless in the day of our Lord Jesus Christ.”48 This
verse involves practical sanctification, of course. The only way you, as a
believer, can achieve misthological blamelessness at the Bema in the day of
Christ is through practical holiness. However, in light of 1Cor 3:11-15, Paul
does not intend for this positive expectation he expresses in 1:8 to be taken
as a guarantee that each believer within that congregation will attain this
misthological status.49
       This is the same epistle in which he shares that he could not even
guarantee his own misthological status (1Cor 9:27)! Paul’s confidence in his
readers is not intended as an unconditional prediction at either a corporate or
individual level. In other words, Paul intends for his words to be taken as an
expression of his positive expectation rather than as a prophetic declaration.
Both he and the Corinthians must rely on God’s strength in order to achieve
the anticipated results. Hodges likewise understands 1Cor 1:8 to refer to
misthological blamelessness and connects it with the misthological
perfection of Col 1:22. As Hodges points out, such maturity in practical
holiness is not automatically guaranteed since Paul conditions it upon their
perseverance in the next verse (Col 1:23). Paul realizes that failure is
possible. Although Hodges interprets Phil 1:6 in a similar manner to Hart, it
is natural to link the discussion of these passages together as Hodges does.50
Page 72                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
       In fact Hart documents that some writers “understand the verse
[Phil 1:6] in a general way to imply that God is presently sustaining us in
His grace, and that this divine ministry continues a process which began at
salvation.” Then, in a note to this statement, he states: “I do not object to
finding in Phil 1:6 the general principle that God is faithful to his
children.”51 The application is not limited to the interpretation. On the
contrary, in Phil 4:12-13 Paul makes a personal application of a general
principle. In this passage, he says that he can endure because God provides
the strength. He has learned to endure by relying on God’s strength. He can
count on God working in him. Paul is confident that he can count on God’s
strength personally and that the Corinthians and Philippians can count on it
corporately, as well as personally. He can rely on God to complete what He
has started in his own life personally and in the lives of his converts as well.
God, who began a work of holiness in the lives of Paul and his converts,
could be counted upon to complete it.
       In the meantime, God could be relied upon for misthological
blamelessness and practical sanctification. Consequently, the good work
includes (but is not limited to) their contribution, and the fruition is not
limited to converts down through the ages, but includes the Philippians
themselves as well. There is no need to understand Paul as saying, “My
words apply only to your contribution, not to you corporately or personally.”
Instead, one may understand the intended application to embrace believers
both (1) personally and corporately and (2) what they do for the Lord,
(3) what they give to the Lord, and (4) what they are becoming in the Lord.
The verse does have application to progressive sanctification, as Bing
summarizes:

              He is not making a promise, but is only expressing his
       confident feelings. Paul is confident that God will “complete”
       or carry through the impact of their support as its effects are
       multiplied in ministry to others until the return of Christ. As a
       pastor, I have learned that if a person attends our church
       several times in a row, supports us financially, and volunteers
       to help out, I can be confident that God will continue to work
       in them to use them with us. But this is different from a
       guarantee they will always be faithful, and certainly gives me
       no right to make a statement about all Christians everywhere.52

       Although Hart and Bing both accept the contribution motif, they
nevertheless are apparently open to a combined application, if not dual
interpretation. It seems that Paul is making a specific application of the
general principle that we can always count on God. Of course, this does not
mean that God can always count on us. Niemelä has provided an excellent
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                          Page 73
chart on the matter of application. His chart was the inspiration for the
following model.53

       My wife apparently had option number one in mind when she heard
Steve Green’s song. In option one, the application is limited to the
interpretation, so generalization is not an option. However, as is frequently
acknowledged, although a passage may have a single interpretation, it is
typically open to numerous applications. Naturally, proper interpretation
should be the foundation for the application. Hart has provided a thorough
and well-documented interaction with the hermeneutical options in deriving
financial support motif as the specific interpretation. Still, even within the
realm of interpretation, following Dillow’s lead, Hart found it necessary to
consider three valid possibilities as to what Paul meant by
perfection/completion in Phil 1:6. It could mean that God would bless their
financial contribution with (1) continuation through the ministries of those
whom they supported, (2) fruition in bearing much fruit through those
Page 74                              Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
ministries, or (3) consummation in eternal rewards at the Bema. Each of
these three interpretive options is viable, and there is no reason to suppose
that Paul intends for the Philippians to limit their understanding of his words
to any one of these possibilities.
       Steve Green’s song, on the other hand, uses Phil 1:6 as a promise to
believers facing the pressures of everyday trials as a believer. And the
present author, although sharing Hart’s understanding of the passage, does
not fault Green’s song, even if it is taken as a source of relative confidence
concerning practical sanctification. Finding underlying principles in
Scripture and applying them to modern day life is biblical. The underlying
principle in this verse is certainly that you can count on God to bless you in
your faithfulness. Thus, the song may be considered valid on the basis of
option two in which the reader takes a specific promise in Scripture and
makes a general application of the underlying principle.
       However, the reason the present author enjoys the song so much is
because he considers it to be an expression of option three. In his opinion,
Paul himself took the general principles (embodied in the truths found within
the texts dealing with external confidence such as Gal 5:10 and 2Thess 3:4),
mixed them with the internal affirmations of God working within the lives of
believers (Phil 2:13; 4:13), and then encapsulated them in one potent
application in Phil 1:6 that is couched in a context that flavors it with a
motivational impetus for supporting missions. This verse is indeed powerful
medicine for believers seeking encouragement. Green is to be commended
for making this tonic widely available through his lyrics. The financial
aspect is just the specific flavor this truth happens to find in this particular
context, but this truth was never intended by Paul to be limited to this one
context. It comes in multiple flavors. In this case, option two was preceded
by option three. Paul condensed it before Green broadened it. Both
approaches are valid.

       Conclusion
       A variety of texts teach that grace is resistible in the lives of believers.
Calvinists might respond that they acknowledge this fact and claim that only
regenerational grace is irresistible, but this claim is suspect in that their
doctrine of perseverance requires some form of irresistible grace, at least as
expressed in the form of God’s parental discipline. Historically, it should be
recalled that this Augustinian view of irresistibility was not applied to
regenerational grace but to persevering grace. Augustinian regeneration
could be lost. Regardless, from the contemporary Calvinistic prospective,
God’s discipline (as an expression of His irresistible corrective grace)
allegedly will not fail to bring His wayward child back into conformity with
the path of righteous, or God’s chastisement will prematurely end the life of
a believer who otherwise would persist in following the wrong path.
Chapter 1. Fallen From Grace                                         Page 75
        The internal inconsistency within this Calvinistic schema is self
apparent: A grace that can be resisted unto the point of death is not
irresistible in its remediation. With Kendall, I would also question whether
premature death is even the final or most severe form of discipline. Rather, I
would assert that misthological discipline is a step beyond terminal
discipline and renders the Calvinistic notion of perseverance absurd.
        In any event, misthological grace is a far better attested category of
grace in Scripture than irresistible grace. Grace can be resisted so that it fails
to result in rewards. In fact, grace itself is a reward that believers may fail to
achieve in certain contexts. Grace can be resisted so that it fails to result in a
fuller manifestation of itself upon the believer. Depending upon the context,
grace may be either merited or unmerited favor. When the result is based on
grace and works (i.e. on our active response), which is what one would
anticipate in the case of enabling grace in synergistic endeavors, then the
result is a reward. When what is granted is based solely on grace and our
passive response, then what is conferred is a free gift. Soteriological grace is
not based on works; misthological grace is based on works. Similar
statements can be made concerning mercy.
        As to unconditional security, since soteriological grace is not based
on works of any type, it cannot be based on misthological faith.
Consequently, the Arminian insistence that one must persevere in faith in
order to reach heaven is necessarily false. Persevering faith is rewarded and
thus falls into the misthological category. Likewise, falling from or being cut
off from God’s enabling grace in no way destroys the foundation of
soteriological grace which makes enabling grace and misthological grace
available to the believer. Fuller experiences of grace are misthological.
Deficient postconversional levels of enabling grace are incapable of
nullifying the saving grace that is granted independent of one’s performance
or perseverance. For this reason, Paul can affirm the soteriological sonship
of apostate believers, even though they have fallen experientially from
enabling grace and have fallen theologically from soteriological grace.
        As to the latter, they fell from soteric grace, but they did not fall
soteriologically from that state of grace. Their departure from the grace that
saved them did not result in their loss of the salvation it freely brought them.
Soteriological grace cannot be forfeited. Soteriologically, this realm of grace
is permanent. Contrastively, believers may fail to realize the fuller, potential
misthological nature of their sonship.
        Proverbial and parental expectations should not be confused with
prophetic promises. Not all children or believers will respond in a positive
manor to the discipline they receive from their earthly parents or heavenly
Father. Nevertheless, optimistically expecting positive results is still
understandable when such discipline is lovingly administered, as long as a
reasonable sense of realism is retained. This biblical realism prevents one
from allowing Calvinists to use the believer’s response to God’s discipline
Page 76                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
as a probability scale to remark that certain believers are probably not saved
because of their deplorable conduct.
        Believers are not robots devoid of free will. They can rebel.
Optimistic expectations of the outcome of God’s discipline fall short of
allowing us to look at rebellious believers and conclude that they are lost—
or even probably lost. One cannot be absolutely certain that God’s discipline
will necessarily result in a positive response. Thus, stating with absolute
confidence that an apostate is lost is irresponsible. Even when such
statements regarding the conversion experience of another believer are
reduced to statements of probability, they tend to manifest a legalistic
mentality. Believers can fall from grace, fall short of grace, and be severed
from grace. Believers can be failures.
        While being frank about the shortcomings of some fellow believers,
we must refuse to allow the despicable results manifested by such believers
to make us pessimistic in our expectations for believers in general. We can
count on God to exert considerable positive influence in response to our
prayers and faithfulness so that positive results are expected. At the same
time, we realistically should acknowledge that the outer darkness shows that
God allows even His own children freewill rebellion. If the outer darkness is
what His children want, as attested by their lack of faith or faithfulness, God
will allow His children to have it. Failure is possible but unnecessary.
Moreover, to those responding positively to God, failure is clearly not the
expected outcome. Optimism should be the overriding rule of thumb, while
realistically acknowledging exceptions to this general rule.
                       Chapter 2.
               Not Fallen from Perfection
       Introduction
       The proveitistic appeal to good works as necessary proof of salvation
springs up in surprising locations.* One commentator, for example, says that
the casting of the lazy servant into the outer darkness in Mt 25:30 does not
teach salvation by works but instead shows that works are necessary as the
evidence of salvation. Obviously, however, the servant was a servant even
though the servant did not serve. His lack of service did not prove that he did
not belong to his master as a servant. He was a servant despite his lack of
service. The evidence-of-salvation interpretation is sadly mistaken in
assuming that all the Lord’s servants serve.
       Some proveitistic appeals to good works or service as necessary
evidence are not so easily unmasked, however. One endeavor of this present
chapter will be to unmask more subtle forms of proveitism and provide
integration with my arguments found elsewhere in disproving proveitism.
Nonetheless, although proveitists are deadly assailants to unconditional
security, the most direct assault comes from forfeitism (classically known as
Arminianism). Both proveitism and forfeitism avow conditional security.
According to both theologies, perseverance in the faith is necessary to enter
heaven. Marrowism, in contrast, affirms unconditional security. In this
chapter, Marrowism will go toe-to-toe with these soteric contenders on the
aspectual battlefield as it relates to the believer’s unconditional permanent
perfective state.
       Perhaps the most famous proof text for regarding works as necessary
evidence is the context of Jesus statement in Mt 7:16-20, where He said that
we would “know them by their fruits.” In the minds of many, these fruits are
thought to prove that good works are necessary as vindicating evidence for

*
  Proveitists is a coined abbreviation for prove-it-tists: advocates of prove-it
theology. Many proveitists will object to being calling Calvinists. Perhaps
they are Calminians or closet Calvinists. In any event, the term proveitists is
broadly used to denote those who (like Calvinists) maintain that the way a
person lives proves whether that person is a genuine believer. Proveitism
asserts that fruit in the form of perseverance is necessary proof of salvation:
If you do not have this fruit, then you were never saved initially.
Perseverance is necessary to prove that you are saved and to enter heaven.
Calvinists, closet Calvinists, and Calminians would thus be included under
the proveitistic umbrella.
Page 78                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
saving faith. Fanning, on the other hand, has advocated taking the fruit in the
heart of this passage as a distributive production of a single fruit by each
tree. My corresponding argument has been that fruit singular in this passage
refers to one’s faith rather than to one’s works. A good tree produces saving
faith; a bad tree produces non-saving faith. Soteriological vindication by
works is not in view.* This text supplies no support for proveitism. Indeed, it
is poorly suited for proveitism.

       Descriptive Distributive Durative
        Supplementation of that discussion would be well served by
considering some common examples of the distributive aspect. Realizing
that the general reader of this book will not be an aspectologist, I will pause
long enough to provide explanations and illustrations that hopefully will
bridge the gap between the technical information and the lay reader. After
all, I not only approach this discussion as someone interested in aspectology
but as a father who would hope to bring the fruits of this study down to a
level comprehensible by his sons.
        So let me begin by explaining that in the Greek verb system the
aspect (or type of action of a verb) can be denoted in a variety of ways.
When aspectologists (i.e., specialists in the study of the type of aspect/action
of Greek verbs) assert that a verb has a distributive force, this simply means
that the action of the verb is distributed over a period of time. It could be
represented as a series of dots (z z z z z z).† Most importantly, for the
present study, the biblical student needs to be aware that the same agent may
not be the one performing the action on each occasion. The same action
might be performed on repeated occasions by different agents. In fact, each
agent/person might perform the stated action only once. I will provide
numerous examples to illustrate this well-known biblical phenomenon.
        The ensuing demonstration of the distributive aspect will draw
primarily on those examples provided by Fanning to further illustrate the
nature of his argument that multiple agents may be in view who perform the
specified action only once each. Subsequent to these illustrative texts, I will
demonstrate that good works are not soteriologically necessitative even if
they are descriptive. In other words, even if conditionalists are handed their
cake on a silver platter so that a text (such as Heb 10:14) is interpreted as
describing progressive sanctification, such progressive sanctification cannot
be construed as necessary evidence of God’s permanent protection.


*
 See Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity and The Outer Darkness.
†
  For those who would like a more detailed discussion as to the type of
distribution, such as iterative versus customary, see Believe: An Aspectual and
Metaphorical Analysis of the Gospel of John.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                Page 79

      Distributive Examples

      Distributive Aorist
                        *
       The aorist ({ ⋱ ) may be used to give an eternal summary of the
individual distributive occurrences (VANT, 167-168).54

       •   Jesus healed many (Mk 3:10): multiple healings of multiple
           people, not multiple healings of an individual.
       •   It has often thrown him into the fire and water (Mk 9:22): an
           agent performing an action repeatedly on an individual. Thus,
           the distributive can be used of individuals who are “involved
           with the occurrences once or more” (VANT, 258).
       •   All seven had her as wife (Mk 12:23): multiple agents with one
           object at separate times, not at the same time.
       •   They contributed of their surplus (Mk 12:44): multiple
           contributions by multiple agents, not multiple contributions by
           an individual.
       •   That they might receive the Holy Spirit (Acts 8:15): multiple
           people receiving the Spirit, not multiple receptions of the Spirit
           by an individual.
       •   Many believed in the Lord all over Joppa (Acts 9:42): many
           came to faith at various times, not all at one time.
       •   All these died in faith (Heb 11:13): these believers died at
           various times.

      Distributive Imperfect and Present
     The present and imperfect ()† may be used to give an internal
summary of the individual distributive occurrences (VANT, 168).

       •   And they were casting out many demons (Mk 6:13): multiple
           expulsions out of multiple people, not necessarily multiple
           demons out of an individual person.
       •   They were anointing many sick people with oil (Mk 6:13):
           multiple anointing of a multiple people, not multiple anointing of
           individual people.

      ⋱ in Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity. My own translation may

       •    Demons were coming out of many (Lk 4:41): multiple demons
            out of multiple people, not necessarily multiple demons out of
