       •    They received the Holy Spirit (Acts 8:17): multiple people
            receiving the Spirit, not multiple receptions of the Spirit by an
            individual.
       •    The kings of the earth bring their glory and honor into it
            (Rev 21:24): multiple kings. Whether or not they each make a
            singular entry or iterative entries would be decided contextually.

       Pas + Articular Participle or Anarthrous Noun
       Generics created from pas with an articular participle or singular
anarthrous noun may find their plurality simply in the durative aspect and
translatable as whoever (VANT, 183, 216). Everyone is also a common
translation.

       •    Everyone who asks receives (Lk 11:10): multiple asking by
            multiple people, not multiple asking by one person. Although
            persistent asking might be indicated by the context (Lk 11:8),
            this information must be provided by the context since the
            construction in v. 10 is insufficient to convey it.
       •    Everyone who asks receives (Mt 7:8): multiple asking by
            multiple people, not multiple asking by one person.
       •    Every good tree bears good fruit (Mt 7:17): multiple trees
            producing good fruit, not multiple fruits being produced by
            individual trees.
       •    Every bad tree bears bad fruit (Mt 7:17): multiple trees
            producing bad fruit, not multiple fruits being produced by
            individual trees.
       •    Everyone who believes is justified (Acts 13:39): multiple
            justifications of multiple people, not multiple justifications or
            linear justification of an individual.
       •    Every house is built by someone (Heb 3:4): building of multiple
            houses, not multiple buildings of an individual house.

       Articular Participle as Substantive
       If the articular participle is a substantive (as is frequently the case and
thus functions as a noun), then the action by the individual normally is
punctiliar (z) with the sense: “the one who (ever) does” (VANT, 183).
Perhaps Fanning means whoever in the sense of anyone.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                 Page 81
       •   Whoever receives you receives Me (Mt 10:40): multiple
           receivers, not multiple receptions by same individual.
       •   Whoever does what is true comes to the light (Jn 3:21): not the
           practice of the truth by a single individual.
       •   Whoever believes in the Son has eternal life (Jn 3:36): multiple
           believers, not linear belief by the believer.

        After all, the NRS translates Mt 10:40 as whoever, NLT as anyone.
CJB, ESV, GWN, NAB, NIB, and NIV translate Jn 3:36 as whoever, the
NLT as anyone. Moreover, although Fanning specifically mentions articular
participles using pas as being understood in the sense of whoever, he
nevertheless lumps these substantive articular participles along with the pas
articular participles cited above as examples in that discussion (VANT, 216).
Therefore, articular participles both with and without pas might jointly be
translated as whoever. On the other hand, Fanning immediately goes on to
insist:

             It should be observed from these examples that generics
      are usually multiple in a distributive sense: the plurality which
      is predicated consists of each one of a group doing an act a
      single time, rather than repeatedly. Thus, the sense of a generic
      utterance is usually an absolute statement of what each one
      does once, and not a statement of the individual’s customary or
      habitual activity. (VANT, 216-217; italics his.)

        Because Fanning prefaces this strong affirmation with the word
usually, we should not take his statement absolutely—independent of the
context. When it applies punctiliarly, it applies absolutely. Whether it
applies punctiliarly is a question of context, syntax, and probability.
        Despite these caveats, the obvious should not go unstated. What is
being asserted is that normally whoever performs these actions even once
(i.e. even a single time) qualifies as a member of the group being predicated.
This means that whoever ever performs the action qualifies for membership
in the group being described.55 Therefore, the true sense of what Fanning is
stating is better expressed as:

       •   Whoever ever receives you receives Me (Mt 10:40): multiple
           receivers, not multiple receptions by same individual.
       •   Whoever ever does what is true comes to the light (Jn 3:21): not
           the practice of the truth by a single individual.
       •   Whoever ever believes in the Son has eternal life (Jn 3:36):
           multiple believers, not linear belief by the believer.
Page 82                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
        Personally, I am not necessarily inclined to agree with the
implications of Fanning’s comments as applied to Jn 3:21—that a single
occurrence of obedience to the truth is intended in this particular example.
Yet, at the same time, I concede that the punctiliar understanding of even
this verse is one of four possible FG interpretations.* Nonetheless,
contextually, in this particular case, I suspect that Jesus probably is referring
to a durative practice of the truth. So, unlike Fanning, I would not have cited
this verse as an example of the punctiliar point Fanning is trying to make.
        To share yet another example where FG interpreters are not
necessarily agreed aspectually, in commenting on 1Jn 5:5, Hodges correctly
states regarding the articular present participle being used as a substantive:

             This construction in Greek is essentially timeless and
      characterizes an individual (or individuals) by some act or acts
      he has (or they have) performed. Such statements have their
      closet analogy to many English nouns (often ending in –er) that
      express completed and/or ongoing [durative] action. For
      example, “He is a murderer.” In this case the person may be
      described this way based on one instance of murder [z] or on
      the basis of many such acts [£].56

        As for Hodges’ personal preference on this particular occasion, he
would understand this particular verse aspectually as thus: “And who is the
one who overcomes [£] the world, but he who believes [£] that Jesus is the
Son of God? Present, experiential victory on the part of a believer is
contingent upon that believer’s persevering in faith. Contextually, Hodges
and likeminded FG interpreters are correct in stressing the possibility (rather
than inevitability) of linear present victory.57
        What about the deduction, then, that asserts (via Fanning) that when a
(present tense) articular participle is used as a substantive, it normally is
used generically to indicate a punctiliar response so that the classification is
intended for whoever ever performs the stated action? Is this assessment
nullified by adopting a linear perspective in the two examples above ? Not at
all. These two exceptions to Fanning’s rule do not nullify the rule. As
already noted, when Fanning’s affirmation of a singular punctiliar aspect
applies, it applies absolutely. Whether it applies is a question of context,
syntax, and probability. I concur with Fanning that his statement applies
absolutely in the two remaining examples he provided. Accordingly, they
may be translated properly as whoever ever:


*
 See “Substantival Categorization” in Believe: An Aspectual and Metaphorical
Analysis from the Gospel of John for many more such examples.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                 Page 83
       •   Whoever ever receives you receives Me (Mt 10:40): singular
           reception is sufficient to be considered a receiver.
       •   Whoever ever believes in the Son has eternal life (Jn 3:36):
           singular response of belief is sufficient to be considered a
           believer.

       To supplement Fanning’s examples with a few more to illustrate that
this construction may refer to anyone, everyone, whoever, those, or the one
who ever performs the stated action, a few more instances will suffice:

       •   "For the gate is small, and the way is narrow that leads to life,
           and few are those who ever find it (Mt 7:14): singular finding is
           sufficient to be considered a finder.
       •    Whoever ever curses father or mother, let him be put to death
           (Mt 15:4): singular cursing is sufficient for the death penalty.
       •   Whoever ever believes in the Son has eternal life; but whoever
           never believes the Son shall not see life, but the wrath of God
           abides on him (Jn 3:36): singular response of belief is sufficient
           to qualify as a possessor; never responding with a singular
           response of belief qualifies one as an unbeliever.
       •   I am the bread of life; whoever ever comes to Me will not
           hunger, and whoever ever believes in Me will never thirst
           (Jn 6:35): singular response of eating/believing prevents one
           from ever hungering or thirsting.
       •   Whoever ever believes in Me has eternal life (Jn 6:47; MT):
           singular response of belief is sufficient to qualify as a possessor.
       •   I am the resurrection and the life; whoever ever believes in Me
           shall live even if he dies (Jn 11:25): singular response of belief
           qualifies one for resurrection.
       •   Whoever ever believes in Me, does not believe in Me but in Him
           who sent Me (Jn 12:44): singular response of belief is sufficient
           to qualify as a believer.
       •   Whoever ever rejects Me, and does not receive My sayings, has
           one who judges him (Jn 12:48): singular rejection is sufficient to
           be considered a rejecter.
       •   Therefore you are without excuse, everyone of you whoever ever
           passes judgment, for in that you judge another, you condemn
           yourself; for you who judge practice the same things (Rom 2:1):
           singular act of judging is sufficient to be considered a judge.
       •   Whoever ever believes that Jesus is the Christ is born of God
           (1Jn 5:1): singular response of belief is sufficient to qualify for
           birth.
Page 84                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
       •    Whoever ever believes in the Son of God has the witness in
            himself; whoever does not ever believe God has made Him a
            liar, because he has not believed in the witness that God has
            borne concerning His Son (1Jn 5:10): singular response of belief
            is sufficient to qualify for the witness; never responding with a
            singular response of belief makes God out to be a liar.

       Distributive Test Cases
       Having now surveyed some basic examples of texts which use a
distributive aspect, we can now focus critical attention on a few critical texts
to see if the conditionalistic claim withstands scrutiny that such texts make
good works the necessary evidence that one has been saved.

       Heb 10:14
       •    For by one offering He has perfected for all time those who are
            (being) sanctified.

       Substantive
       This articular present participle is translated in one of two basic ways:
as either saying that we are sanctified or are being sanctified. As just noted,
such expressions usually are substantive and indicate a punctiliar aspect (z)
for the individual. According to Fanning, this aspect is distributed as a
plurality for the multiple individuals composing that class of individuals:

             Generics are usually multiple in a distributive sense:
      the plurality which is predicated consists of each one of a
      group [i.e., each individual] doing an act a single time, rather
      than repeatedly. Thus, the sense of a generic utterance is
      usually an absolute statement of what each one does once,
      and not a statement of the individual’s customary or habitual
      [or iterative] activity….the possibility of a habitual sense
      cannot be ruled out entirely…but it seems less likely”
      (VANT, 217; italics his).

        Probability would lean in favor of regarding this phrase to be a
substantive referring to those who are sanctified, the sanctified, the saints.
The writer of Hebrews is assuring his reader that as saints they are perfected
for all time (z▬▬ ). OT sacrifices cannot add to that perfection. Whoever is
a saint (i.e., set apart as belonging to God) is perfected for all time (in terms
of belonging to God). More specifically, the text may be understood
aspectually as asserting:
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                               Page 85
       •   For by one offering He has perfected (z▬▬ ) for all time
           whoever ever is sanctified (z).

Stated conversely, anyone who is sanctified at anytime (z) has been
perfected for all time (z▬▬ ). If this probable perspective is adopted, then
the verse confirms OSAS (Once Saved Always Saved), which herein will be
equated with unconditional security).

      Timeless
       A closely related perspective is to express much the same position in
terms of timelessness. Some commentators prefer simply to affirm this
timeless perspective. Whoever has been sanctified at any time has been
permanently perfected for all time (z▬▬ ). If this perspective is adopted,
then the verse confirms OSAS.
                Illustration 8. Timeless View Plotted in Time
Page 86                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
        Perhaps the writer of Hebrews was thinking in such vague terms as
whoever and whenever. On the other hand, he uses the plural form saints to
affirm that saints throughout the ages are perfected for all time, regardless of
the time they came to saving faith or the length of time they maintained that
persuasion. Such a view naturally lends itself to an iterative graphical
representation that can be plotted in time. Suppose, for example, that during
the course of their first year at their college while attending a Monday night
Bible study: Joe came to saving faith the first month, Sue the second, Sam
the third, and so on. The point in time (z) that they each became
permanently perfected as belonging to God could be plotted on a graph,
showing the point in time they came to belong to God eternally (z▬▬ ).
Nevertheless, at this point in the discussion, I am only plotting the points in
time (z) on the graph so as to focus on the distributive nature of the group
action.

       Distributive
       Logically, the timeless interpretation of Heb 10:14 leads to a
distributive aspect () when plotted for the multiple individuals to
whom it applies. Each individual saint is perfected for all time (z▬▬ ) when
he or she comes to faith. I am therefore asserting that at the punctiliar (z)
moment of belief, a believer comes into the permanent (z▬▬ ) possession of
eternal life.
                         Illustration 9. Stative Result




        The action of believing (i.e., the saving response of faith) is itself
punctiliar (z), while the corresponding stated benefit (namely, having
eternal life) is stative (▬). The perfective tense is generically represented as
a combination of punctiliar action with stative results as: z▬ . The stative
aspect of the perfect tense refers to the resultant state: ▬. The perfective and
stative categories can be used roughly interchangeably.

¾ “He who believes (z) in the Son has (▬) eternal life” (Jn 3:36).
¾ “He who believes (z) has (▬) eternal life (Jn 6:47).
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                  Page 87


       In terms of the chart at this stage of the discussion, however, I am
only plotting the points in time (z) for each individual when he or she first
came to saving faith. Thus, each believer is represented by a single dot.
Some would plot the distribution of these dots along a single axis so that it
looks like a horizontal series of dots: z z z z z z. Such a representation is
fine as long as one remembers that each dot represents a different agent (i.e.,
a different person in terms of the illustration). Otherwise, this representation
would be taken mistakenly as a simple iterative aspect performed by one
agent.
       The distributive aspect, however, is an iterative aspect performed in
reference to multiple agents, not one agent. To avoid confusion and to allow
greater specification, stretching out this distribution with two axis over an
indefinite period of time provides a better model. In the timeless model
pictured in Illustration 8, the dots slant up in order to show the number of
converts going up through the countless ages. The focus is on the number of
converts, not on the time of their conversions. The distributive model used
herein, in contrast, has the dots going down: . One normally speaks of
proceeding down through the ages when focusing on time. The number of
agents increases as one proceeds down the corridors of time.
            Illustration 10. Distributive Distribution of Perfection
Page 88                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
       Christ’s perfection is applied to the believer the moment he or she
comes to Christ in saving faith. To clarify (as Fanning does) that the action
is a singular occurrence that happens only once for each agent, and is
therefore punctiliar (z) for every participant on whatever particular occasion
the event occurs, a better model would stress the punctual nature of the event
for the individual: . Joe is perfected forever the moment he comes to
Christ in saving faith; he does not have to wait until the second semester (or
even the next split second) to be perfected objectively forever as belonging
to God. If the distributive perspective is adopted (regardless as to which
symbol is used to picture it), then the verse confirms OSAS. This
distributive option is popular among the commentators.

       Descriptive
       Conditionalists love to speak of progressive sanctification as being
descriptive as opposed to prescriptive. Bearing good fruits in terms of
Christian behavior is said to be a description of those who are saved rather
than a condition for salvation. Securitists, in contrast, acknowledge that good
works are the natural outflow of a believer’s life. The contention, therefore,
between conditional securitists and unconditional securitists comes when
those advocating a descriptive mindset try to make it a necessitative aspect.
Conditionalists insist that perseverance is necessary to reach heaven, but
some of them try to make it necessary as a description rather than a
condition. According to such a perspective, if you are elect, you will
persevere and go to heaven as a result. Perseverance is the proof that you are
elect. According to this necessitative theory advocated by proveitists, heaven
is conditioned on election rather than on perseverance
       The present passage provides an opportunity to demonstrate that such
a skewed perspective is deficient, both logically and biblically speaking.
Before proceeding to consider the necessitative outlook, however, a word
needs to be said about the descriptive aspect. Hodges cuts the necessitative
argument off at its descriptive knees:

              The use of the present tense does not imply that the
       action involved cannot stop. On the contrary, the present
       participle is used of actions that have stopped! For
       example....In the final analysis the Greek construction
       translated by “he who believes” or “the one who believes” is
       merely descriptive. It identifies a person as “a believer,” but it
       does not specify anything at all about the continuity of the
       action.58

     If this definition of the descriptive aspect is adopted, then it leaves no
room for the conditionalistic appeal to linear action (£) as a necessary
component of that aspect. The writer of Hebrews simply would be
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                 Page 89
describing the person as a saint, not as one undergoing the sanctification
process. Grammarians, on the other hand, tend to define the descriptive
aspect as a brief action that is portrayed as vividly taking place now in the
present.59 As Fanning points out, it would be a little odd (albeit not
impossible) to picture a punctiliar act as vividly occurring before one’s very
eyes (VANT 161). In short, the perspective adopted herein will be that the
descriptive present (→) is to be distinguished from the progressive present
(£) in that the latter portrays the action as taking place continuously even
now; whereas, the former only portrays it as simply taking place now. Since
the action is taking place now, the most natural impression is that it is in
process of taking place. Even the swing of a baseball bat, if portrayed in
slow enough motion, may be portrayed as a process. Adopting this
perspective allows dialogue with the conditionalistic perspective on its own
turf. In deference to the point Hodges is making, however, and as will be
demonstrated below, let it be noted further that this descriptive aspect (→)
does not specify the necessity of the continuity of this brief action. The
descriptive aspect may hint at or allow brevity of action, but it does not
necessitate a linear view of this action. The simple descriptive model allows
the progressive (or better yet ingressive) aspect, but it does not demand it.
                   Illustration 11. Descriptive Distributive
Page 90                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
        Christ’s perfection is applied to the believer in Heb 10:14. The stress
is not on the believer’s progression in faith (→). Rather, anyone who may be
described as a believer at any point in time is assured of perfection for all
time, regardless of how long he or she may believe. This conclusion follows
necessarily from the biblical affirmation that such a person is perfected
forever. Even aside from the previous deductions regarding aspectual
probability, logically it follows that whoever ever believes is perfected
forever. This deduction is absolutely necessary logically.
        Joe is progressing in sanctification and is perfected forever as a result
of his sanctification. Does this mean that he will or must continue to undergo
sanctification to have this perfection? No, it does not. The aspectual stress is
not on his progression but on his perfection. In order for conditional security
to be true, the writer of Hebrews would have to have a durative, not merely
descriptive, aspect in mind.
        One could even use the simple distributive symbol  to denote a
distributive action taking place any time and the punctiliar descriptive
modification  to describe the distributive action as it is taking place
right now. The big dot comes in the middle of the dots coming before and
after it and displays the action as currently happening right now, rather than
at the point of time indicated by the dots which come before or later. This
aoristic accentuation might be taken as descriptive of the timeless principle
being applicable anytime, specifically now, without necessitating any notion
of linearity for the event as it applies to that particular individual. This
modification would be a hybrid of the distributive and descriptive models
and still be denoted by .
        If anything, the non-emphatic arrow → merely points to the large
center dot  as being a punctiliar action taking place right now. The
non-emphatic arrow only points to the emphatic dot. If Heb 10:14 is simply
descriptive, then those who are being sanctified now are perfect forever,
regardless of however briefly their sanctification process may last. Those
who presently are being sanctified are perfect forever, even if they do not
continue in this sanctification process for any appreciable amount of time. If
either the simple descriptive perspective or the hybrid descriptive model is
adopted, then the verse confirms OSAS.

       Durative
        As counterintuitive as it may seem to the text and to the normal use of
the distributive aspect, because conditionalists need a durative aspect (£),
let it be granted, for sake of argument, that the writer of Hebrews may have
had such an aspect in mind. To express the conditionalistic mindset in its
strongest possible terms, let it be assumed that the writer of Hebrews is
assuring his readers that believers who persevere (£) in faith until the end of
their lives are perfected forever. Since the benefits of Christ’s perfection
already have been applied explicitly to such believers for all time, according
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                   Page 91
                                                       *
to Heb 10:14, the application must be permanent. For argument’s sake,
nonetheless, let it be further entertained that the writer is thinking of the
believer as a perseverer.
              Illustration 12. Distributive Durative Until the End




       Have I at last entertained a hypothetical concession that results in
NOSAS? Not necessarily. One might conjecture the possibility of some type
of descriptive durative (£) aspect in Heb 10:14 but merely regard the
durative aspect as descriptive of a process going on now. Such an approach
does not make it necessary to progress or persevere in that process until the
*
  With reference to the speaker’s portrayal of the event, the indicative mood
normally indicates absolute time: past, present, future (PPF). Using that coding,
I use this symbol to integrate time with aspect for the descriptive present:
  ¤        . Compare the corresponding short arrows in Illustration 11.
Descriptive Distributive. Similarly, the progressive present would be: Î .
Note the corresponding long arrows in Illustration 12. Distributive Durative
Until the End. The latter arrow reaches to the end of the present time period.
        Pertinent to the present discussion of Illustration 11, I am allowing the
possibility that the writer of Hebrews conceivably might have had a descriptive
¤ aspect in the back of his mind as he wrote Heb 10:14. Even if this is the
case, it is certainly not in the forefront of his thinking.
Page 92                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
end of one’s life in order to be a recipient of the promised perfection. If this
text is conveying a distributive durative, then it may do so descriptively
without making the durative aspect a necessity. Even if the writer is
affirming that those who persevere until the end are perfected forever, doing
so would not make persevering necessary for being perfected forever. If this
verse merely is affirming a telological descriptive distributive durative
aspect, then it is affirming OSAS.
       In other words, suppose the writer is asserting: For by one offering
He has perfected for all time (z▬▬ ) those who are (being) sanctified (£).60
That he is asserting that one must necessarily persevere in order to be
perfected forever does not necessarily follow. In fact, the converse is true.
Because their perfection which already has been granted and completed is
forever (z▬▬ ), those who are being sanctified (£) cannot necessarily need
to continue being sanctified in order to be perfected. Logically, the
conditional argument still begs the question. It still fails to show that the
linear aspect is a soteric necessity.
             Illustration 13. Distributive Durative in the Present




       Moreover, I have been overly gratuitous in granting the possibility
that the writer is making this promise to believers who persevere until the
end. The text does not make this assertion. The addition of until the end is
missing from this text. There is a gap in the text—a missing link in the
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                     Page 93
conditionalistic argument—that makes it impossible to insist that the present
sanctification must be until the end in order for the permanent perfection to
be applicable. If the text is allowed to stand as it is written, at most the writer
is saying that those believers who are currently persevering are perfected
forever. He does not add the stipulation, or even the description for that
matter, that the persevering he has in mind is until the end. To do justice to
the integrity of the text, the most integrity that I can grant to the
conditionalistic perspective is that the biblical writer possibly might be
thinking of a present durative sanctification that reduces soteriologically to a
descriptive durative (→). That is, present perseverance conceivably might be
in mind, but telological perseverance is ruled out as a logical soteriological
necessity.
       For example, if at the end of the first semester, the teacher were to
announce to those believers in attendance at the Bible study that Christ had
perfected them forever, then the teacher might be thinking in descriptive
terms of their present ongoing (→) faith without making their continuation
in faith a condition for such perfection. Even so, the writer conceivably
might simply be reassuring those who are presently persevering that they
have been permanently perfected. If this verse merely is affirming a present
descriptive distributive durative aspect, then it is affirming OSAS.

       Necessitative
       Conditional securitists will contend that the students in the Bible
study must not only progress in sanctification in order for the promised
perfection to be applicable but that they must also progress permanently.
(Conditionalists apply the forever to the wrong part of the verse.) For sake
of argument, the present discussion will not pursue the counterpoint which
demonstrates that making such progression a necessity makes it a condition.*
This durative aspect (if that is indeed the aspect intended by the writer) must
be necessitative rather than merely descriptive in order for one to derive
conditional security. NOSAS needs a necessitative descriptive distributive
durative aspect. A simple descriptive distributive durative results in OSAS.
Two considerations mitigate against the NOSAS necessitation theory.
       If the writer meant to make their progressing in sanctification until
the end necessary for this permanent perfection, then it is very strange that
he did not supply this phrase since he had used this very phrase on three
previous occasions within this epistle (Heb 3:6; 3:14; 6:11). The Greek word
for end in each of these texts is telos. Misthologists would contend that such

*
  As demonstrated in my other writings, much to the chagrin of many
conditionalists, their making progressive sanctification the necessary evidence
of positional sanctification makes progressive sanctification a condition for
glorification.
Page 94                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
telological texts are misthological. That dispute and omission aside for the
moment, however, does Heb 10:14 allow a necessitative interpretation for
the distributive durative aspect so that believers must endure until the end in
order to have the stated benefit? Certainly not. The soteriological perfection
in this text is conveyed by the perfect tense. Even expressing the latter part
of the verse in a descriptive distributive durative fashion leaves no doubt as
to the nature conveyed by the perfect tense perfection in the first part of the
verse:

       (1) Christ has perfected forever (z▬▬ )
       (2) those who are being sanctified now (→).

        The perfect tense is being used in the first part of the verse for an
action that has been completed in the past and whose resulting state is now
being stretched out eschatologically into the eternal future by the adverb
forever. That which has been completed by Christ is progressing
distributively to individual believers in terms of its application as they come
to faith.
        They are sanctified as belonging to the Lord in objective
sanctification and are perfected forever in terms of their position in Christ
the moment they believe. All that is necessary on their part is a moment of
faith, not a duration of faith. The distribution of the perfection does not await
the individual’s progression in sanctification. Rather, the application of this
perfection only awaits the individual’s punctiliar (z) entrance into the state
of positional sanctification. Once that state is entered, the perfection is
applied permanently (z▬▬ ). Linear contingency is ruled out logically for
an eternal static state that already is entered into as a gift. Even if the
Calvinistic claim is granted that the second part of the verse is describing a
distributive durative (£) aspect, the durative part of that aspect cannot be
made necessitative.
        When Joe came to saving faith during the first month of the Bible
study, the completed benefit of Christ’s perfection was applied to him
immediately and eternally. The same thing happened to Sue the moment she
came to saving faith during the second month of the Bible study. They did
not become more perfect during the third month. In terms of their state, they
were perfectly perfect. Their resulting state is stative, static, unchanging
(▬▬ ).
        What if Joe and Sue became sexually involved during the Christmas
break and did not return to the Bible study the second semester and even
abandoned the faith by the end of the school year, would they still be
perfect? Yes. According to the writer of Hebrews, they had been perfected
forever. Therefore, one cannot impose a linear necessity upon their
individual progression. Even if the writer of Hebrews is describing those
who are progressing (→) individually in sanctification as being perfected
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                 Page 95
forever (z▬▬ ), logically he must be thinking dualistically of them as being
(A) descriptively sanctified progressively (→) but (B) necessitatively
sanctified punctiliarly (z). For his statement to make sense, the only aspect
of their sanctification that could be necessary for them to be perfected
eternally would be their punctiliar (z) appropriation. Subsequent,
retrogressive sanctification could not nullify the perfection they already had
been granted eternally. The commencement of their sanctification
necessarily (logically speaking) resulted in their immediate and permanent
perfection.

       Distributive Perfective
        The durative aspect (£) has been entertained above, for sake of
argument, and found to be quite compatible with OSAS from a purely
descriptive aspect (→). The only way to derive NOSAS would be to invest
this durative aspect with a necessitative force. The aspect would need to be
descriptive (→), which is a possibility; however, the context would have to
make it necessitative (£). Instead, the context renders a necessitative linear
aspect suspect, and the syntax makes a necessitative linear aspect abject (£
                                                                           ).
The contextual stress is on the believer’s perfected state (cf. the perfect
participle in 10:10). The perfection provided by Christ is permanently being
distributed to those who come to saving faith in Christ the moment they
individually come to faith. Nonetheless, such perfection does not protect
those who are sanctified from severe misthological punishment if they turn
their back on their Sanctifier: “How much severer punishment do you think
he will deserve who has trampled under foot the Son of God, and has
regarded as unclean the blood of the covenant by which he was sanctified,
and has insulted the Spirit of grace?” (Heb 10:29).
        The moral and theological apostasy that Joe and Sue have committed
will not be without tragic consequences—both temporally or eternally. Even
so, those consequences cannot nullify their perfection which they were
granted eternally in 10:14. They will be both perfected and punished for all
eternity. The writer confirms both truths. Even after their apostasy, they are
still the Lord’s people (Heb 10:30). Their apostasy did not nullify their
perfected state—they still belong to the Lord as His people. The terrifying
results of their apostasy do not nullify the fact that they still belong to the
Lord in just the same way and to the same extent before they committed
apostasy as they do after they commit apostasy. Their relationship with the
Lord is permanent. The purpose of their judgment is not to see if they belong
to the Lord. That they are perfect in terms of belonging to the Lord has been
affirmed already by the writer of Hebrews. Rather, the purpose of the
judgment is to render rewards—both positive and negative—to those who
belong to the Lord by virtue of their permanent perfection in state. The
rewards will be based on their practice.
Page 96                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
                    Illustration 14. Distributive Perfective




       Summary
       OSAS has been shown grammatically viable by regarding the present
participle in 10:14b from any one of variable vantage points: (1) substantive,
(2) timeless, (3) simple distributive, (4) descriptive distributive, or
(5) descriptive distributive durative. NOSAS has been shown to have only
one aspectual hope for legitimacy: necessitative descriptive distributive
durative. This NOSAS option was found to be grammatically possible when
10:14b was considered in isolation. Nevertheless, this approach is
contextually improbable when considered in consultation with the passage
and syntactically impossible when conjoined with 10:14a. Those saints who
are progressing presently in their sanctification can have assurance of their
absolute perfection regardless as to whether they progress in the future. They
can have absolute assurance. That they can have an absolute assurance
(while they were being sanctified) that is not contingent on their progressing
in sanctification indicates that they could retain this assurance, even if they
ceased progressing in sanctification, since their assurance is not contingent
on their future sanctification.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                   Page 97

         Until the End
        The analysis thus far might give the false impression that a
necessitative distributive durative is, at most, a theoretical possibility when
used with a perfect tense. To counterbalance this potentially mistaken
assessment, considering a counterexample pertinent to the above discussion
would be well advised. In Heb 3:14, the first part of the verse again uses a
perfect aspect while the latter part of the verse denotes an ingressive aspect
(an aorist subjective aspect being stretched adverbially by telos): “For we
have become Christ’s partners, if we hold fast the beginning of our
assurance firm until the end” (TM).61
        Believers have become (z▬) partners with Christ and remain in that
state as long as they hold fast (£) their assurance. Telological perseverance
is required for continued misthological qualification.* Continuation in this
perfective state is conditioned on a parallel linear contingency ( ●▬   £). The
contingency runs parallel to the perfective state and is necessary to maintain
that state. Continuing to hold fast is necessary if one is to remain in the state
of being qualified for participation in Christ’s ruling house (Heb 3:6).
Temporally, the Lord rules in the faithful believer as His house.
Misthologically, the faithful believer remains qualified to be a member of
the ruling body of His house. Instead of being members of the House of
Representatives, such believers will be members of the House of Christ: co-
rulers with Christ.62
        Yet some conditionalists of the Calvinistic variety would treat this
verse as if the second part of the verse is merely descriptive rather than
necessitative (much less prescriptive). Perseverance is claimed, by some
Calvinists simply to describe, those who are truly elect. One problem with
such a perspective is that the text is conditional: The text says if. Contrary to
proveitism, the question is not whether the past action has occurred but
whether the present state continues. In order for it to continue, one must hold
fast. Arminians are correct in that aspect. What conditionalists on both sides
of the debate fail to realize is that the verse is misthologically necessitative
(so Marrowism), not soteriologically necessitative (contrary to
Arminianism), nor soteriologically descriptive (contrary to Calvinism).
Having Christ ruling in our hearts temporally and sharing rulership of His
house eternally are both conditioned on our holding fast His words
telologically. The text is strongly misthological.
        Additionally, unlike 10:14, which had forever in the first part of the
verse, the writer of Hebrews places the durative (£) remark—until the end
(mechri telos)—in the second half of the sentence in 3:14. Holding fast is
durative. We must continue holding fast if we are to remain in the state of
being a partaker. The benefit of being a partaker will cease if we cease to
*
    See “Telology” in The Outer Darkness.
Page 98                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
hold fast. The writer of Hebrews stresses this aspect: We are (i.e., continue
to be) Christ’s house if (i.e., on the condition that) we continue holding fast
our hope firm until the end (Heb 3:6). In Greek, the protasis of a conditional
sentence can be placed after the apodosis. Typical English order, though,
would be rendered as: (A) If we continue holding fast, then (B) we continue
to be Christ’s house: A ⇒ B.
        The writer of Hebrews uses a synonymous phrase in Heb 6:10-12,
where he exhorts his readers to show the same diligent working love (£)
until the end (achri telos) in order that (hina) they may inherit the object of
their misthological hope. Their loving work is a conditional necessity for the
eschatological realization of the misthological reward. The Scripture uses
this expression yet again: “And he who overcomes [£], that is, who keeps
[£] My works until the end, I will reward Him with authority over the
nations” (Rev 2:26; TM). The reward for holding fast until the end is a
reward in the end.* Telological perseverance results in misthological reward.
The adverbial phrase until the end stretches the distributive aspect of
overcome and keep so as to make them durative (£). Overcoming and
keeping are necessitative, not merely descriptive, since they result in a
reward. Rewards, in turn, are conditioned on the performance necessary to
acquire them. Consequently, this text provides an example of a necessitative
distributive durative aspect.
              Illustration 15. Necessitative Distributive Durative




*
   The kai in Rev 2:26 may be taken epexegetically (overcomer = keeper),
dualistically (overcomer-keeper), synchronously (overcomer/keeper), or jointly
(the overcomer of the world is the keeper of Christ’s commands). Epexegetically
stresses identity. Dualistically stresses sphere. Synchronously stresses time.
Jointly combines the various perspectives together. Those who pose a distinction
between the one who overcomers and the one who keeps are making a false
differentiation. One-in-the-same person is being pictured by both terms.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                 Page 99

       To the End
        Several passages of Scripture use a similar phrase to teach a similar
truth: “The one who endures to the end [eis telos] will be saved” (Mt 10:22;
24:13; Mk 13:13; TM): will be saved, not has been saved. The focus of
these texts is on attaining final misthological salvation, not on providing
evidence of initial soteriological salvation. Those Calvinists who treat these
texts as providing evidence of initial salvation are misconstruing the focal
point of these texts. These passages do not provide a litmus test by which to
determine who has received initial salvation as a gift. A final salvation
conditioned on perseverance is a reward, not a gift. Misthological salvation
is at stake.
        The phrase the one who endures is an articular participle denoting a
distributive aspect () that has been made durative (£) by the
adverbial phrase to the end. Endure is durative (£) for the individual and
distributive () for the group to which that individual belongs. Each
believer must continue to perform this action to achieve the envisioned
benefit at the end of their lives. In contrast to Heb 10:14, these passages
teach that the eternal benefit is yet to be realized fully and finally.

       Perfective Examples

       Jn 5:24
       •    Truly, truly, I say to you, he who hears My word, and believes
            Him who sent Me, has eternal life, and does not come into
            judgment, but has passed out of death into life.

       Conversely, several passages of Scripture use the articular participle
to denote completed action and resulting state in a manner analogous to
Heb 10:14. The benefit already has been bestowed perfectly and
permanently on the members of this class. This benefit is described by
forever in Heb 10:14 and by eternal in these texts: The one who believes has
eternal life (Jn 3:36; 5:24; 6:47; cp. 6:54a). As noted in Illustration 9, such
verses may be stated thus: “He who believes (z) in the Son has (▬ ) eternal
life”
Page 100                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
            Illustration 16. Punctiliar Action within Stative Result




To reiterate in a little more detail, the punctiliar action of saving faith lives
on (virtually so to speak) in the stated result, at least into the present, so that
the person would still be a (virtual) believer even if he or she ceases to
believe.* The action of belief (z) plus the resultant state of having eternal
life (▬) combines to produce a perfective aspect (z + ▬ = z▬ ). Or if one
wishes to restate the same aspectual reality and call attention to internal
punctiliarity (~), one could do so with:


                             x-—
                         ~+▬=~ ▬
        A particularly interesting clarification is added by the biblical text to
one of these verses mentioned above: has passed out of death into life
(Jn 5:24b). This addition of the perfect tense supplies a parallel to the
situation found in Heb 10:14. In both cases, there is a distribution of a
perfective aspect. These distributive perfects might be summarized this way:

    •   In Heb 10:14, those who () distributively are saints
        (z▬▬ ) have been permanently perfectively perfected.

    •   In Jn 5:24, those who () distributively are believers
        (z▬▬ ) have been perfectively enlivened with eternal life.

       These verses make a nice conceptual and aspectual fit. Those who are
distributively becoming saints are permanently perfected. Those who (as a
 class) are becoming believers (as z individuals) have life forever.
The moment a person joined this class by becoming a believer, that
individual was sanctified and perfected forever. Such perfection is static.

*

Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                Page 101
The moment a person believes, that individual is enlivened forever since
what he or she was enlivened with is eternal life. In terms of possession, the
passing from death into life is pictured as a static state with the perfect
aspect.
                Illustration 17. Static Perfection in Heb 10:14




         As individual members of the Bible study believe (represented by
the dot z ), he or she is perfected forever (z▬▬ ). The resulting state applies
for all time, eternally thereafter, for that individual. Aspectually, this is
represented by the solid bar (▬▬ ) without an arrow at its end. The number
of individuals for whom this classification applies continues to increase
distributively () down through the corridors of time as more people
come to saving faith.
Page 102                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
                 Illustration 18. Static Possession in Jn 5:24




        In both verses, the action happens at a point in time for the individual
represented by the dot (z ) and the resulting state (▬▬) applies for all time,
eternally thereafter, for that individual. The number of individuals for whom
this classification applies continues to increase distributively as more people
come to saving faith, represented by multiple occurrences of the permanent
perfective symbol (z▬▬ ). The individual’s progression in faith is not in
view. By no means can the person’s progression in faith be made a condition
or necessitative description of the static benefit envisioned. These
individuals already have passed from one sphere into another, and that one-
way passage into that sphere cannot be undone since the resulting sphere is
eternal—applying forever thereafter.
        Conditionalists are fond of pointing to the present tense for believes
and has in these Johannine texts and asserting that the corresponding aspect
means: The one who continuously believes continuously has eternal life.
Their doing so demonstrates a considerable insensitivity to the distributive
perfective aspect. The distributive aspect also is frequently illustrated with a
dashed line (- - - - - -). So a dashed line will be used to further clarify the
distributive aspect for believes. The continuous aspect is not to be applied to
the individual but to the class to which that individual belongs. As more and
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                  Page 103
more people believe, the class of believers continues to grow. The intent of
the present tense is not to suggest that an individual must continue to believe
in order to continue to have eternal life. The phrase has eternal life in
Jn 5:24a is not describing an action but a state. This perfective aspect is
clarified in Jn5:24b where it is paired with perfect tense: has passed into life.
                 Illustration 19. Present Has = Perfective Has




        The arrow belongs to the slanted dashed line (- - - - - ->) showing
action, not to the horizontal solid line (▬▬) showing resulting state. In the
punctiliar moment (z) a person becomes a believer (showing action), that
individual comes into the possession of eternal life and passes into the
sphere of this life (as the resulting state). Thereafter, that person’s presence
in the sphere of life and that person’s possession of that life is a resulting
static state. The ongoing slanted distributive action is composed of numerous
punctiliar actions as each individual comes to saving faith. In summary, the
dashes and dots represent actions; the solid lines represent resulting states.
The action is ongoing in terms of the dashes (- - - - - ->) representing the
distributive group but punctiliar in terms of the dots (z) representing the
individuals who become members of that group. The thought is not that as
long as a person continues actively to be a believer, that person retains
possession of eternal life. Rather, after a person becomes a believer, that
person remains in possession of eternal life. Continuing to believe would not
Page 104                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
be necessary to remain in a state of having eternal life. Having eternal life is
a result of having believed, not a result of continuing to believe. In reference
to the necessitative aspect for the individual, the distribution is punctiliar.
Therefore, it would be denoted with distributive dots: . Unlike
Heb 3:14, these texts state no remaining if for the one who has entered this
Johannine state.

       1Jn 5:1
       Calvinistic Ordo
          Other than Jn 5:24, the combination of a present participle for
believe with a positive perfective state is found one other time in Johannine
literature: “Everyone who believes that Jesus is the Christ has been born
[z▬ ] of God” (1Jn 5:1; TM). Most translations simply focus on the state by
rendering the phrase as is born of God (▬ ). Some Calvinists attempt to find
support for their notion that regeneration precedes faith by stressing the
perfective aspect. In their opinion, John is saying that everyone who believes
has been born of God already before the person believes. After all, John
uses a present participle for believe in conjunction with a perfective state in
Jn 3:18 where the perfective state appears to precede the action conveyed by
the participle: “He who does not believe has been judged already.”

       Already
          One difficulty with concurring with this Calvinistic ordo salutis in
1Jn 5:1, however, is that in 3:18 of his gospel, John added the word already
(ede). Contrastively, in 1Jn 5:1, he omits it. Apparently, John did not regard
the perfect tense as necessarily conveying antecedent action in this type of
syntactical arrangement, so he added it to perform this function in Jn 3:18.
Additionally, the only other time John uses the present participle for believe
with a negative perfective would not allow the meaning of already: “The
one who [a] does not believe God [b] has made Him a liar, [c] because he
has not believed in the witness that God has borne concerning His Son”
(1Jn 5:10). Surely, John does not mean that a disbeliever makes God out to
be a liar before the disbeliever disbelieves! It is the disbeliever’s disbelief
that makes God out to be a liar. The perfective state of making God out to be
a liar occurs after the disbeliever’s disbelief, not before it—as the becausal
part of the verse makes clear.
          The unbeliever’s state of disbelief puts the unbeliever in a state (▬)
of having made God to be a liar: The action in part a logically comes before
the perfective state in part b as explained by part c. If Jn 3:18 is considered
in its entirety, then a similar observation is valid there as well: “He who
[a] does not believe [b] has been judged already [c] because he has not
believed.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                  Page 105
         The action conveyed by the present participle in the first part of the
verse logically comes before the perfective state expressed in the middle part
of the verse, as the last part of the verse clarifies. In other words, part a
comes before part b as explained by part c. The unbeliever’s state of
unbelief (in part a) puts him or her in a state of judgment (in part b). In both
cases, the action portrayed by the present participle logically precedes the
state described by the perfect tense.

       Johannine Ordo
         Calvinists, in turn, might object that since the becausal clarifier is
missing in 1Jn 5:1, that it would be improper to consider these verses as
syntactically parallel to Jn 5:24. They might try to turn the tables by
remarking that John does not regard the perfect tense as necessarily
conveying a subsequent state in this type of syntactical arrangement since he
adds the becausal clarifiers when a subsequent state is intended.
Notwithstanding, the fact remains that whenever John uses a present
participle for believe in conjunction with a resulting perfective state, the
action represented by that participle logically precedes the state. Even
though Jn 5:24 does not have the becausal clarifier, faith still is placed
before coming into possession of life and, therefore, before the state of
having life. Since the state of having life conceptually is parallel with being
born of God, Jn 5:24 provides the Johannine ordo salutis: Faith precedes
coming into life. So faith precedes being born of God. Moreover, as proven
by Jn 3:18, even if John had included the word already in 1Jn 5:1, it would
not necessarily convey the notion that birth precedes faith. In Jn 3:18,
already only means in the present time. It does not refer to antecedent time.
John is not saying that unbelievers are judged as such before they disbelieve.

       Stative
         Both Jn 5:24 and 1Jn 5:1 are distributives: . Whoever at any
time believes has passed into (a state of) life, described in the latter verse as
being born of God. Arminians insist on linear contingency (£) in the first
verse. But the parallelism with 1Jn 5:1 shows the inconsistency of such
insistency. One does not have to believe continually in order to be in a state
of being born of God. Being born of God is not an ongoing action. This
verse is not describing linear regeneration. The perfect tense is used. The
believer has been born of God (z▬ ). The action of the new birth was
completed when the individual came to saving faith. Now, the believer is in
a resultant state (▬ ) of having been born of God. Arminians are quick to
point out that they do not believe that a believer can be unborn of God.
Good. Yet they think that a believer can die spiritually and lose the free gift
of eternal life. To the contrary, John has placed the believer in a state that
cannot be undone. Being born of God cannot be conditioned on linear
Page 106                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
contingency. Furthermore, since a believer cannot be unborn, Arminianism
puts the apostate believer in the untenable position of being born of God but
not having eternal life. Impossible!
          Neither Jn 5:24 nor 1Jn 5:1 are using the present participle for
believe to denote ongoing belief on the part of the individual. Rather, both
verses simply are using the verb as a substantive (i.e., as a noun) for
believer. Incidentally, this may be one reason that the perfect tense does not
apply antecedently to this construct.63 Whereas the action of the perfect
tense may at times be antecedent to the action of the main verb, believe is
not being used as a verb but as a noun: believer. Therefore, the action
expressed by the perfective birth would not be antecedent to substantive
believer. One is not born of God before one becomes a believer. The entire
Calvinistic argument that the state of birth comes before the action of faith
misstates the case. A believer is not an action but a person.
          Moreover, even if one insists on the verbal nature of believe
(pisteuo), it is only distributive: . The antecedent argument certainly
fails to apply to the distributive group. Berry was not born of God before Joe
became a believer! John simply is saying that believers (the plural is used
herein to denote the distributive in this case) have passed into a state of
having eternal life and being born of God. Neither having eternal life nor
being born of God is a linear action (£). They are states (▬).
          The very moment a person becomes a believer (z), that person
instantaneously passes into the possession and sphere of life and thus into a
born again state (z▬ ). The believer is pictured on top and the resulting state
into which he or she simultaneously enters is depicted on the bottom in this
joint representation (●●▬). Arminians are engaging in utter folly in trying to
turn this construct into a linear contingency in which one has to continue to
                                                       ▬
believe in order to remain in a born-again state (●▬    £). Calvinism is even

worse in trying to make the linear contingency the result of the new birth
( ●▬
   £). As shown elsewhere, Calvinism cannot make progressive
sanctification the necessary result of new birth without making the retention
of new birth conditioned on progressive sanctification.
          These present participles with perfective state provide solid ground
for unconditional security. These present participles do not denote linear
contingency in these soteric verses. Granted, the perfect tense itself does not
prove that the state is permanent. The permanent nature of the state is
supplied by the context, sometimes by the immediate context. In fact,
sometimes that context is so immediate so as to be another word in the same
sentence. Indeed, in Heb 10:14, the state is affirmed to be forever (NKJ). In
Jn 5:24, the state is confirmed to be eternal. In Jn 5:1, the state is that of
being born of God. Again, even Arminians admit that one cannot be unborn.
Securitists are right to maintain that the perfective state in such cases must
be permanent and cannot be undone (●●▬▬).

       Eph 2:8-9
        •   For by grace you have been saved through faith; and that not of
            yourselves, it is the gift of God; not as a result of works, that no

       The periphrastic perfect in Eph 2:8-9 provides a relevant example that
has applicability to Jn 5:24 as well.* Some translations render this phrase in
Eph 2:8-9 as you are saved (▬), emphasizing the present resulting state (like
Jn 5:24a) while others render it as you have been saved (z▬ ), emphasizing
the perfective nature of the resulting state (like Jn 5:24b). Both types of
translations are correct. However, rendering the verse as if it said you are
being saved, or you are continuing to be saved, or you are continuously
saved (£) would be a blatant blunder. Similarly, twisting Jn 5:24 into any
such meaning would be equally erroneous. Whereas Eph 2:8-9 uses the
designations gift and not as a result of works to show that resulting state is
perpetual, Jn 5:24 uses the adjective eternal.
                        Illustration 20. Have Been & Are




      If someone gave me an eternal wallet, it might be stolen. If someone
gave me an eternal set of car keys, I might eventually lose them. If someone

gave me an eternal cell phone, I might misplace it. In contrast, eternal life
that already has been received is one thing that cannot be lost or stolen.
Losing eternal life, much less eternal life as a free gift, is intrinsically
impossible. Likewise, being granted such a salvation, that in its reception
and continued possession is not the result of works, cannot be lost on the
basis of works.
        Some Arminians attempt to counter by saying that we do not actually
have eternal life or salvation yet. Salvation is said to be a pilgrim’s process
and that the believer could turn away from the process anytime along the
way, even at the gates of heaven itself. Eternal life is said to reside strictly in
Christ rather than in us. Accordingly, if Christ were to leave us, we would
lose eternal life. Counter wise, though, the Bible says that this life has been
imparted to us via regeneration. We actually have it. This life is intrinsic to
our new nature. The proper conclusion, therefore, would be that since we
intrinsically have eternal life in Jesus, Christ will never leave us. In terms of
intrinsically possessing eternal life, Christ is the safe in whom we are placed.
        Aspectually, the past action of being saved (when the believer first
came to saving faith) was apart from works. Just as importantly, Paul further
clarifies that the resulting state of this salvation is also apart from works.
Those conditionalists who say that believers initially are saved by faith but
subsequently are kept saved by works have completely missed the
significance of the perfective aspect. Reception and retention of this
dimension of salvation is apart from works. Entrance into this state of
salvation was by faith apart form works (ergon), and continuation in this
soteric state continues apart from ergonomic considerations.
                    Illustration 21. Reception and Retention

                                Reception                    Retention
 Through Faith                                                 
Apart From Works                                               
        Contrary to conditionalists, Paul is not saying that we remain in a
saved state through continuous believing. Since perseverance in faith is a
work, remaining in this soteric state must be apart from perseverance in
faith. In this verse, the expression through faith is talking about the means of
experiencing the initial act of salvation—you have been saved, not you will
be saved, or even you are being saved—apart from works. Paul is not
making linear faith a necessary means of remaining in this state of salvation.
To recap in terms of the chart, the reception of this salvation is through faith.
The retention of this salvation is not conditioned on the continuation of that
initial faith. Neither the reception nor retention of this dimension of
salvation is by works.

     Eternal




        In soteric texts (such as Heb 10:14 and Jn 5:24), the emphasis is on
the enduring state, not the enduring faith. The benefit is applied initially to
an individual at the point in time that individual comes to faith and
unconditionally remains applicable thereafter. In mistholic passages (such as
Heb 3:14 and Mk 13:13), on the other hand, the emphasis is on the enduring
faith. In Heb 3:14, this enduring faith allows one to stay in the enduring state
until the end.* In Mk 13:13, enduring faith allows one to attain salvation in
the end. This final salvation is mistholic, not soteric.

       In the soteric passages, you turn the key to the safe. Once you do so,
the Lord opens the door, places you in the safe, closes the door, and locks it.
No key exists to open this door from the inside. The only person who could
take you out of this safe state is the Lord. Emphatically, however, He has
promised not to do so (Jn 10:28) by promising you eternal life and
permanent perfection the moment you initially turn the key. At the punctiliar
moment of faith, you are permanently locked into this resulting state (●●▬▬).
In mistholic passages, in contrast, the key is not punctiliar faith but
persevering faith. Punctiliar faith is presumed as the starting point in the
mistholic passages because only believers can earn the envisioned rewards.
Yet in mistholic texts the emphasis is on the necessity of subsequently

       Conceptual Perfects
         In the Johannine texts considered above (Jn 3:36; 5:24; 6:47), the
present tense of has in the expression the one who believes has eternal life
has been found to have a perfective aspect even though the perfect tense is
not used in this phrase. The perfective aspect is supplied by the concept of
having eternal life and is provided by the contextual clarifier in Jn 5:24b.
Not all conceptual perfects need to be stated in the perfect tense. For that
matter, they do not necessarily require a verb. In Mk 9:43 (cp. Mt 18:8),
Jesus uses an adjective to denote the perfective aspect: “And if your hand
causes you to stumble, cut it off; it is better for you to enter life crippled,
than having your two hands, to go into hell, into the unquenchable fire.”
Crippled is an adjective, yet it describes the resulting state of the verb cut.

                      Illustration 24. Cut then Crippled




*
  In this case, subsequently durative aspect is being pictured below and to the
right of the original state to show contingency ( ●▬£). However, a subsequent
descriptive aspect would not convey contingency (●▬ ¤).
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                 Page 111


         In this case, the dot (z) would be used to denote the aspect
conveyed by cut, and the bar would be used for the resulting perfective state
(▬ ) described by crippled. The resulting perfective state of the verb is
conveyed by the adjective. Conditionalists would at least use a long bar (if
not an arrow) and insist that the cutting must last until the end of one’s life.
They make this insistence in order to make this crippling sacrifice a
theological necessity for final salvation from hell at the end of one’s life.
Such insistence is clearly open to question. Certainly, Jesus ideally would
intend for the resulting state to last until the end of one’s life. But does He
make this ideal response a necessity? Even aside from the consideration of
theological versus psychological necessity, is Jesus speaking ideally or
necessitatively of a state that lasts until the end of one’s life? Harmonization
with what Jesus has said elsewhere with what He says here leads to the
conclusion that He does not necessarily imply that the state of being a
crippled necessitatively must be stretched until the end of one’s life. Ideally,
yes; necessitatively, no. After all, Jesus implies this conclusion when He
refers to what is necessary to enter life.
         The verb for enter (eiserchomai) is in the aorist tense and would be
represented by a dot as well. Aspectologists point out that an aorist may look
like a dot on the outside but the inside may be unspecified ({). For sake of
argument, I will adopt this understanding for cut. Enter is a horse of a
different color, however. Since saving faith immediately results in the
impartation of eternal life via regeneration, enter must be illustrated with a
dot on the inside to denote its internal aspect (~). In computer terms, this
aspect is WYSIWYG: What You See Is What You Get. It looks punctiliar on
the outside and that is what you get on the inside. To restate the verse in
terms of its various aspects, it would look like this: “If your hand keeps
causing you to stumble (£), then cut ({) it off. It is better to enter (~) life
crippled (▬ ), than having your two hands, to go (~) into hell” (TM). Like
entrance into hell, entrance into life is punctiliar. The question is, “How long
must one keep cutting?”
         When I was a child, I used to have a large, dangling wart on the
back of my right knee. One of my uncles kept teasing me about it, saying
that I should cut it off. Knowing that it would be a painful procedure, I kept
ignoring his advice. However, on one occasion, when my parents would be
gone from the house for most of the afternoon, I finally decided to perform
this minor surgery myself. I got my dad’s straight razor and an ice cube and
went to the bathroom to perform what I hoped would be a relatively quick
procedure. I applied the ice, hoping to deaden the sensitivity of that area of
skin. Then I tried to cut off the wart. I had completely overestimated the
ability of the ice to numb that wart and woefully underestimated how much
it would hurt! I could not make anything more than a tiny slice before
recoiling from the pain. Then I would reapply the ice, make another attempt,
Page 112                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
recoil, regather my resolve, reapply the ice, and try to slice again. It must
have taken an hour to finally saw off that wart. When my mother came home
and found the bloody razor in the bathroom, she was aghast at what had
happened. I told her that I had cut off the wart. If I had been speaking in
Greek at the time, I might have used an aorist tense to summarize the event
by saying, “I cut off ({) the wart,” but internally I certainly would have
been thinking of linear activity ({¤ ). Thankfully, my wart did not grow back.

As a child, I was afraid it might.
         What if the individual has something keeping him or her from
coming to saving faith, and this potential convert fears not having the
resolve to cut off this hindrance permanently or fears that this obstacle might
come back subsequently, must such a person live in fear of losing the gift of
eternal life? No. Naturally, if something is preventing an individual from
coming to saving faith, that individual needs to cut off that activity,
regardless of however long it may take to cut it off. But what if that activity
shows a propensity to come back later? Must one keep cutting it off in order
to keep eternal life? No! Jesus is talking about what such a person must
do to enter into life, not keep eternal life! Present entrance, not an
eschatological entrance, is the topic of discussion. In accordance with the
Lord’s usual emphasis, He is referring to initial entrance, not final entrance.*
Conditionalists who insist that believers must keep cutting out things in their
lives that are preventing them from attaining final entrance have missed
Jesus’ point.
         Suppose that Joe had an addiction to Internet pornography that was
preventing him from coming to saving faith. He determined to give up
Internet access so that with a clean conscience he could trust in Jesus to
forgive him of his sins. To be sure, Joe knew that forgiveness was free and
did not require his giving up porn. He simply was unwilling to come to
Christ for forgiveness without relinquishing everything for which he was
seeking forgiveness. It just did not seem right to trust in Christ for
forgiveness for something for which he was unwilling to stop doing. So he
spent an hour figuring out how to delete the software interface for the
Internet connection on his laptop. Then, having taken care of business, he
was ready to get down to business. He got down on his knees and prayed to
Jesus, trusting in Christ for forgiveness and for the free gift of eternal life.
This conversion took place the first month of the Bible study. Not
surprisingly, Joe quickly found out that not having Internet access made it

as earning future entry as a reward. What is given as a gift will not be taken
away and turned into a reward (Rom 11:29).
