very difficult for him to keep up with important school announcements and
perform classroom assignments.
         Some at the Bible study were teaching that since Joe was a new
creature in Christ (2Cor 5:17), sin would no longer hold dominion over him
(Rom 6:6). He could no longer be captivated by sin like he was in the past.
Falling into the practice of old sinful habits would be impossible now that he
was a new creature in Christ (1Jn 3:9). God would make him stand
(Rom 14:4). With this new-found confidence in mind, Joe went to the school
IT department and had his Internet access restored. He figured he could trust
Christ to give him the will power to avoid the porn while doing his school
assignments. This worked until the weekend came. Then he made one slip.
One slip led to another. His old porn habit returned with a vengeance. It
seemed like it was even worse now that he was a Christian than before.
         To be sure, at least now that he was a Christian, he was fighting the
porn, rather than merely sitting back and enjoying it. Still, the only thing he
really could tell for sure that was different, as far as his experience was
concerned anyway, was that now he felt guilty. The only difference he could
verify was that Christianity made him feel guilty. He had been told that the
only way to prove that Christianity is true was to try Christ. Well, he had
tried Christ, but things were not working out very well. Hoping to get some
help, he started dating Sue, a member of the Bible study. Morally, things got
worse. Joe and Sue became involved sexually during the Christmas break.
The guilt and shame did not help motivate either of them to return to the
Bible study the second semester. Joe began to wonder what was the point of
returning to the Bible study if all he actually was getting was a guilt trip. By
the end of the school year, Joe went from being agnostic about his adventure
in Christ to being antagonistic. He had tried Christ, found Christianity to be
a lie, and he was not afraid to say so, lashing out at the pain caused by this
new-found religious guilt. Things did not end well. Joe was killed in a car
wreck that summer while driving drunk. Did he go to hell?
         Conditionalists will say that Joe went to hell. The text, however,
suggests a different answer. Joe had cut off the thing preventing him from
coming to saving faith long enough to believe in Christ alone for eternal life.
By definition, such a response would have qualified as saving faith. It would
have met the requirement laid out by Jesus in Mk 9:43 for entrance into life.
By dealing with the matter that was preventing him from trusting in Christ
for eternal life, and by then trusting in Christ for eternal life as a free gift,
Joe had met the requirements laid out in both Mk 9:43 and Jn 5:24 for
coming into the state of being vivified with eternal life. His crippled state
did not need to last long for Joe to come into that eternal state, just long
enough for Joe to believe in Jesus for eternal life. Nor did this crippled state
have to last anytime thereafter for him to remain in possession of eternal life.
After all, it is eternal life (not a commodity that can be given and then
subsequently taken away) that Joe had received. Furthermore, Jesus was
Page 114                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
laying out the necessity for entrance into life, not for keeping such life. The
moment Joe became a believer he came into permanent, ongoing possession
of this life. His state of being a crippled ceased, but his state of being a
possessor of eternal life could not cease. Joe’s vivacity must be affirmed, or
Christ’s veracity must be denied.
          Granted, Joe’s last state as an apostate was morally worse than his
previous state as an unbeliever. Moreover, his last state as an apostate had
become worse misthologically than his previous state as a new believer (cp.
2Pet 2:20). Nevertheless, the implications of Mk 9:43 and Jn 5:24 are that he
died in a saved state—in regards to heaven versus hell. True to His word,
Jesus will not place Joe in a judgment where the issue is life versus death in
terms of having eternal life versus going to hell. Joe’s possession (much less
retention) of eternal life was never conditioned on Joe’s works. Cutting off
the Internet was a necessary psychological precondition to Joe’s coming to
faith. However, it was never a co-condition (much less postcondition) for the
free gift of eternal life.
                      Illustration 25. Crippled Believer




         Looking back at the aftermath of what happened to Joe, Sam was
saddened by Joe’s death, even though Sam had become a believer in the
third month of the Bible study and was alarmed by Joe’s moral and
theological apostasy. Sam had been praying for Joe and trying to figure out
what went wrong. Along the way, Sam discovered that apologetics provide a
better anchorage for one’s faith than one’s personal experience.
         The wiser course of action is to ground one’s faith objectively in the
Bible rather than subjectively in one’s experience. The experimental
approach, Sam discovered (unfortunately too late to help Joe) is seriously
flawed because, among other reasons, it fails to take into account that while
the believer is trying out Jesus, Jesus is trying out the believer. Admittedly,
God occasionally does invite believers to put Him to the test in certain
respects (Mal 3:10). Due caution must be exercised, however, because God
also warns that testing Him in certain aspects can be very dangerous
(Heb 3:9).
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                   Page 115
         Even more alarmingly, God will put believers to the test (Gen 22:1;
Jam 1:3; 1Pet 1:7; 4:12). He will even test their works at judgment to see if
believers are worthy of reward (1Cor 3:13). As for Joe, God was willing and
able to make him to stand (Rom 14:4). Expectation of a positive outcome
was warranted but not guaranteed unconditionally. God would not force Joe
to stand. Multiple exhortations to stand firm make it impossible to conclude
otherwise (1Cor 16:13; Eph 6:11, 13-14; Phil 4:1; 1Thess 3:8; 2Thess 2:15;
1Pet 5:12).
         Looking back at the event a few years later, Sam came to realize
that Joe would not be a part of Christ’s ruling house (Heb 3:6,14) because
Joe had not stood firm until the end. One day Sam hoped to be a member of
that ruling class and be able to visit Joe. As a result of Joe’s confinement in
the outer darkness, and thus his exclusion from the capital city, Joe would
not be able to visit Sam in the capital. Yet if Sam achieved ruler status, he
would be able to visit Joe during one of his regal rounds outside of the city.
Perhaps Sam could even take Joe flying.64

       Conclusion
        Both linear contingency and linear necessity have been found
impossible for an eternal benefit that already has been fully and freely
assured. Unconditional soteriological security is the biblical result.
Conversely, both linear contingency and linear necessity have been affirmed
in the cases of misthological benefits that are yet future. Rewards are
conditioned on present, ongoing performance—gifts are not. The distributive
aspect can be used in either capacity. The propensity should be to take
distributives simply as punctiliar for the individual unless context or
syntax suggests otherwise. Punctiliarity has certainly been affirmed in the
case of soteric texts. Ingressive, progressive, or durative aspects, however,
can be readily affirmed, even as necessary, if contextually justified—such is
the case of mistholic texts.
        The Arminian thesis that believers can lose the gift of eternal life has
been found seriously defective in some classic proof texts that have been
erroneously thought to convey such a notion. Cutting out things in one’s life
that prevent one from reaching heaven is better explained as dealing with
preconditions to coming into possession of eternal life rather than as co-
conditions or postconditions for retaining one’s possession of eternal life as
a free gift.
        The Calvinistic perception that the way one lives proves whether one
is saved has been countered by the argument that fruit is prescriptive, not
merely descriptive. Fruit is necessary. Therefore, the final outcome is
conditioned directly on the fruit, not merely on the root. In soteric texts, this
fruit can be properly identified as saving faith. The plurality of this punctiliar
response is due to its being distributed over a group of people producing this
response. Saving faith is not a linear condition for individuals composing
Page 116                                Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
this group. The punctiliar requirement is distributed over a class of people
and met by the singular response of saving faith on the individual part of
each participant in that soteric class.
        The verbal aspect of the Greek verbal system is well suited to
demonstrating this theological aspect of saving faith. The distributive aspect
allows the possibility of many individuals performing the necessary singular
punctiliar action to belong to this increasingly large class of individuals.
Hence, one can explain how Jesus is able to sanctify once and for all those
coming into this class. As the number of individuals coming into this class
increases, more dots (z z z z z z) are added to the representation of that
class. Entrance into this class is once and for all. Once one has entered into
this class, it is impossible to leave this class. Classification is static for the
individual who has entered this class but dynamic as more individuals join
this class.
        Although the simple distributive ()* suffices as the least
technical and most probable distributive explanation (in terms of Occam’s
razor) for the aspect being affirmed in classic texts such as Heb 10:14, more
sophisticated possibilities were explored in order to evaluate the plausibility
of a conditionalistic interpretation.
        The next most likely approach would be to consider the possibility of
a descriptive distributive† in which the dots within the distributive are really
perceived to be short, non-emphatic arrows (→) when magnified under
closer scrutiny. Even if this perspective is adopted, the linear activity cannot
be made necessitative even in this soteric text. Moreover, it is probable that
the supposed internal linear aspect actually gives way to simply being a
temporal marker—denoting a punctiliar action happening in the present
moment so that present tense (time) rather than linear aspect (action) for the
given individual who is becoming a member of that class is truly what is
being affirmed. Aspectually, this latter approach reduces to  with the
alleged arrow only pointing to the central dot. In terms of soteriological
necessity, the same would be true of the reputed non-emphatic linearity.
Thus, while the descriptive distributive may hold interest for the
aspectologist, the laity may be excused if they see it as bleeding to death
from needless complexity.
        Even if the former approach is able to avoid Occam’s razor in
specialized studies, it is very difficult to believe that needless complexity has
not been introduced when one considers the improbability of the durative
distributive in which the linear aspect (£) is perceived to be distributed
across the members of the class. The question that must still be addressed is,
“In what manner is the linear aspect distributed?” Is it merely descriptive or

*
    See Illustration 10. Distributive Distribution of Perfection, 87.
†
    See Illustration 11. Descriptive Distributive, 89.
Chapter 2. Not Fallen from Perfection                                   Page 117
is it prescriptive? In other words, does the writer of Hebrews assert the
necessity of persevering in order to be perfected forever? No. The permanent
perfection already has been granted by the biblical writer to everyone who
has entered into this class of those who are being sanctified upon their initial
entry into this class. They already have been granted permanent perfection
(z▬▬ ) regardless as to how long their linear (£) sanctification continues.
Hence, linear sanctification cannot be the condition for permanent
perfection.
        Soteriologically speaking, the durative distributive in Heb 10:14
basically reduces to a descriptive durative in that the pronounced gap—
between the amount of time one must be sanctified versus the perfection
which is specified to last forever—logically could be expanded for any given
individual so that however briefly his or her sanctification might last (even if
only for one second) it would still qualify for permanent perfection. In the
highly unlikely event that the durative is intended in Heb 10:14, it collapses
into a descriptive distributive durative and thus to .
        Soteriologists may join hands with aspectologists to affirm that the
static perfective state (in a soteric text such as Heb 10:14) is distributed
dynamically to individuals at the point in time they individually come to
saving faith (●●▬▬). Unconditional security is thus affirmed aspectually.
Conversely, the FG misthologist is free to stress simultaneously that (in a
mistholic text such as Heb 3:14) the distribution of the crown of life is
distributed contingently to those believers seeking to remain in a qualified
state for the attainment of this reward by linearly holding fast the faith ( ●▬
                                                                              £).
        Alternatively, soteriologists are on firm footing if they choose simply
to regard the articular present participle in a text such as Heb 10:14 as a
substantive. Many translations so render it: “For by one offering He has
perfected for all time those who are sanctified.” Indeed, the articular present
participle in the related text of Heb 2:11 may be similarly rendered: “For
both He who sanctifies and those who are sanctified are all from one Father;
for which reason He is not ashamed to call them brethren.” Linear activity is
not being stressed. Substantively, the verse is saying, “For both the
Sanctifier and the sanctified are…brethren.” These texts do not need to be
translated as referring to those who are being sanctified. They already are
sanctified. Their status as brethren has been granted already.
        That a contextual static status is intended is acknowledged by English
translations two verses later. The aorist of Heb 2:13 is rendered as a perfect.*
These brethren are referred to as the “children whom God has given Me.”
The writer of Hebrews is referring to the brethren who have been given. The
action is completed. They are not in the process of being given.

*
  This verse is an example of a perfective (stative) aorist. See Believe for other
such examples.
Page 118                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
Consequently, they are not in the process of being sanctified in regard to
qualifying as brethren or children. They already are given and already are
sanctified and hence are called brethren (of Jesus) and children (of God).
Linear contingency is ruled out. Indeed, even any distributive aspect is
rendered suspect. The substantive trumps even the simple distributive as
being the best means to convey the writer’s intent.* Those who have become
Christian brethren and God’s children already have been permanently
perfected once and for all in that state forever: Once sanctified always
sanctified (OSAS). The affirmation of OSAS in terms of once sanctified
always sanctified leads directly to an affirmation of OSAS in terms of once
saved always saved.
           Distributive Aspect




*
  The writer introduces the discussion of the believer’s sanctification in a
manner that leads one to conclude that punctiliar (and thus positional)
sanctification is in view (Heb 2:11). He concludes his epistle with punctiliar (z)
sanctification as well, using an aorist subjective in Heb 13:12.
                     Appendix 1.
            Perfection and Sanctification
        Progressive sanctification has been found to be very improbable in
Heb 10:14. Even if progressive sanctification were present in the verse, it
simply would be distributed across the class and, at the most, descriptive for
the individuals within that class, but certainly not necessitative. Much more
likely, however, the verse is referring simply to punctiliar sanctification or to
stative sanctification. For simplicity sake, a punctiliar distribution ()
may be used to describe everyone as sanctified who at anytime has believed
in Christ for soteric perfection. The point being made by the writer of
Hebrews is that such individuals are perfected forever: “He has [z▬▬ ]
perfected for all time those who are [z] sanctified” (Heb 10:14). Likewise,
they are sanctified [z▬▬ ] once for all time (Heb 10:10).
                        Illustration 26. Already Perfect




       Consequently, anyone who has been sanctified in this sense (at
anytime) is perfect in terms of that sanctified state for all time. This gift of
soteriological perfection and sanctification is permanently bestowed on
everyone who receives it. Specifically, the apostate individual referred to by
the writer a few verses later in Heb 10:29 is permanently perfected and
sanctified even though he has fallen from grace: “How much severer
punishment do you think he will deserve who has trampled under foot the
Son of God, and has regarded as unclean the blood of the covenant by which
he was [z] sanctified, and has insulted the Spirit of grace?” Progressing in
practical sanctification was not necessary for him to remain in the state of
perfection and sanctification that was affirmed of him a few verses earlier.
       Nevertheless, necessitative progressive sanctification is certainly
present a couple of chapters later: “Pursue peace with all men, and the
sanctification without which no one will see the Lord” (Heb 12:14).
Sanctification is required to see the Lord. Believers are actively (£) to
pursue this sanctification (along with peace) in the present time period so
Page 120                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
that they may qualify to see the Lord in a mistholic sense in the future.
Everyone will see the Lord in some sense, of course. After all, they are to be
judged by Him! However, not everyone will have unrestricted personal
access to Him.
       For example, it is one thing for the common citizen to say, “I saw the
president last night” versus the president’s child telling a friend at school, “I
had supper with Dad last night.” Both saw the president. Yet seeing him on
television giving a speech cannot be equated with sitting at the table with
him. Similarly, the experience of seeing Jesus by those in the outer darkness
cannot be equated with the experience of seeing Him by those believers who
reside in the New Jerusalem. Remote access cannot be equated with intimate
access. Intimate access is inferred in Heb 12:14. Such access is a reward for
those believers who progress and persevere in their sanctification.
                        Illustration 27. Not Yet Perfect




       This sanctification is to be pursued by those who already are
sanctified. Those who are sanctified in their position must pursue practical
sanctification if they misthologically are to see the Lord. In the same
fashion, those who already are perfect soteriologically are not yet perfect
misthologically (Heb 11:40). Paul pictures himself as running in a race,
trying to win misthological perfection: “Not that I have already obtained it
[misthological perfection], or have already become perfect, but I press on in
order that I may lay hold of that for which also I was laid hold of by Christ
Jesus” (Phil 3:12).* Such perfection is to be pursued as if running a race:

*
  The verb in each case for perfect is teleioo (Phil 3:12; Heb 10:14; 11:40).
Although those OT believes who died are not yet perfected misthologically
(Heb 11:40), they are perfected soteriologically (Heb 12:23). Teleioo is the verb
employed in the latter passage as well. Contrary to the opinion of some, the
perfection being run after by Paul in the context of Phil 3:12 is not present
perfection/maturity. He already had attained this temporal perfection/maturity
(teleios; Phil 3:15). Rather, he pursues eschatological/misthological perfection.
The writer of Hebrews, likewise, would affirm the temporal perfection/maturity
(teleios; Heb 5:14) of many of the OT saints (such as Abraham) described in
Heb 11. Their soteriological perfection is certainly a given (Heb 10:14; 12:23).
Nevertheless, they have not obtained the misthological perfection specified in
the conclusion of that chapter (Heb 11:40).
Appendix 1. Telological Perfection and Sanctification                    Page 121
“Brethren, I do not regard myself as having laid hold of it yet; but one thing
I do: forgetting what lies behind and reaching forward to what lies ahead, I
press on toward the goal for the prize of the upward call of God in Christ
Jesus” (Phil 3:13-14). Intimately knowing Christ as one of His co-rulers is
the prize to be won.
              Illustration 28. Already and Not Yet Misperception




      Conditionalists make a monstrosity out of the soteriological and
misthological aspects depicted in Illustration 26 and Illustration 27. They
combine them as if one necessarily entails the other so that if one does not
progress, one loses the gift or does not have the gift.
                     Illustration 29. Concurrent Perfection




       The more accurate representation is to acknowledge that two
simultaneous, yet distinct, truths are being affirmed within two different
contexts in the epistle of Hebrews. If (and only if) the expositor clearly
explains (as does the writer of Hebrews) that two different truths are being
signified, then the symbol ●▬   £ might be used profitably to combine
soteriology and misthology in a theologically permissible manner for the
advanced reader. Not only are two different aspects being discussed (in
terms of Greek aspectology), but two different dimensions are being
discussed contextually.*

*
  Alternatively, if Heb 10:14 is entertaining the possibility of a misthological
progression that is not contingent soteriologically, then in that case one does not
need to interject that two different dimensions are being discussed contextually.
Page 122                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
                  Illustration 30. Entrance versus Rulership




       As to the gift dimension pictured on top in the above illustration,
believers already have been sanctified permanently and perfected
unconditionally, in terms of being qualified to enter heaven freely. This
qualification puts them in the running (so to speak) to qualify for the costly
reward of kingdom rulership (i.e., misthological perfection) pictured in the
bottom part of the illustration.
       Since the top aspect pertains to the gift of kingdom entrance and the
bottom aspect relates to reward of kingdom rulership, picturing the top
aspect as entering through the kingdom gates helps clarify the distinction
between free kingdom entrance and costly kingdom rulership. Accordingly,
the top bar should be extended through the gates to match its symbol ( ●▬  £).
In order to be even more technically precise, however, an alternate symbol
(●▬
  ¤) is being used herein to denote the non-contingent nature of the
relationship. If one carefully notes the difference, one possibly might use
both symbols profitably. Although the subsequent action is a contingent
durative (£) for the crown, it is only non-contingent descriptive in relation
to the gates (→).


Both dimensions would be present in the same context (even the same verse),
yet not in such a way as to denote mutual contingency. In other words, if the
writer is saying that those running in the race for the crown are perfected
permanently regardless as to whether they win the crown, then it is possible that
soteriology and misthology have been combined by the writer in one verse.
Although not taken as probable herein, the writer conceivably might be alluding
to progressive sanctification (cp. Heb 12:14) as a simple descriptive and
showing that those pursuing such sanctification are perfected permanently.
Because they already are perfected permanently by the blood of Christ (cp.
Heb 10:10), rather than by the sweat of their perseverance, their remaining
perfected forever cannot be contingent upon their sweaty perseverance.
Appendix 1. Telological Perfection and Sanctification                   Page 123
                        Illustration 31. Contingent State




        Contrastively, in a text such as Heb 3:14, the symbol ●▬     £ uses both
verbal aspects concurrently for misthology. At the punctiliar point in time
(z) a believer comes to saving faith, that individual becomes a potential heir
of the kingdom—a prospective ruler of the future kingdom. Believers
continue in that state (▬) of being qualified to be a ruling partner with
Christ in that coming kingdom by holding fast (£), or in Pauline terms, by
continuing to run the race until the end. Eschatological realization requires
successful completion of the race.
        A word of caution is necessarily in order. To be true to the authorial
intent of the epistolary writer, the interpreter must treat a mistholic text (such
as Heb 3:14) misthologically and a soteric text (such as Heb 10:14)
soteriologically. Combining these texts together so as to treat them as
mutually contingent mistholic-soteric (costly-free) texts is strictly
prohibited. Doing so not only would violate the writer’s distinction, it would
create confusion in the mind of the reader so that the gift would be
misconstrued as a reward or the reward would be misperceived as a gift. The
integrity of the soteriology, misthology, and aspectology of each distinctive
passage must be maintained. Even if the writer has allowed the combination
in Heb 10:14, at most he has done so only descriptively (●▬    ¤), not in terms of
mutually contingency ( ●▬  £).
        Nevertheless, the symbol ●▬   £ might be used in the hands of a careful
practitioner to illustrate unconditional soteriological security in conjunction
with conditional misthological security. Or it might be used simply to denote
misthological contingency. Biblically, it cannot be used to avow conditional
security of either the Arminian or Calvinistic variety. Using ●▬    £ to advocate
such conditional security is disavowed and is subject to a malpractice suit in
God’s court as a sloppy handling of the text.
Page 124                   Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection




           Abject Aspect
                       Appendix 2.
                    Boasting before God
       Introduction
      The supposition has been made herein that eternal life in Rom 2:7 may
be granted misthologically on the basis of perseverance in good works.
Eternal life will be rewarded to those who seek for it according to the
principles of God’s word. Of course, only believers have the potential to
earn eternal life in this capacity. Paul’s immediate point in this context is to
demonstrate the impossibility of earning eternal life legalistically.
Nevertheless, Paul does entertain, and even affirms, the possibility of
misthological justification as one proceeds through the epistle. Would not
misthological justification (which qualifies one for a reward from God)
entitle the one being misthologically justified to boast—even before God?

       Mistholic Justification before God
      The most natural reading of James’ affirmation of justification by
works in Jam 2:13-16 would be a misthological (i.e., Bema) justification
before God that results in the believer being shown mercy at the Bema. That
such a justification is before God is implicit in the Jacobean texts. Other
texts, however, make it explicit that believers (and only believers) can obtain
misthological vindication before God. Believers can be justified by works
before God; unbelievers cannot be justified by works before God. Likewise,
believers can be justified by words; unbelievers cannot be justified by words
(Mt 12:37). Misthological justification is only possible for believers.
Believers either will be confessed or denied misthologically before the
Father (Mt 10:32-33; Rev 3:5). Paul is concerned with how believers will be
presented before God (Col 1:22).

       Justification by the Law before God
      Granted, Rom 2:13 would seem to be a problem for this point of view
since it says that the doers (rather than the hearers) of the law are justified
before God. Even so, Paul proceeds to demonstrate that no flesh is justified
before God on the basis of the law (Rom 3:20; Gal 3:11). Thus, in harmony
with Jam 2:10 and Gal 3:10, perfect obedience to the law may be viewed as
necessary for justification before God—when being justified by the law—
which is impossible. Consequently, the purpose of the law is not to show us
how to earn justification before God but to show us the necessity of being
justified by faith before God (Gal 3:24). Because of these considerations, the
Page 126                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
standard deduction is that justification by works before God is impossible
(Rom 4:2), and that those who pursue justification on such basis fail to attain
it (Rom 9:22).

       Reward or Gift
      One problem with this approach is that it fails to take into account that
Paul simultaneously is affirming a justification by works within this very
epistle (Rom 6:16). Just as eternal life can either be a reward (Rom 6:22) or
a gift (Rom 6:23), so justification can be a reward or a gift. The reward of
eternal life (which is conditioned on works in Rom 2:6-7) cannot be attained
by those who seek a sinless standing before God on the basis of works
(Rom 4:7-8). Paul urges those who have found soteriological justification by
faith to pursue misthological justification by works (Rom 6:16) and
misthological life (Rom 6:22) and misthological glory (Rom 8:17b).

       Misthological Justification
      Even Lopez (who seeks to discredit viewing eternal life as a reward in
Rom 2:6-7) acknowledges that eternal life is a reward in Rom 6:22.65
Moreover, although he stresses the present aspect of this reward in harmony
with his trying to discredit taking dikaiosune as referring to justification in
Rom 6:16 (in favor of present “moral righteousness”), he implicitly admits
that not all the reward benefits of eternal life in the passage are reaped in the
present.66 They extend telologically (telos) into misthology (Rom 6:22).
      Dillow shares the view taken herein that Rom 2:7 includes a promise of
eternal life as a reward to believers who work for it, and he takes Rom 6:22
in the same fashion, along with passages like Gal 6:8; Jn 12:25-26;
Mt 19:29.67 Only those who are sinless can hope to please God on the basis
of their works. For sinners to point to their works and ask God to give them
what they deserve would be disastrous. Their works would condemn them
before God. In contrast, because believers have been forgiven of all their
sins soteriologically, they may point to their Spirit-enabled works as a basis
for positive reward misthologically.
      Unbelievers who seek a relationship with God on the basis of their
works will be rejected. God’s children, on the other hand, have a relationship
with God by faith and are encouraged to seek misthological justification by
works before God at His judgment seat. In fact, their works will be their
only hope for misthological justification before God when believers stand
before Him at His bema (Rom 14:10-12; 2Cor 5:10). The point of the matter
in both Rom 2 and Jam 2 is that misthological justification presupposes
soteriological justification. How can those who (because of their sinful
works) have no hope of entering heaven hope to earn rewards in heaven on
the basis of their works? They have no hope. Justification by faith before
God must proceed justification by works before God.
Appendix 2. Boasting before God                                       Page 127

         Mature Faith
       Contextually, Paul evidently can envision a misthological justification
before God based on mature faith.* Although this misthological justification
would give Abraham grounds for boasting before God in the mistholic
sense, it does not give him grounds for boasting in the soteric arena. In
Rom 4:2, Paul can conceive of Abraham being justified by works and having
grounds for boasting (implicitly even before God in a mistholic context).
However, in a context in which righteousness is being imputed
soteriologically to Abraham, on the basis of simple faith (such as in
Rom 4:3), justification by works (and hence any boasting before God) is
ruled out. Yet Paul implicitly leaves the door open to misthological boasting
before God.
       In contexts focusing on misthological justification, the possibility of
boasting before God is affirmed implicitly. Abraham was justified by simple
faith before God at least thirty years before he was justified before men by a
mature faith that expressed itself in the action that resulted in him being
called the friend of God—by both God and men (cf. Is 41:8; 2Chron 20:7
respectively).† James comes very close to explicitly affirming misthological
boasting before God (Jam 2:21-23). His statements lead logically to the
conclusion that such boasting (if that is what one is to call it) is permissible
under certain conditions.

         Implicit Boasting before God
       Lopez admits that “Paul does not condemn boasting in an absolute
sense, nor does he regard all forms of boasting as sinful….But when it
comes to legal [i.e., soteric] justification in declaring a sinner righteous,
there was no ground of boasting before God.”68 Well said. However, would
not such a statement imply that in some non-legal [i.e., non-soteric] sense,
grounds might be found for boasting even before God? Otherwise, why not
just say that there are never any grounds for boasting before God rather than
just deny such grounds in specific situations? Yet Lopez goes on to remark:
“Paul implies another type of justification, by works, before men (Jas 2:21),
but not before God” (emphasis his).69 What happened to Lopez’s implication
that there are some grounds on which such non-legalistic boasting before
God might not be sinful?

         Boldness before God
       Paul and FG writers are clearer elsewhere. Paul clearly affirms the
possibility of misthological blameless before God: “He has reconciled in the
*
    See “Before God” and “Misthological Boasting” in Carnal Corinth.
†
    See “Abraham’s Major Life Events” and “Before God or Men?” in Salvation.
Page 128                          Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
body of His flesh through death, to present you holy, and blameless, and
above reproach in His sight if indeed you continue in the faith firmly
established and steadfast” (Col 1:22-23, NKJ). Leach is right on target when
he concludes: “The phrase in His sight refers to one’s standing before the
Judgment Seat of Christ, the place where all believers will stand to be
recompensed for how they lived” (emphasis his).70 This phrase also may be
translated as before Him (katenopion): “He has now reconciled you in His
fleshly body through death, in order to present you holy and blameless and
beyond reproach before Him—if indeed you continue in your faith firmly
established and steadfast” (TM).
       Likewise, Jude uses this same Greek word to affirm that God is able
to keep believers from stumbling (if they will avail themselves of His
enablement) so that they may stand “faultless before the presence of His
glory with exceeding joy” (Jude 1:24). Both biblical writers are using the
same Greek word to denote the possibility of being able to stand in a
misthologically blameless manner before God at His Bema. Doskocil, the
FG commentator for Jude, aptly concludes that only faithful believers will
have this standing.71
       Similarly, Tanner understands Heb 11:2 as affirming that OT faithful
believers “obtained a good testimony in the eyes of God, thus gaining His
approval and meriting His rewards.”72 Indeed, faithful believers may have
misthological boldness before God at the Bema (1Tim 3:13; 1Jn 4:17), as is
noted by the FG commentators for these passages73 If one may have
misthological boldness, why not misthological boasting?

      Explicit Boasting before God
        At the very least, Scripture affirms the possibility of misthological
justification before God. Yet it seems to allow more. The faithful stewards
are portrayed by Jesus as boasting before Him when they stand before Him
at the Bema. They boldly urge their master to look at what they have
accomplished. They boast of what they have gained for their master
(Mt 25:20,22). Basically, when each of these faithful servants stands before
the Lord at judgment, they boldly and proudly are saying to their master,
“Look what I did!” Of course, they did not accomplish this gain apart from
their master’s enablement (representing Jesus’ enabling believers to serve
Him). After all, their parabolic master gave them the capital investment.
Nevertheless, these servants portray the misthological boldness described by
the epistolary writers.
        One might surmise that boasting before God is permitted when (1) it
is not being discussed in a soteriological context, (2) it does not entail
boasting of achievements accomplished independently of God’s gracious
enablement, (3) it does not forget the gracious nature of the opportunity to
earn rewards, and (4) it is not done to demean others.
Appendix 2. Boasting before God                                     Page 129

      Immediate Context
        Accordingly, Paul’s words might be better understood in context as
saying, “For if Abraham was justified by works, he has something to boast
about; but not before God regarding soteric justification. For what does the
Scripture say? ‘And Abraham believed God, and it was reckoned to him as
righteousness’” (Rom 4:2-3). As noted by the introductory for, verse three
explains the context in which the boasting of verse two would be a pretext,
but it does not rule out boasting before God in every context. After all, Paul
immediately goes on to say, “Now to the one who works, his wage is not
reckoned as a favor, but as what is owed [even by God]” (Rom 4:4). If
Abraham was justified by works (and he was, but not in a soteric sense),
then his reward is owed to him by God. Might not Abraham rightly boast of
his work before God if he were justified by works before God?
        Little children love to coming running up to their daddies saying,
“Daddy, Daddy, look what I did! Look at the picture I drew! Look at the
way I cleaned my room!” beaming with pride in their accomplishments
when they think their daddies would be pleased with what they have done.
Surely faithful believers can be depicted as running up to their Heavenly
Father with the same thrill and exclaiming, “Daddy, Daddy, look what I
did!” How else would one explain their boldness and not being ashamed of
what they have done for the Lord when they stand before the Lord? Faithful
believers need not cower in the corner. They need not be ashamed. In fact,
approved workers are not ashamed (2Tim 2:15). They will not be ashamed
of what they have done for their Lord when they stand before their Lord.
Will their Father look sternly down His nose and rebuke them for their
exuberance? Far from it! Daddy will take them up in His arms and exclaim,
“Well done!”
        Consider the alternative, as if Paul meant: “For if Abraham was
justified by works, he has something to boast about; but not before God
regarding being justified by works. For what does the Scripture say? ‘And
Abraham believed God, and it was reckoned to him as righteousness’”
(Rom 4:2-3). The mismatch is readily apparent. The explanatory for (gar)
makes little sense when it is made to apply to what would happen in the case
of mistholic justification before God. For in that case Abraham would have
justifiable grounds for boasting before God. Paul has abbreviated his
thought. The fuller implication of what he is saying would be: “For if
Abraham was justified by works in some other context, he has something to
boast about; but not before God in this context. For what does the Scripture
say? ‘And Abraham believed God, and it was reckoned to him as
righteousness’” (Rom 4:2-3).
Page 130                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

       Negative Boasting
       Fleshly (i.e., legalistic) boasting is ruled out (cp. Rom 4:1). Such
boasting is absolutely abhorrent. This type of boasting would be depicted by
a child running up his or her Daddy and exclaiming, “Look at the picture I
drew! Look at the way I cleaned my room! This makes you my Daddy!”
What a tragedy. Frontloading the gospel is an insult to God. Backloading is
no better: “Look at the picture I drew! Look at the way I cleaned my room!
This means you will stay my Daddy!” Proveitism is no better either: “Look
at the picture I drew! Look at the way I cleaned my room! This proves you
are my Daddy!” Perish the thought that God’s children have to look at their
performance in order to know whether God is their Daddy. Let not assurance
of paternal relationship be based on fleshly performance!

       Positive Boasting
        On the other hand, it is no affront to God when His children, who are
absolutely confident of their relationship with Him as their Daddy, come
running up to Him and eagerly exclaim, “Daddy, Daddy, look what I did!
Look at the way I cleaned my room! This means that you can take me for the
pony ride you promised if I cleaned up my room!” *
        Will their Father be displeased because they are eager to receive the
reward He had promised them? Will He be upset that they actually have the
audacity to think that they can please Him by their performance? Absolutely
not. Is such boasting sin? No.
        All fleshly, legalistic boasting is sin. Mistholic boasting is not sin.
Rather, it is encouraged. It simply goes by more positive names: boldness,
confidence, assurance, courage. Children are not to be encouraged to boast
about their works as a reason they are given presents by their parents (cp.
Eph 2:9). Nonetheless, children are not to be discouraged from being pleased
with their performance when seeking the rewards from their parents that
their parents have promised if their parents are pleased with their
performance. Paul should be credited with common sense, even when he
gives the Reader’s Digest version.

*
  In this example, I am comparing God to an earthly father who would promise
some reward (in this case, a pony ride) to his children upon their successful
completion of some task he has given them. Their loving earthly father does not
look for dust under their beds in hopes of disqualifying them from the reward he
has promised. Instead, he hopes that they will have performed their task
sufficiently well to qualify for the reward he wants to give them. Similarly, God
actually wants to reward us and is looking for ways to help us qualify for the
rewards (Heb 12:6-8). Discipline is an expression of love, not an attempt to
disqualify us from rewards by looking for dust under the bed.
Appendix 2. Boasting before God                                      Page 131
        James would agree with Paul that some forms of boasting are sin
(Jam 4:16). At the same time, James would also agree with Paul that not all
forms of boasting are sin (Jam 1:9). The Greek word in both these Pauline
and Jacobean texts for boasting is kauchaomai. In this latter verse James
says, “Let the poor brother boast in his exaltation” (TM). Let him boast in
the trials he is enduring. Or in terms of the illustration being employed
herein, let him boast in the way he is keeping his room clean. Boast to
whom? Can he not rejoice (KJV) in the Lord and thus before the Lord that
his enduring entitles him to the pony ride (so to speak) that the Lord
promised him a few verses later: “Blessed is a man who perseveres under
trial; for once he has been approved, he will receive the crown of life, which
the Lord has promised to those who love Him” (Jam 1:12)? Some think that
this crown of life is a pony ride in this life (GNTC, 1104). In view of
Jam 2:5 (cp. GNTC, 1113), however, it seems much more likely that James
has in mind the way God enriches our misthological experience of life: “Did
not God choose the poor of this world to be rich in faith and heirs of the
kingdom which He promised to those who love Him?” Being a crowned heir
means you get to keep the pony.

       Broad Context
        Clearly, the type of boasting Paul is excluding from consideration is
legalistic (i.e., soteric) boasting: “Where then is boasting [kauchesis]? It is
excluded. For we hold that one is justified by faith apart from works of the
law” (Rom 3:27-28). At the same time, yet in a different context and in a
different sense, but still within the same epistle no less, Paul can affirm:
“Therefore in Christ Jesus I have found reason for boasting [kauchesis] in
things pertaining to God” (Rom 15:17). Just as a child can be proud of his or
her work for Daddy, Paul may be understood as saying, “In Christ Jesus,
then, I have reason to be proud of my work for God” (ESV).
        If it is permissible for Paul to be proud of His work for God, might it
not also be permissible for him to be proud of his work before God?
Apparently so. Would an earthly Daddy want his children to be proud of
their work for him but be ashamed of their work before him? Certainly not.
        Some questions do not require a background in Greek to answer.
Nevertheless, for Greek enthusiasts, perhaps this tidbit will be of interest.
The phrase translated in Rom 5:17 by the ESV as Paul being proud of his
work for God (pros ton theon) is the identical phrase translated as before
God in Rom 4:2. Paul is boasting before God in Rom 15:17, so Rom 4:2
should not be taken to mean that Paul rules out all boasting before God. Nor
is this the only occasion in which Paul uses this expression in reference to
boasting before God.
Page 132                          Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection

      Relative Before
    Pros can be translated in various ways, of course. Some commentators
would say that Rom 4:2 excludes boasting in reference to God or
Godwards.* Obviously, though, Paul is boasting in reference to God in
Rom 15:17. Consequently, Rom 4:2 does not preclude boasting in reference
to God in every context. Pros can also be understood as Godwards in the
sense of toward God. Paul has confidence toward God (pros ton theon,
2Cor 3:4). John uses this same phrase and concurs (1Jn 3:21) that this
boldness (parresia) may be had at the Bema (1Jn 2:28; 5:14). Boasting, in
the positive form of boldness and confidence, is encouraged biblically:
before God, toward God, and in reference to God.
       Understandably, negative aspects of boasting (such as legalism and
haughtiness) are ruled out. Paul may be understood appropriately as
asserting, “For if Abraham was justified by works, he has something to boast
about; but not legalistically before God” (Rom 4:2). One should not take
pros ton theon in an absolute sense so as to rule out all forms of boasting
before God. To be sure, all legalistic boasting is ruled out before God.
Misthological boasting before God is not ruled out, however. In fact,
misthological boasting before God is encouraged in a variety of other
contexts. God does not want His children to be haughty, but being confident
before Him is not naughty.




*
  Just as a relative all (pas) is commonly recognized by interpreters in
certain contexts, a relative before (pros) should be acknowledged in this
context. The Bible does not say that God is impossible to please
misthologically, only that He is impossible to please without faith. For that
matter, the faith described in Heb 11:6 is the confidence that pleasing God
by one’s misthological performance is possible. God is not impossible to
please. Much to the contrary, God is pleased when we seek to please Him on
His terms, so much so that His faithful servant can joyfully look forward to
hearing Him say, “Well done!” Expecting a positive outcome is not sinful
when one has been faithful. In view of Heb 11:6, not expecting a positive
outcome would be sinful if one has been faithful. God wants us to believe
that He is possible to please. To disbelieve that He is possible to please
would displease Him.

     1
        Geisler enjoined S with P by claiming that God determinately knows
who will persevere. Geisler’s determinate election results in a conditional
security in which the result is positively guaranteed. Therefore, in terms of
results, Geisler’s Calminianism looks more like PEARP. This might be the
best acronym by which to describe Calminianism. Since the perseverance is
guaranteed in TULIP, the security of the elect is also. They will not lose
their regeneration since they will persevere. Therefore, this security is both
guaranteed and conditional. Geisler’s approach results in the same outcome
and, therefore, is best considered a modification in which the S in PEARS
has been replaced with a synthetic form of P in TULIP to yield PEARP.
        Alternatively, since the acronyms for Calvinism and Arminianism
both teach that perseverance is necessary to reach heaven, the Calminian
mediating position forged by affirming the soteriological necessity of
perseverance from the S of PEARS and the P of TULIP might be better
described by a combination of S + P. Because Geisler affirmed this core
doctrine of perseverance from a TULIP perspective but rejected the rest of
TULIP in preference for an approach more in harmony with PEARS, his
Calminian position may be described appropriately as somewhat analogous
to a PEARS+P since he affirms the TULIP form of P. Conversely, some
Calvinists frankly acknowledge that Calvinistic retention of regeneration is
conditioned on perseverance. This stress might be regarded by some
Calvinists to be TULIP+S, at least in terms of stress. The Arminian S+P and
the Calvinistic P+S forms of Calminianism might be represented by the
acronyms PEARPS and TULIPS respectively. From the viewpoint of
predestination, perseverance is guaranteed in both forms of Calminianism.
Since the majority of Calminians seem to affirm the body of the PEARS, the
best recourse is to either describe them in terms of PEARP, PEARPS, or just
stress the tail-end agreement with PS.

        Jacob wants to win grace from Esau by offering him a bountiful

means to obtain grace rather than an expression of grace. Joseph found grace
in Potiphar’s eyes because his service to Potiphar caused him to prosper.
Joseph found grace because of his service (Gen 39:4). A man’s wife may not
find grace in his eyes because of some indecency in her (Dt 24:1). Ruth
Page 134                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
found grace in Boaz’s eyes because of her service to her mother-in-law, and

(1Sam 16:21-22). David expects his men to find grace in Nabal’s eyes

(Dan 1:9). Similarly, the Lord gives grace to those who walk uprightly
(Ps 84:11). Those who are truthful find grace (Prov 3:3-4). To seek goodness
is to seek grace as its accompanying reward (Prov 11:27).
        7
          The Scripture repeatedly says that rewards are based on works.
Conversely, works are rewardable. Rewards are certainly not limited to
works of the law; therefore, it is futile to try to limit works to works of the
law. To be sure, the word works is a polymorphous term capable of many
usages within its semantic range. Even so, this polymorphous affirmation
simply means that the word has a semantic range with contextual limitations.
However, in no context would works denote behavior that results in a gift.
Anything truly falling under the works umbrella denotes performance and is
thus meritorious in nature. Since all polymorphous usages of work fall
within the realm of performance, and thus have meritorious associations,
saving faith cannot be a work. Soteriological faith cannot be a work in the
meritorious sense; hence, it cannot be a work in any true sense. Therefore,
soteriological faith falls in the category pictured by number one in the
illustration.
           Illustration 32. Soteriological versus Misthological Faith
                                                               Works
           Faith
Endnotes                                                              Page 135
Saving faith is not meritorious and thus falls within the spectrum of #1.
Saving faith is not a work. Soteriological justification is by faith apart from
works (Rom 3:28), just as it is by grace apart from works (Rom 11:6). All
works are meritorious and fall with the spectrum of #3. Perseverance in faith
is a work and, therefore, would fall within the overlap of #2. For further
discussion of this illustration, see The Outer Darkness.

             Illustration 33. Conditionally Loved by God in Christ

of our Lord Jesus Christ resulting in eternal life” (Jude 1:20-21; TM).

          The debate as to whether the grace referred to in Acts 18:27 is
Apollos’ gift of grace is inconsequential. He would be the intermediate agent
of God’s grace. One might compare Rom 10:17 (faith comes from hearing).
Such hearing is a necessary prerequisite to faith (Rom 10:14). Hearing does
not guarantee faith, but it does make faith possible. Of course, there is no
need to read irresistible grace into Acts 18:27. If their faith was a result of
God’s irresistible grace, then they certainly did not need Apollos’ help to
grow in this grace! One could just as easily retort that God’s prevenient
grace is in view.

antithetical to a previously held persuasion is a dissuasion in terms of the

      16
         According to Corner’s assessment of Gal 5:2,4, “for a Christian to
fall from grace to the point where Christ is of no value at all, can only mean
no more salvation.” Corner alleges that securitists “never teach that
Christians can fall from grace that saves to the point where Christ is of no
value” (Myth, 77, bold and italics his, underline mine). Had Corner wished
to speak truthfully, however, he might have spoken a little more cautiously.
      To be sure, some advocates of eternal security contend that genuine
believers are not in danger of being separated from Christ or of having no
value from Christ (e.g., Seymour, Gift, 171-172; MacDonald, Forever, 187).
Corner quotes other securitists who hold this view (Corner, Security, 240).
Corner’s allegation that securitists fail to acknowledge that genuine believers
are in danger is true for such advocates of eternal security.
      Corner’s statement is false, however, when advocates of unconditional
security who affirm an alternative securitist point of view are considered.
For example, that the warnings (about falling from grace, being severed
from Christ, and having no corresponding value from Christ) are addressed
to genuine believers is acknowledged by Dillow (p. 425). Moreover, Corner
Endnotes                                                               Page 137
is familiar with Dillow’s book and yet acts as if no securitist has ever taught
what he knows Dillow to teach. For that matter, Wiersbe, in his popular FG
commentary, likewise understands this passage to be warning genuine
believers (BEC, vol. 1, 715). In the FG commentary by GES, Wilkin
similarly understands the passage as having intended application to genuine
believers (GNTC, 845-846). In his book on OSAS, Kendall applies this
passage to believers (p. 170). I agree with such writers that Paul’s warning is
addressed to genuine believers. The falseness of Corner’s statement that no
one advocating OSAS would ever apply this passage to believers is evident.
Wuest, while affirming eternal security, interprets the passage to mean that
believers can fall from grace and fail to have Christ profit them in terms of
the present “Christian experience” rather than “their Christian standing.”
Wuest rightly emphasizes,

      Just because the process of sanctification is temporarily retarded in a
      believer’s life, does not say that his justification is taken away. If that
      were the case, then retention of salvation would depend upon the
      believer’s works, and then salvation would not depend on grace
      anymore. And we find ourselves in the camp of the Judaizers, ancient
      and modern. (Wuest, Galatians,138, italics his.)

      This assessment is common. For example, a quick Google will bring up
a link from Bible.org that takes the same position: “What does it mean to be
‘severed from Christ’ in Gal. 5:4?” Experiential loss is a common
assessment. When I was in seminary, I had a teacher state this as his
interpretation, and this teacher was a Lordship Salvationist! At the very
least, Corner has not spoken with forthright honesty. Many securitists do, in
fact, teach that this passage applies to genuine believers.
      Additionally, on the next page (Myth, 78), Corner further claims that
OSAS teachers do not present the following passages in their discussion:
Mk 10:30; Rom 2:7; Gal 6:8-9; Tit 3:7. Yet such passages are addressed by
both Dillow and Hodges (whom Corner also repeatedly quotes in his earlier
writing). Corner’s own citations show that Corner has not spoken the truth.
What is more, the FG commentaries mentioned above, not to mention my
own writings, deal with such passages. I point this out in hopes that Corner
may repent of his bearing false witness against OSAS writers. After all,
since he believes that unrepentant liars who fail to bring forth the fruit
worthy of repentance will spend eternity in the Lake of Fire, his salvation is
at stake—given his theological outlook. So for his sake, I hope that he
repents of his misrepresentation of FG theology. Given his theology, it
would be unchristian not to point out his error so that he can make the
necessary corrections in order to reach his performatistic heaven.
      From my perspective, however, given Corner’s own testimony that at
one time he briefly held to OSAS (Myth, 160), I would prefer to regard
Page 138                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
Corner as a genuine brother in Christ who has fallen from grace into
legalism yet who is not in danger of losing his salvation from the Lake of
Fire. He would be a modern-day picture of the Galatian brethren.
      The negation of opheleo in Gal 5:2 regarding these fallen believers is
rendered variously as Christ being of no advantage, benefit, or profit to
them. Christ will profit such brethren nothing at all in regard to the subject
matter at hand, which is inheriting the kingdom. Fallen brethren will enter
the kingdom but not inherit the kingdom. Misthological rather than
soteriological profits and losses are the subject matter at hand.
      Thus, I expect to see Corner in heaven; however, unless he repents of
his legalism, I do not expect to see him ruling with Christ in heaven. Instead,
I expect to see him cast into the outer darkness. Contrastively, he would
expect me to be cast into hell because I teach OSAS (Myth, 73-74). The
stakes are high, of that I have no doubt. Some might think that the stakes are
much higher for me than for him. Mathematically, I think otherwise.
Severity is perhaps on his side; probability, however, is decisively on my
side. Perhaps is as much as I can allow in terms of probability because more
perceptive Arminians (such as Picirilli, pp. 204-205) discredit the saved-by-
faith-and-kept-by-works approach taken by their fellow Arminians (such as
Corner). Accordingly, an astute Arminian might conclude that Corner is in
danger of an Arminian hell, while faithful securitists are not in danger of an
Arminian hell. Thus, in terms of Arminian probability, Corner is taking a
very precarious position even in terms of severity. For further discussion of
probability, see my discussion of “Pascal’s Wager” in Woolly Wolves and
Woolless Sheep.
      Mathematically, I find this discussion of probability interesting. Yet
one need not be content with probability. Contextually, this mathematical
probability gives way to misthological certainty. As a former math major, I
appreciate Corner’s appeal to mathematics to prove his point about reaping
destruction in Gal 6:8 (Myth, 116). Basically, it goes something like this:

     A = Reap Destruction (Gal 6:8a).
     B = Not reap eternal life (Gal 6:8b).
     C = Not inherit kingdom (Gal 5:21).

     Because A = B and B = C, therefore A = C. Furthermore, reaping
destruction is contrasted with reaping eternal life and therefore is equatable
with reaping death (cp. Rom 8:13). Thus, A = reap death = die. Also,
because A = B = C, not inheriting the kingdom is equivalent to death. I agree
with Corner on such matters. For that matter, so does Wilkin (“Legacy,” 33-
34). Where Wilkin and I disagree with Corner is that, as securitists, Wilkin
and I regard this reaping destruction to be misthological; Corner regards it to
be soteriological. Corner believes that the gift of eternal life is at stake.
Securitists do not. Rather, securitists readily can admit that the passage is
Endnotes                                                             Page 139
dealing with life and death as rewards, “because he who puts in the seed of
the flesh will of the flesh get the reward of death; but he who puts in the
seed of the Spirit will of the Spirit get the reward of eternal life” (BBE).
     BBE has done its ABCs. Paul says that the inheritance is a reward
based on works (Col 3:23-24). (Unfortunately, Corner did not present this
passage in either of his books.) Since C = reward, A and B = rewards also.
Paul is talking about how we can have rewards within heaven, not entrance
into heaven. He is discussing life and death in terms of inheriting the
kingdom, not entering it. To be separated from God in the outer darkness
and devoid of eternal life as a reward would be misthological death. Fallen
brethren are in danger of misthological death. They have the gift of life, but
they fail to reap the reward of life.
            Illustration 34. Extrapolation of Misthological Death




      In the above illustration, misthological death occurs when the effects of
the Bema are carried out. When unfaithful believers are expelled from
Heavenly Jerusalem (to live in more remote locations of the kingdom) and
are excluded from the privilege of ruling with Christ throughout eternity,
their qualitative experience of eternal life will flat line into misthological
death. Qualitatively, in terms of having misthological life, these believers
will reap misthological death. Nevertheless, in terms of retaining their
quantitative possession of eternal life as a free gift, entering the kingdom,
and living within it, their experience of eternal life cannot drop below the
soteriological threshold. Believers who fall from grace are in danger of
misthological death, not soteriological death.
      The broader context confirms that misthological rather than
soteriological issues are at stake for these fallen brethren because even in
their fallen state they remain sons of God, children of promise, and genuine
brethren who have the Spirit crying out from within their regenerate hearts,
“Abba! Father!” I appeal to my fallen brethren to repent of taking the
doctrine of rewards so lightly. Eternal misthological separation from God in
the outer darkness is not as bad as being cast into the Lake of Fire, to be
Page 140                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
sure; nevertheless, misthological separation is not a matter to be taken
lightly. One need not resort to legalism in order to take the wages of death
seriously. The legalists threatened the Galatians with hell. Paul, in contrast,
threatens them with not inheriting the kingdom. Let us follow Paul’s
example, not that of the legalists.

         Like Corner, Lalond is a fellow Arminian in his camp who makes
much the same arguments and mistakes: “The Galatian epistle is addressed
to Christians, that is, to the born again—the saved….Surely, the Galatians
were the spiritually regenerate children of God” (p. 358). The manner in
which Lalond has worded this affirmation gives away the argument,
however. Lalond speaks as if they were still spiritually regenerate Christians
at the point of Paul’s writing. If that is the case, however (and it is), then the
securest point is proven. They remained God’s regenerate children even
though they had fallen from grace. Lalond glosses over this most significant
fact. Not only had they received the gospel (Gal 1:9) and the Spirit (Gal 3:2)
in the past, and not only had they successfully run the race in the beginning,
Endnotes                                                                Page 141
and not only were they called Paul’s brethren (Gal 1:11) and children
(Gal 4:19) in the past, but, most importantly, Paul still calls them brethren
and children in the present, that is, at the point of his writing, even after they
had committed apostasy!
      Indeed, they had committed apostasy, yet they were still regenerate!
Only six pages after affirming that born-again, regenerate, children of God
are being addressed in Galatians, Lalond states: “The point Paul was making
to the Galatians is that they were not believers” (emphasis his, 364). So true!
They had stopped believing the gospel. They had fallen from grace;
nevertheless, they remained God’s regenerate children. Lalond should not
have glossed over such a critical point of the argument and such an obvious
point of the epistle.
      Additionally, Lalond fails to perceive that the term believer may be
used substantively of those who no longer are believers functionally. Static
classification as a believer is not conditioned on remaining a believer
dynamically. See “Believer ≠ Ongoing Believer” in Sealed and Secure and
“Substantival Categorization” in Believe. In the present book, see Articular
Participle as Substantive (p. 80) and consider that just as whoever ever
commits murder is a murderer, so whoever ever believes is a believer.
      In addition to Gal 5:2 and 5:4, Lalond also stresses a third verse as
allegedly demonstrating that these regenerate believers were in danger of not
ultimately entering heaven: “I fear for you, that perhaps I have labored over
you in vain” (Gal 4:11). Leland asks, “Could it honestly be said that Paul’s
labor was in vain if the Galatians would ultimately enter the kingdom of
God” (p. 363). Absolutely! I have spent many years laboring over my sons
in the things of Christ. A large part of my motivation for writing books such
as this is to continue that labor. I am not doing all this labor in hopes that
they will eventually enter the kingdom. I am absolutely certain that entrance
is a given in their case since they have believed in Christ alone for eternal
life and now belong to Him eternally and unconditionally. If, however, they
were to fall from grace, then all my work would be in vain because I am
doing this work in hopes that they will inherit the kingdom. Relative to why
I am doing all this work on their behalf, if they were to fall from grace, then
my work on their behalf would be completely in vain. Legalists will not
inherit the kingdom.
      Lalond is of the opinion that Paul’s anathema in Gal 1:8-9 applies
soteriologically to those who teach unconditional security. Thus, he believes
that Tony Evans is on his way to hell (p. 360). I, on the other hand, believe
that Evans is on his way to heaven and that Lalond may very well be on his
way to hell. Lalond believes that final salvation is soteriologically contingent
on perseverance (p. 118); that is, soteriological security is based on
perseverance (p. 119). With a woefully unpersuasive explanation, he claims
that his security-in-perseverance theology is not “salvation by works”
(p. 130). Yet he indicates that God may remove us out of Christ as wages
Page 142                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
for our sins (p. 152) if He finds fault with our works (p. 146). His not-saved-
by-works-but-saved-by-works claim is nothing more than contradictory,
back-to-back doubletalk (p. 155). He believes that final salvation, and thus
eternal destiny, is affected by works (p. 309-312). His appeals to a Reformed
illustration in a failed attempt to explain the situation: “Evangelical faith and
works are like sunlight and heat, they are inseparable” (p. 313) However, if
they are inseparable, then how can one be justified by faith alone?
Regardless, his previous comments show that he conditions the final
outcome on the heat, not just the light. Obviously, final justification by faith
alone is a myth in such theology. See Daniel Lalond Jr., The Lying Promise:
Testing the Gospel According to Chuck Swindoll, Tony Evans, and Erwin W.
Lutzer (Fox Lake, Ill: DTG Books, 2006), 60.
       19
          The irony is that the carnal believers in Galatia who are disqualified
from inheriting the kingdom had fallen into legalism, not licentiousness. Yet
legalists fail to realize that the warnings are addressed first and foremost to
them—carnal legalists. Granted, the vice list in Gal 5:19-21 (if taken out of
context or put into another context such as 1Cor 6:9-10) might give one the
impression that the Galatian believers had given themselves over to a
compromise with a worldly lifestyle. However, in the Galatian context, these
believers were trying to fight fire with fire—fleshly indulgence with fleshly
performance. They were combating licentiousness with legalism. Paul’s
point is that they are losing the battle—badly.
       As Wilkin correctly and repeatedly notes in his commentary on
Galatians, legalism requires absolute perfection (Gal 3:10; GNTC 848). Any
failure in that capacity is catastrophic. Without doubt, those who manifest an
unloving, worldly lifestyle will fail to inherit the kingdom (Gal 5:19-21). Yet
Paul indicts these legalists with biting and devouring one another. “Legalists
claim to produce love, but what they actually produce is just the opposite”
(Gal 5:15; GNTC 848). These Christian legalists have been caught with their
hand in the fleshly, vice-list cookie jar. Indeed, the flesh (even legalistic
flesh) can be expected to produce such deeds as those described in the vice
list. Fleshly performance produces such results. How can those severed from
Christ by legalism be expected to do anything else than produce fleshly
performance? “Legalism poisons one’s motives and hence one’s works”
(Gal 5:4; GNTC 846). Given the context of his whole discussion, Wilkin is
right to conclude: “In this epistle sowing to the flesh is living legalistically”
(Gal 6:8; GNTC 854). “The works the Judaizers proclaim [i.e., claim to
produce] only seem to be good. In reality legalism produces bad works”
(Gal 5:19-21; GNTC 850; emphasis his). Legalistic love is a façade.

          The normal objection to the proposal that Esau represents someone
who is saved comes from Heb 12:16. Esau is described there as immoral
(pornos) and worldly (bebelos). This generally is assumed to mean that he
was probably lost. But this is not necessarily true. The only other occurrence
of pornos in Hebrews is in 13:4. And certainly it applies to believers there.
Immoral believers who defile their marriage bed are not excluded from
judgment. The Pauline passages seem to support a misthological rather than
soteriological exclusion. In 1Cor 5:9-11, Paul exhorts us to not fellowship
with immoral Christians. He then proceeds to warn believers against
immoral behavior, saying that those guilty of this type of behavior will not
inherit the kingdom (1Cor 6:11). The same is true in Eph 5:5, where
believers are warned of misthological exclusion. To be sure, pornos is
soteriological when used to describe those who are immoral in the eternal
state (Rev 21:8; 22:15). But then again, it would have to be when describing
those who are considered immoral by nature for all eternity. This appellation
does not necessarily apply to those who are immoral in the temporal state.
      As to worldly, the fact that Paul warns Timothy not to participate in
worldly behavior would indicate that Christians can, in fact, be guilty of
being worldly (1Tim 4:7; 6:20; 2Tim 2:16). Esau is best seen as an example
of a worldly child of God. The immediate context would confirm that what
he lost was not soteric salvation, and what he sought was something other
than such salvation. He did not cease to be Isaac’s son, and he did not seek
to be Isaac’s son. He was Isaac’s child by birth. He lost his birthright, not his
birth. Entrance into heaven is not for sale. The broader context would bear
out that Esau is being used to warn genuine believers about the dangers that
they themselves face pertaining to their inheritance.

            Robert N. Wilkin, “Are Esau and Cain in Heaven or Hell?” GIF

         Lenski contends that since it is feminine in Heb 12:17, the antecedent
cannot be the phrase place for repentance because this is a singular concept
with the masculine object place being modified by the feminine genitive
repentance. Had the writer intended to indicate that Esau diligently sought a
place for repentance, he would have had to use a masculine form of it to
refer to the inability to find such a place.
      The grammatical weight certainly seems to be in favor of adopting this
understanding. Gar (for), which introduces this phrase (for he found no
place for repentance), may certainly be understood as introducing a
parenthetical element. See second primary meaning listed by BDAG. Some
translate it accordingly in order to clarify that the antecedent is blessing:
“For ye know that also afterwards, desiring to inherit the blessing, he was
rejected, (for he found no place for repentance) although he sought it
earnestly with tears” (DBY). Another option is to change the word order to
clarify that the antecedent is the blessing rather than repentance: “As you
know, when he wanted to obtain the blessing afterwards, he was rejected
and, though he pleaded for it with tears, he could find no way of reversing
the decision” (NJB). For further implementation of these two techniques see
Bruce’s citation of Watkins (NIC, 368, n. 120). But the more popular option
among those translations taking it upon themselves to clarify the antecedent
as the blessing is to simply translate it accordingly: “You know that later,
when he wanted to inherit the blessing, he was rejected, for he found no
chance to repent, even though he sought the blessing with tears” (NRS; cp.
NIV, NIB, NAB, GNV. Also see NET).
      The typical translation implementing it (without any of these above
techniques to clarify) leaves the English reader with the false impression that
there is nothing but ambiguity, which might even favor taking repentance as
the antecedent. Rather than convey this false impression, it would be better
to use one or more of the above techniques, perhaps with italics to indicate
implication.
