      34
          To put 1Thess 5:24 in the same category as other texts making
positive expectations of certain Christians because of their present or
anticipated behavior is tempting. After all, 2Thess 2:3-4 certainly puts the
Thessalonians in this category. Even so, it should not be taken as meaning
that God unconditionally will cause believers to persevere so that they can
be presented blameless. This is a mistaken perception of God’s faithfulness.
While it is true that God’s faithfulness is the basis for unconditional
soteriological security in 2Tim 2:13, God’s misthological faithfulness, in
contrast, is very much conditioned upon performance. In 1Cor 1:8-9, God is
faithful to enable us to meet the requirements to be misthologically
blameless. (See endnote 50). This does not mean that God unconditionally
causes us to meet such requirements, however. Later, in this same epistle,
Paul clarifies that God is faithful to enable us to overcome temptation by
providing a way of escape (1Cor 10:13). Nevertheless, taking advantage of
the means of escape is our responsibility. Victory is not a foregone
conclusion. Similarly, the affirmation in Heb 10:23 that God is faithful is
enjoined with the exhortation to hold fast. God’s faithfulness does not
unconditionally cause us to hold fast.
         So, although 1Thess 5:24 might refer (in Pauline thought) to the fact
that God is faithful in terms of making the practical sanctification of 5:23
possible (cf. 2Thess 2:3-4), such an interpretation seems improbable in v. 24.
Rather, this latter verse appears to refer to the misthological preservation of
v. 23. The imperative of 1Thess 5:19 and the optative of 5:23 suggest that
what God is faithful in bringing about is the promised reward for those who
meet the contextual stipulations. God is contingently faithful in this context
for this benefit. The contingent nature of this presentation is clearly stressed
by Paul in Col 1:22-23. In other words, the fact that God is faithful to reward
those who are faithful means that His faithfulness to reward is conditioned
on their faithfulness to obey His injunction. His faithfulness to reward the
faithful does not unconditionally guarantee a positive outcome for those
addressed by the injunction. God is misthologically faithful. Whether
believers are misthologically faithful is conditioned upon their free response
to avail themselves of the resources God faithfully provides. Soteriological
faithfulness is a different matter altogether. God is soteriologically faithful to
the unfaithful (2Tim 2:13).
         Qualifying my statement by saying that the biblical writers tend to
have positive expectations concerning their converts was necessary because
there are exceptions. Paul tells Timothy to gently discipline believers who
opposed him, “if perhaps God may grant them repentance” (2Tim 2:25).
Peter was far from certain that Simon would be forgiven: “"Therefore repent
of this wickedness of yours, and pray the Lord that if possible, the intention
Page 146                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
of your heart may be forgiven you” (Acts 8:22). Church history informs us
that Simon did not repent. The writer of Hebrews says that sometimes it is
impossible for wayward believers to repent (Heb 6:6).

         R. T. Kendall, The Complete Guide to the Parables: Understanding
and Applying the Stories of Jesus (Grand Rapids: Chosen Books, 2004), 257.

      37
         The statement in Jn 14:12 is taken by some in LS as an absolute
expectation and thus a litmus test of regeneration: “Truly, truly, I say to you,
he who believes in Me, the works that I do shall he do also; and greater
works than these shall he do; because I go to the Father.” If you truly believe
in Jesus, then, according to this LS perspective, you will work. Such a LS
argument takes this verse as true of every single genuine believer: If a
person claims to be a believer and yet is not doing the works of Jesus, then
that person is not a genuine believer. Those adopting this mindset typically
consider these works to be evangelistic in scope. Taken to its logical LS
conclusion, any person claiming to be a believer but not actively leading the
lost to Christ is himself or herself lost. If you are not an active and effective
soul winner, then you are lost according to this LS argument. If you are a
believer in Christ, then you are a fruitful worker for Christ. No fruit, then no
root. If you are not bearing fruits, then you are not a believer in Christ.
      To support this line of thought, those in LS might assert that every
other time this phrase (he who believes in Me) occurs in GJ, it is true of
every single genuine believer: Jn 6:35; 7:38; 11:25; 12:44,46. Thus, it cannot
be claimed that the phrase is referring merely to believers collectively.
Workers are not a subset of believers. All believers are workers.
      To further advance this argument from the LS perspective, Lordship
Salvationists could appeal to multiple texts. Jesus said to them, “I am the
bread of life; he who comes to Me shall not hunger, and he who believes in
Me shall never thirst” (Jn 6:35). Surely, one would not read this passage
and conclude that only some of those who believe will never thirst or that
this promise only applies to believers collectively rather than individually.
This verse is a picture of regeneration, and regeneration applies to each and
every believer. “He who believes in Me, as the Scripture said, ‘From his
innermost being shall flow rivers of living water’” (Jn 7:38). Again, this
phrase makes an assertion that is true of every believer. If you do not have
living water, then you are not a believer. Jesus said to her, “I am the
resurrection and the life; he who believes in Me shall live even if he dies”
(Jn 11:25). The promise of life is to every believer without exception. If you
do not have life, then you are not a believer. And Jesus cried out and said,
“He who believes in Me does not believe in Me, but in Him who sent Me”
(Jn 12:44). Again, there are no exceptions. Everyone who believes in Jesus
believes in the Father. “I have come as light into the world, that everyone
who believes in Me will not remain in darkness. (Jn 12:46) If you claim to
be a believer and yet remain in the darkness, then you are a liar. Only
Endnotes                                                                Page 147
followers of Christ have the light of regeneration (Jn 8:12). If you truly
believe in Christ, then you will follow Him and not walk in the darkness.
“Truly, truly, I say to you, he who believes in Me will do the works that I
do and also do greater things than these he will do because I go to the
Father” (Jn 14:12; TM). Again, to claim that this is just true of believers
collectively is to reject the usage throughout the book in which the phrase is
associated with believers individually. According to LS theology, therefore,
one might conclude that every believer in Christ will do Christ-like works or
such a person is not a believer in Christ.
      Indeed, many of these above texts have been cited in the present book
as being true of whoever ever believes. See Articular Participle as
Substantive, 80. Therefore, proveitists might interject that this text likewise
should be translated: “Whoever ever believes in Me will do the works that I
do and also do greater things than these he will do because I go to the
Father” (Jn 14:12; TM). Consequently, from the proveitist point of view, if
you do not produce greater works than Jesus, then this proves that you never
ever believed savingly in Jesus.
      Further support for this LS interpretation is derived from explaining
what doing Christ’s works in Jn 14:12a means by means of the subsequent
phrase: because I go (Jn 14:12b). Jesus immediately goes on to explain that,
because of His going, the Father would send the Spirit back to convict the
world and enable believers to abide in Jesus and have an effective prayer
life. Those believers who abide will bear much fruit. They will produce
more conversions than even Jesus did. Those who do not abide are reputedly
not true believers according to this LS understanding. The greater things of
14:12 are the much fruit of 15:5. If you do not bear much fruit, then you are
not doing greater things, and you are not a genuine believer. Thus, LS might
respond that for FG to claim that a person can be a believer in Christ and not
do the works of Christ and not do even greater things than Christ in terms of
bearing much fruit for Christ is to generalize Jn 14:12 in such a manner that
is completely inconsistent with the manner in which this phrase is used
everywhere else in the GJ. According to the LS gospel of Jesus, if you are a
believer, then you are a worker.
      FG advocates may respond appropriately, however, that the distributive
translation of whoever ever is an assessment based on probability and
maintain that Jn 14:12 is an exception to the general rule so that in this
particular case Jesus intends a durative faith. In this case, Jesus only intends
to signify that those believers who persevere in the faith will produce these
types of results. The present book, in fact, provides examples where
exceptions to the rule exist and examples of FG interpreters on both sides of
that discussion. After all, the rule of punctiliarity for the articular participle
used as a substantive is only a statement of probability.
      Accordingly, the present discussion will allow that the LS argument
may be commended for individualizing good works and for making these
Page 148                             Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
good works the expectation for all believers. Indeed, such expectations are
permissible within FG parameters, as long as one asks: “Which believers? Is
it a relative all that is restricted to a certain class of believers in the context,
or is it a universal all that applies indiscriminately to all born-again
believers?” This line of inquiry should be quite natural to those in the
Reformed heritage who are accustomed to arguing for a relative point of
view in other Johannine contexts. One need only consult the arguments of
those advocating a limited atonement in Jn 12:32, for example. Did Jesus
draw all without exception or merely all without distinction? Or how about a
simple text such as Jn 3:16, do the words world and whosoever really apply
to everyone? Are the words whole world limited to the elect in 1Jn 2:2, or
did Christ die for everyone? Does everyone in Heb 2:9 only mean that Christ
died for every one of the elect? Five point Calvinists who want to take
certain words in a restrictive sense in certain contexts certainly should not be
averse to considering the context of the present passage in Jn 14:12 to see if
a restrictive understanding is best suited to its context.
      As to broad context, the Gospel of John can be divided into three basic
sections: Evangelism (1-12); Discipleship (13-17); Cross & Resurrection
(18-20). Christianson notes that pisteuo is used 14 times in GJ to “refer to
the continuing, deepening belief of those who already possessed eternal life
by means of an earlier act of belief (John 11:15; 13:19; 14:1a, 1b, 10, 11a,
11b, 12, 29; 16:30, 31; 20:8, 25, 29a).” Richard W. Christianson, “The
Soteriological Significance of Pisteuw in the Gospel of John,” Th.M. thesis
(Grace Theological Seminary, 1987), 10. All but the first of these references
is found in the discipleship section, but even that first reference introduces a
theme to be found in this latter section: the need for believers to believe.
Jesus repeatedly stresses this need.
      As to the immediate context, in this very chapter, Jesus starts off by
using an imperative to urge His eleven believing disciples to believe in Him
(Jn 14:1b): “You believe in God, believe also in Me” (Jn 14:1b; NKJ).
Radmacher’s preference for the imperatival understanding of second pisteuo
is to be preferred. Gary Derickson and Earl Radmacher, The Disciplemaker:
What Matters Most to Jesus (Salem: Charis Press, 2001), 94.
      Jesus again uses a double imperative just a few verses later to urge
these believers to believe. “Believe Me that I am in the Father and the Father
in Me, or else believe Me for the sake of the works themselves” (Jn 14:11;
NKJ). In the very next verse, which is the verse under discussion (14:12),
Jesus makes a promise to these believers that if they will believe in Him,
they will do great works. Yet He generalizes the promise so as to make it
universally true of all believers who progress on to believe in Him in the
prescribed manner. Contextually, the appeal and promise are for those who
already have trusted in Christ for eternal life to now trust in Him more
deeply for something additionally. Those believers who develop a deeper
faith in Christ will produce greater results in terms of their fruitfulness as
Endnotes                                                              Page 149
they pray for the Lord of the Harvest to send out workers into the harvest.
Our individual prayer and efforts will have a greater collective evangelistic
result than Christ had in His earthly ministry.
      Although I would not want to overstress the tense of the imperative in
Jn 14:11 unduly, it is a present tense imperative, which generally would be
expected to describe continuing in faith rather than coming to faith.
Radmacher is of this opinion and further asserts that the present imperative
is used here to denote the fact that Jesus is urging “them to continue in that
faith a little further” (Disciplemaker, 113). They are not only to keep on
believing but to advance in believing with durative faith. The exhortation is
both to persevere and progress in their faith in Him. Thus, the promise and
expectation of such works described in Jn 14:11-12 are not simply for those
who come to saving faith, but for those who advance beyond saving faith to
a mature faith. Mature believers will bear more fruit collectively through
their individual function in the body of Christ than Christ did while He was
on earth. This fruit is promised to mature believers who abide in Christ, not
to immature believers who fail either to progress or persevere in the faith.
Trying to make Jn 14:12 a test for regeneration is a perversion because the
passage is talking about a faith that moves beyond mere regeneration to
maturation. In this context, an abiding faith is a faith that not only lasts but
that drinks more and more fully from the sap of the vine to which it is
attached.
      John also picks back up on this theme in his epistle where he says that
Jesus commanded believers to love one another and to believe in Him. “And
this is His commandment, that we believe in the name of His Son Jesus
Christ, and love one another, just as He commanded us” (1Jn 3:23).
Apparently, John is alluding back to this private discourse in the upper room
in which Jesus commanded these believers to love one another and to
believe in Him. The lost are not the ones being commanded to love one
another. Nor are the lost being exhorted to believe in Jesus in this context.
Believers need to believe. Believers are also the ones to whom John wrote:
“These things I have written to you who believe in the name of the Son of
God, that you may know that you have eternal life, and that you may
continue to believe in the name of the Son of God” (1Jn 5:13; NKJ).
Believers who progress on in discipleship to believe in Christ in the manner
in which He exhorts will bear much fruit. Those believers who do not
develop a mature faith will not achieve these results. This verse (Jn 14:12) is
not the litmus test for saving faith but for mature, fruit-bearing faith.
      Jesus’ teaching here is compatible with His teaching elsewhere that
some seed which springs forth in generating new life will produce no fruit,
while some will produce “no fruit to maturity” (Lk 8:14), while other fruit
will produce an abundant harvest of mature fruit. Fruit bearing is not a test
of whether the seed has sprouted and produced life. Rather, fruit bearing is
the hallmark of maturation, not of simple regeneration. In Jn 14:12, the
Page 150                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
believer is to be understood contextually as being the mature believer. The
promise of doing more than Jesus does not apply to unbelieving believers;
that is, it does not apply to believers who merely have experienced
regeneration but who fail to persevere and progress in the faith (as Jesus
exhorts in Jn 14:1,11). In this context at least, a restricted understanding of
what it means to be a believer is justified. Of course, any NT text that
exhorts believers to believe or that makes conditional promises to those
believers who progress to walking and living by faith becomes a validating
cross-reference. Believers in Christ who believe in Him in the manner
described in this context will bear more fruit collectively than Christ did
while He was on earth as they abide in the vine and bear fruit as a function
of His body—the church.
           Illustration 35. Durative Believers ⊂ Punctiliar Believers




      Aspectually, the articular present participle of Jn 14:12 may be
understood as a substantive that contextually is referring to mature believers
who persevere and progress in the faith: “Truly, truly, I say to you, the
mature believer in Me, the works that I do shall he do also; and greater
works than these shall he do; because I go to the Father” (TM). Aspectually,
if one wishes to stress the action of the verb, one might regard it as a
distributive durative. Although such an aspect would be feasible
contextually in Jn 14:12, it would be exceedingly unlikely in Heb 10:14. See
Illustration 12. Distributive Durative Until the End, 91. Illustration 13.
Endnotes                                                             Page 151
Distributive Durative in the Present, 92. For the plausibility of considering
1Jn 5:5 from a similar perspective as is being suggested here for Jn 14:12,
see endnote 57.
      38
         As discussed elsewhere herein, if one wishes to bring out the irony in
the context between Gal 5:7 and 5:10, then peitho would be better translated
as persuaded in that context. However, if one wishes to express the
conceptual parallel with 2Thess 2:4, then translating it in terms of having
confidence in the Lord is to be preferred.
      In the author’s personal memorization of this epistle, he rendered
Gal 5:10 as persuaded but underlined the word in red and the rest of the
parallel in black and cited 2Thess 2:4 as parallel in a column. He then
provided the same corresponding marking for 2Thess 2:4 when memorizing
that epistle. The use of color coding in one’s memorization enables one to
better express the flexibility of the Greek semantic domain and denote
contextual and conceptual considerations. But it is beyond the scope of the
present book to use such techniques for the translations provided. The
general practice herein is to translate verses with some flexibility in
recognition of such factors without necessarily explaining all facets that
have been taken into consideration. In other words, that a verse may be
translated in one manner in one part of my writings and in a different
manner in another part of my writings may be taken as denoting flexibility
rather than inconsistency.

          John F. Hart, “Does Philippians 1:6 Guarantee Progressive

      40
         Whereas I perceive simultaneous faith and doubt, Wilkin only sees
vacillation between faith and doubt: “Paul vacillates in his thoughts about
the Galatians. One minute he expresses his doubts about them (4:11, 20; 5:4,
15) and the next minute he expresses his confidence in them (5:10; 6:1-2,
9).” (Robert N. Wilkin, Galatians, GNTC, 847). Since Wilkin is an advocate
of the degreeless view of faith, any doubt would signify no faith.
Correspondingly, he pictures Paul as vacillating rapidly between faith and
doubt. Does this mean that Wilkin regards Paul to be unstable (Jam 1:6-8)?
Also, as an advocate of the degreeless view, Hodges likewise comments
regarding the Jacobean passage: “When one doubts, he is not believing”
(Zane C. Hodges, James, GNTC, 1102).
      Suppose an advocate of the degreeless view were to read these passages
from Paul to him and interrogate him by saying, “Which is it Paul, do you
believe or not believe? You cannot do both at the same time!” How would
Paul respond?
      I doubt that Paul would be impressed by the degreeless advocate who
would charge him with being inconsistent, unstable, or contradictory. To the
contrary, Paul surely would respond, “Both, I believe but have my doubts.”
Some propositions biblically allow both doubt and faith, whereas other
Page 152                          Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
matters are expected to be free of doubt. As Hodges correctly notes
regarding the Jacobean passage,

      James is not speaking here [Jam 1:5] of any and all wisdom,
      since believers will always be deficient in many such areas
      while still in the body. Rather, in this context, James is
      speaking of that particular wisdom needed to cope with the
      various trials they experience. (James, GNTC, 1102; emphasis
      his.)

      A prayer for this particular type of wisdom should be free of all doubt
because God, no doubt, desires to answer that prayer as being in complete
accordance with His will and as not violating the free will of others.
However, God may not necessarily require that same degree of confidence
pertaining to requests for wisdom in other areas in order for such prayers to
qualify for being answered.
      What if a young man is praying for wisdom as to how he might win a
certain young lady’s heart? Is the young man biblically required to ask in
faith without any doubting that God will give him the wisdom necessary to
irresistibly win her heart? Will God give him the ability to take away her
free will? No. She is free to marry whom she wishes, as long as she does so
within the biblical parameters (GNTC, 736). One cannot accuse the young
man of being unstable if he recognizes her free will. Furthermore, how can
he know for certain that marrying this particular woman brings maximum
praise to God (GNTC, 455)?
      Nor can one accuse Paul of vacillating in an unstable state or speaking
contradictory because he simultaneously recognizes the free will of the
Galatians and the possibility of their freewill failure, while nevertheless
affirming that he believes that the outcome will be positive. It would be a
misapplication of James’ comments to say, “Let Paul not think that his
prayers to God for the Galatians will be answered because his is an unstable
man who is not asking in faith without any doubting.”
      Paul believed in free will. Otherwise, he would not have had any
doubts about their faithfulness. Even so, he also had confidence in the Lord
concerning them (cp. 2Thess 3:4) that they would heed his warning.

      50
         Hodges, Siege, 89-96. Constable would take 1Cor 1:8 as denoting
soteriological standing: “It means every Christian will stand before the Lord
guiltless, unimpeachable, because God has imputed the guilt of our sins to
the Savior and He has borne them.” Yet this seems inconsistent with his
definition of bebaioo: “By God’s sustaining power Christians will stand free
of guilt before Him” (1Corinthians, 8). Translating bebaioo as confirm
would be more consistent if one prefers this particular soteriological
interpretation. In that case, if Paul had said that we will be confirmed in the
end, perhaps Constable’s soteriological interpretation would be more
plausible. However, that the action is to be preformed unto the end suggests
present activity. In the minds of most, this would be an ongoing
strengthening activity. Thus, my preference within the present book is to
adopt the alternate popular translation of bebaioo as strengthen in this
particular context. God empowers us now, as Constable correctly indicates,
but the purpose for which God strengthens believers is so that they may be
approved misthologically on that day, not so that they can be confirmed as
being genuine believers on that day. Thus, I am in agreement with Hunt that
the issue is being blameless at the Bema. Dwight L. Hunt, 1 Corinthians,
GNTC, ed. Robert N. Wilkin (Denton, TX: Grace Evangelical Society,
2010), 712. Correspondingly, a translation of 1Cor 1:8 such as that provided
by the CJB is well taken herein: “He will enable you to hold out until the
end and thus be blameless on the Day of our Lord.” Or as the NAB
expresses it, to confirm is to “keep you firm to the end” in this passage. But
such confirmation is not irresistible or soteriological when this verse is
translated in such a manner.
      Similarly, rather than take Rom 14:4 as denoting a soteriological
standing, it would be better understood as Paul’s positive expectation that
the believer will stand (histemi) by God’s strength in the present (i.e., logical
future) so that he may stand (paristemi) in the eschatological future
misthologically blameless before the Judgment Seat of Christ (Rom 14:10,
MT). Once again, I commend the GNTC on its interpretation of this related
passage as well. Obviously, this positive expectation is not an ironclad
guarantee of experiential success in the logical future or of misthological
Page 154                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
success in the eschatological future, as Paul indicates when he warns that the
believer may be hurt, destroyed, torn down, caused to stumble, and
condemned (Rom 14:15,20,23).

         I have not included the examples of distributive kata (VANT, 174).
See BDAG for 49 such instances. Additionally, I did not include Fanning’s
example of 2Tim 3:11 (“I endured persecutions”) in examples of distributive
aorists, nor Jn 18:2; Acts 10:41; 13:31; Rom 1:21; 3:23, 2Cor 8:22; 11:24-
25; Phil 4:16; 2Tim 3:11. (See VANT, 168, 259). Some of these texts might
serve better as examples of simple iteratives. It depends on how one defines
the distributive. Fanning uses this distinction: “The multiple situations may
be either iterative (one individual repeating the action or state) or
distributive (each one of a group involved with the occurrence once or
more)” (VANT, 258). From a distributive perspective, Rom 3:23 could be
taken as referring to each person (in the group of sinners) performing the
action of sinning more than once, even to the extent of sinning constantly—a
distributive durative (in which each distributive member sins in a durative
manner). Still, if one allows that a group can perform an iterative action, this
group action need not necessarily be regarded as a descriptive action. For
example, when Paul tells the Thessalonians, “You sent a gift more than once
for my needs” (Phil 4:16), an iterative group action seems to suffice. Surely,
each member of the group does not send a different gift. They contributed
individually (and thus distributively), but the gift was sent collectively.
Grammatically, a distributive aspect would refer to all members of a group
individually.
         Despite Fanning’s frequent affirmations that various types of
distributives (such as generic presents, present infinitives and subjunctives,
present and aorist commands, and present and aorist participles) depict the
individual performing the action only once, his concession above, that the
distributive action need not necessarily be performed only once by the
individual, might find collaboration in his positive citation of Burton and his
corresponding discussion. While discussing the present participle, Fanning
might be understood as tentatively suggesting that “perhaps” the customary
sense might include a distributive aspect “in which the indefinite individual
is seen as engaged in the occurrence repeatedly or habitually.” Nevertheless,
at the same time, what Fanning stresses, as usual (and on the preceding
page), is that the present tense definitely can be used in a distributive (as
opposed to customary) sense in referring to an individual “engaging in the
occurrence only once.” The aorist may also be used in this genuinely
Endnotes                                                             Page 155
distributive manner, but “the present participle is far more frequent”
(VANT, 410-411). Consequently, it appears more likely that Fanning is
indicating that some of the examples he provides in this section may either
be customary or distributive—not both. If it is distributive, then “each
individual does the act only once” (VANT, 410).
        Burton comments as follows: “A class may consist of those who
habitually or constantly do a given act, or of those who once do the act the
single doing of which is the mark of the class. The former case is illustrated
in Matt 5:6; the latter in Rev 14:13” (SMT, 124). So Mt 5:6 is taken as a
habitual present: “Blessed are those who [habitually or constantly] hunger
and thirst for righteousness.” Yet Rev 14:13 is distributive: “Blessed are the
dead who die [once] in the Lord from now on!” One only has to die in the
Lord once to be part of the blessed dead class. The first blessing requires
habitual action; the second blessing requires punctiliar action. Burton also
cites Mt 7:13 as an aspectual cross-reference for Rev 14:13, where Jesus said
in this Matthean passage that there are many who enter through the broad
gate. Distributive (and thus punctiliar) entrance for the individual is thus in
view. Like Fanning, Burton asserts that the present participle can be used for
a habitual class, but the punctiliar class can take either a present or aorist
participle. “Customariness applies not to the action of the individual member
of the class, but to that of the class as a whole” (SMT, 124). Such a
comment, if taken absolutely, would rule out a distributive customary (i.e., a
distribution of the customary action to each individual member so that each
member performs the action in a customary fashion). Of course, a
descriptive durative would also be unacceptable.
        Wallace seems to concur: “There are two types of iterative imperfect:
(1) Iterative proper, in which the imperfect indicates repeated action by the
same agent; and (2) Distributive, in which the imperfect is used for
individual acts of multiple agents” (GGBB, 546). Yet he cites McKay, who
seems to suggests that the multiple agents may perform multiple acts: “Just
as a series of dots or dashes may be seen as a line, so a series of similar
activities (whether actions or processes) may be viewed as a continuing
process….Its distributive realization…occurs in circumstances where a
number of agents perform similar activities” (NSVNT, 44). Apparently,
distributive agents can perform processes. Nevertheless, when talking about
the iterative present, Wallace continues to associate the distributive agents
with individual acts: “The distributive present belongs here, too: the use of
the present tense for individual acts distributed to more than one object.)”
(GGBB, 520, emphasis his.)
        Grammarians are not necessarily agreed with one another or
internally consistent in whether to use the distributive to refer individual
acts. For that matter, they are not necessarily agreed as to when an iterative
may be distributive. For example, Wallace regards Lk 3:16 as distributive:
“This is an instance of a distributive present: John baptizes each person only
Page 156                           Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
once, but the action is repeated” (GGBB, 521). Fanning, in contrast, regards
it as simply iterative (VANT, 207). Wallace’s definition of the distributive
as referring to individual acts by multiple agents would limit it to punctiliar
action by multiple agents. Fanning repeatedly stresses the punctiliar nature
of the distributive action as done only once by the individual but makes
allowance for the possibility of the individual performing the action once or
more. McKay appears simply to regard the individual aspect as matching the
group aspect, whatever that may be contextually. My synthesis is to adopt
the mediating position represented by Fanning so that my propensity is to
take distributives simply as punctiliar for the individual unless context or
syntax suggests otherwise. By default, I will stress the punctiliar probability
unless context or other factors lead me to think otherwise.
        Fanning acknowledges that generic present participles can denote
customary aspects (VANT, 411). A couple of examples that may be gleaned
from his list are:

       •    Gal 6:6 is customary: Let the one who is taught the word share
            all good things with him who teaches.
       •    2Jn 1:9 is customary: The one who abides in the teaching, he has
            both the Father and the Son.

One reason I point out this concession is because some within my own camp
stress the punctiliar nature of the gnomic articular present participle in
soteric texts so much so that one might think that we potentially are
overstating our case by implying that such participles always are punctiliar.
To clarify, I am not saying that gnomic participles necessarily are punctiliar
just because of the syntax. General probability and the writer’s propensity
may point in the direction of individual punctiliarity, but certainty regarding
the soteric punctiliarity is derived from contextual and logical
considerations. I also would add that the same phrase regarding humbling
and exalting is used distributively in Lk 18:14 (for soteric punctiliarity) but
customarily in Lk 14:11 (for mistholic linearity). See my discussion of these
texts in my book, Believe: An Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the
Gospel of John. I do not need to overstate my case to prove my case.

         Whoever ever = who ever. The former more emphatic form is chosen
herein to avoid confusing whoever with who ever (since the latter would not
come across orally and since Fanning mixed the examples with pas with
those without pas). Otherwise, one simply might have reserved the more
emphatic form for the pas constructions.

      57
         As Hodges notes, John’s statement that God’s commandments are
not burdensome suggests the possibility of present victory (1Jn 5:3).
Consequently, overcoming in the present is the probable stress of 1Jn 5:5.
The initial (ontological) victory which has occurred in the past for all
Endnotes                                                              Page 157
believers (denoted by perfect tense in 1Jn 5:4) can be a present (experiential)
victory (denoted by the switch to the present tense). Eaton cites the change
back to the present tense as grounds for affirming present victory. Michael
Eaton, 1, 2, 3 John (Great Brittan: Christian Focus Publications, 1996), 177.
Constable well elaborates that a present victory, as opposed to a future
(eschatological) victory, is in view.

      Continuing to overcome is not automatic for the Christian. Not
      all Christians continue to overcome the world (cf. 2 Tim. 4:10).
      Only those who continue to live by faith (i.e., trust and obey
      God) do. Furthermore no one can overcome the world unless he
      or she believes that Jesus is the Son of God. That trust is the
      key to any and all overcoming. Obviously every believer will
      overcome the world ultimately when we go to heaven, but that
      thought is foreign to the context here. (1 John, 49).

     Most significantly, like Hodges, Constable correctly notes that this
present victory is not guaranteed automatically. In contrast, however,
Ryrie’s comments regarding overcomers and 1Jn 5:5 lead to the conclusion
that whoever ever believes in Christ will overcome experientially.
Accordingly, if you do not overcome experientially, then you were never
ever a believer. Ryrie’s dispensational proveitism is at odds with his FG
comments elsewhere. For documentation and interaction concerning Ryrie,
see The Outer Darkness. In my assessment, John’s statement in 1Jn 5:5 not
only lays the foundation for the experiential overcoming found in Rev 2-3;
but a proper understanding of 1Jn 5:5 is informed by the latter text and
proves that proveitism is false.
     Alternatively, Dillow rightly takes issue with Reformed proveitism (as
represented by Rosscup) which argues (like Ryrie) on the basis of 1Jn 5:4-5
that all genuine believers are experiential overcomers. This Reformed
assessment is then allowed to control their exegesis of Rev 2-3 so that this
context is twisted tortuously to mean that all genuine believers are
experiential overcomers (p. 37). In counter distinction to the FG
counterarguments posed by Hodges and Constable to proveitism, Dillow’s
FG counterargument to proveitism is that 1Jn 5:5 does not refer to
experiential overcoming (p. 470). Dillow completely discounts the
proveitistic appeal to the articular present participle as proving continuous or
habitual overcoming:

            The [proveitistic] argument from the articular present
      participle is simply wrong. While it is true that the present tense
      can sometimes carry a durative force (“continue”), it is not
      intrinsic to the tense and must be established from the context.
Page 158                          Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
      The articular present participle, however, rarely, if ever, has
      durative force; it is merely a substantive. (Reign, 200)

     In terms of the internal transformation that was wrought within the
believer by his or her new birth, the believer is an overcomer whether the
believer is experiencing the reality of victory in terms of his or her
performance. Theologically and aspectually, Dillow’s FG interpretation is
very appealing. Nevertheless, in deference to Hodges, the present discussion
will concede that the contextual probability outweighs the normal punctiliar
probability so as to make a distributive durative a reasonable suggestion in
1Jn 5:5. Also compare endnote 37.
        Illustration 36. Durative Overcomer ⊂ Perfective Overcomer




      For whatever has been born (z▬ ) of God overcomes (£) the
      world; and this is the victory that has overcome (z▬ ) the
      world—our faith. And who is the one who overcomes (£) the
      world, but the one who believes (£) that Jesus is the Son of
      God? (1Jn 5:4-5; TM).

In 1Jn 5:4, the saving faith of all believers already has overcome the world
because this initial faith has resulted in a new nature for each believer that
Endnotes                                                               Page 159
continues to overcome the world soteriologically (and thus positionally and
ontologically). In all likelihood, 1Jn 5:5 builds upon this soteric foundation
by suggesting that a present experiential victory is also available to believers
who continue to believe.
     58
        Zane C. Hodges, A Biblical Reply to Lordship Salvation: Absolutely
Free! (Grand Rapids: Zondervan Publishing House, 1989), 211-212.
     59
        SNTG, 84; GGBB, 518; GGNT, 879; VANT 161, 199-120.
     60
         The position entertained herein is that the durative position, if
correctly nuanced, can be expressed in a manner harmonious with OSAS.
Indeed, in a book dedicated to a defense of OSAS, Olson affirms:

             Did you notice that Heb 10:14 is used as a reference to
        both justification and sanctification? It shows that parallel
        but disjoint nature of these two concepts. The believer is
        already perfected (justification is as solid as the Greek
        Perfect Tense!) while they are being conformed to the
        image of Jesus Christ (sanctification is only as continuous
        as our growth). (p. 283, n. 373)

     As Olson is well aware, an articular present participle (such as found
here) can be used in a gnomic sense and thus not convey ongoing action (pp.
52-53). Nevertheless, if ongoing action is to be inferred in the present
context, then he is correct to insist upon its compatibility with OSAS. See
Lloyd Olson, Eternal Security: Once Saved; Always Saved (Mustang, OK:
Tate Publishing & Enterprises, 2007).
     For technical accuracy, I would prefer to say that the ongoing action in
such a case is descriptive rather than durative. Certainly, this ongoing action
would be descriptive rather than prescriptive in this case. If linear action is
intended in Heb 10:14b, then a conditional relationship is impossible
between 10:14a and 10:14b. The writer of Hebrews is not saying that
(A) believers have been sanctified and remain sanctified if (B) they are being
progressively sanctified. The for all time, which is applied by the author of
Hebrews to the first half of the verse, would rule out linear conditionality in
the second half of the verse, even if the second half of the verse is linear.
     In contrast, Heb 3:14 is clearly conditional. In this verse a perfect is
followed by linear condition and connected by an if: Believers have become
and remain (z▬) partners with Christ if they hold fast (£). The if connects
3:14a with 3:14b. The condition is expressed in the second half of the verse
so that B ⇒ A. If you hold fast, then you remain Christ’s partner.
     The conditionality of 3:14 would be expressed with a durative ( ●▬    £). If
Heb 10:14b is linear, then Heb 10:14 would be descriptive (●▬    ¤). If you are
being conformed to Christ image (at any time), then you already are
perfected (for all time). Inversely, if descriptive, Jn 5:24 might be
expressed as saying that he who believes has passed into life ( ●▬            ¤).
Page 160                            Fallen From Grace but Not From Perfection
Likewise, 1Jn 5:1 would be acknowledging that the one who believes
descriptively has been born of God ( ●▬¤). The one who presently believes has
been born of God already and passed into life eternally. This birth and
passage into life occurred previous to the individual’s present belief (but not
previous to his or her initial belief). This concession to the antecedent
argument is permissible if (and only if) the linear aspect for believe is
considered descriptive (rather than durative/prescriptive/necessitative). This
perfective state cannot be conditioned on linear belief. Albeit, since a
descriptive aspect is less probable than a substantive or distributive aspect in
these verses, this possibility was not mentioned in the body of the present
text. See Stative, 105.
      61
          The genitive in Heb 3:14 is a possessive genitive and appropriately
rendered Christ’s partners (meaning a partaker with Christ) in the
misthological calling (Heb 3:1). They remain partners by holding fast. See
Dillow, Reign, 102-106.
      62
           Moses was faithful in building God’s house (i.e., the Mosaic
tabernacle), personally serving therein, and overseeing the priestly services
conducted within it (Num 12:6-8; Heb 3:5). The writer of Hebrews make a
transition from looking at the house as (A) the place where priestly
representatives serve to (B) the people composing that household by means
of their priestly service. Believers are now the ones who are to function in
that capacity and therefore, by means of that function, compose that
functioning household (Heb 12:28; 13:15-16). Remaining a part of that
household would be contingent upon continuing to perform that function.
         In 1Pet 2:9, Peter confirms, “But you are [to be] a chosen race, a
royal [basileios] priesthood, a holy nation.” He applies this title to these
believers provisionally, that is, providing they meet the requirement for its
future realization. The OT background stresses the contingent nature of this
promise: “If you will indeed obey My voice and keep My covenant, then
you will be…to Me a royal [basileios] priesthood” (Ex 19:5-6; TM). The
full experience and exercise of our position as ruling priests is contingent
upon our present performance and must await the future for its full
manifestation.
         Just as house refers to ruling family in 2Sam 7:16, so it refers to
ruling priests in Heb 3:6. Currently, believers function as Christ’s
representatives on earth merely as priests, but the full realization of that role
will involve their functioning as ruling priests. They will be a ruling
household of priests. In order to function in that capacity, and therefore be a
part of that house, we must hold fast. Moreover, just as the priestly function
carried out in the tabernacle prefigures the priestly function that is to be
carried out in its heavenly counterpart (Heb 8-9), so those believers who
wish to serve as a member of that household in the Heavenly City (the
capital of the kingdom) must be faithful. The political House of
Endnotes                                                            Page 161
Representatives is nothing but a pale illustration of this priestly House of
Representatives.
      63
         The argument being made within the body of the present book is that
the perfect aspect in Jn 5:24 and 1Jn 5:1 cannot be antecedent may be
amended if believe is merely being used descriptively. See endnote 60.
      64
          Frank D. Carmical, “The Coronation of the King: An Annotated
Work of Fiction: Part 1,” JOTGES 2:2 (Autumn 1989): 53-68. Frank D.
Carmical, “The Coronation of the King: An Annotated Work of Fiction: Part
2,” JOTGES 3:1 (Spring 1990): 55-70. This comment is inspired by
Carmical’s fictional portrayal of Rudy (an overcomer) taking Joe (a
nonovercomer who had fallen into preaching legalism) on a flying
expedition. I highly recommend these articles. However, I would amend
Carmical’s conception of the outer darkness as only lasting a thousand
years. Perhaps the location of this “Zone of Darkness at the North and South
Poles” will be only for a thousand years (Part 1, 60), but the misthological
realities pictured by this Zone of Darkness will not be “a measly thousand
years” (Part 2, 63). Far from it, the misthological results envisioned by this
biblical imagery are eternal. Rewards are eternal. Therefore, the loss of
rewards (which is what is depicted by the Zone of Darkness) is eternal also.
The special location may change, but not for the better. If anything, the
remoteness of this Zone of Darkness may become increasingly remote in
eternity future as humanity spreads throughout the universe. The Remote
Zone, as I accordingly will call it, may become increasingly remote.
